THE BED PAGE.
IRISH LITERATURE
THE well-known and popular Cabinet
of Irish Literature now appears in a
new edition of four handsome volumes
issued by the Gresham Publishing
Co., London. The first three volumes
reprint the original work, with some
omissions and slight additions. In the
fourth volume, freshly compiled, the book is
brought practically to date by inclusion of
most of the young Irish writers of the last
twenty years. The included portraits of
authors appear to greater advantage, and
several are added ; and the whole work has
thus become a more interesting and valuable
record than formerly.
The first editor of the Cabinet was Charles
Read, whose industry and knowledge gave
the work a solid backbone that one has often
had occasion to appreciate. The new editor,
Katharine Hinkson, has brought to the task
a lighter mind and a taste less trustworthy.
She seems less occupied with what is litera-
ture in the high sense than with the current
performances that represent the new school
of writers. Thus her additions give an
agreeable review of interesting work
that has been published recently,
rather than an authoritative choice of the
part of that work which may be expected
permanently to remain. Her personal fancy
is a pleasant companion, but one distrusts
her judgment. She has included apparently
every author with the least claim to distinc-
tion, and has not insisted upon Irish birth
when there is a properly-attested Irish
parent—L. I. Guiney, for example, being
added from America and “George Egerton”
from Australia.
This Cabinet —so wide is its range, so
diverse its contents—-affords in comparatively
small compass a picturesque view of Irish
literary achievement—an achievement rather
high than broad, rather fitful than steady,
rather blazing than enduring. Irish-English
literature is always walking upon summits
and falling into abysses. Of the greater
part one might maintain the paradox that it
is not literature at all : it is essentially
oratory, a written rhetoric that would be
more effective if spoken. The Irish are a
race of prophets, seers, bards, but not a race
of writers, though their writers are numerous
and admirable. Yet even as one admires, one
is impelled often to reflect how the causes
of admiration are categorically weapons from
the orator’s arsenal, needing an orator’s
speech and personality to hurl them above
the imminent bathos of the printed page.

W.M.
Oratory is the nation’s native mode : litera-
ture is still an alien garment that cramps its
Irish wearers.
In these volumes masters of prose such
as Steele and Goldsmith, Burke and
Lecky, are of course excerpted : there
are speech and anecdote, song and
story; but limitations of space inevi-
tably make many citations fragmentary.
It is the poets, and especially the lyric poets,
who are best displayed. For fullness of
emotion, for splendour of colour and melody,
and for imagination that rises and rises till it
is lost in dream, there are none to surpass
them. In art they often fail: sometimes
one feels the want of substance in their
11 immortal, proud shadows ”; yet their best is
maybe as near perfection as mortal hand can
compass. And in lighter vein, what agility
and gaiety! “Father Prout ” is well
known : William Maginn, whose astonishing
“ Potoris Gloria ” is here given in Latin and
English, deserves not to be forgotten. These
and a hundred other men add of their wealth
to make The Cabinet of Irish Literature a
treasure.
MEMORANDA
A scrap of verse found last week in N.9.W.
Arbitration Court:
A. Bierce rises to the surface of things in The
Cosmopolitan. “The man who is always taking
you by the hand is the same who if you were
hungry would take you by the” restaurant.
Barrier Truth prints some stories of early
Broken Hill. One of them refers to two of the
bowwof the “Big Mine ” - Harper and Holly.
Said Mrs. Patton, the general manager’s wife, to
Holly on one occasion, “ I hear you are a terrible
man to swear, Mr. Holly?” “! t i
Mrs. Patton, you should hear Harper!” * *
With compliments to the excessive literature of
the Stars and Stripes : Blow Paul Jones.
Whistlers Spirit still lives, and an unlucky
publisher h«ts trodden on its tail. (One presumes
non-alcoholic Spirits are neuter.) Elkin Mathews
whs to circulate in London an American
publication, containing copies of letters written by
Whistler to one Richards. As the copyright of
letters vests in the sender and not in the recipient,
Miss R. Birnie Phillip, Whistler’s executrix,
(being mighty in the Spirit of Whistler, and
C ' u,l attributes), was able to compel
withdrawal, apology, and payment of solicitors’
costs. This new triumph over the sordid Philistine
presumably sent the Spirit whooping in ecstasy
through nine spheres of stars.

W.M.
“The parentage of that great social and politi-
cal upheaval was intellectual, the idea was ele-
vated,” writes Joseph Conrad, referring to the
French Revolution ; “ but it is the bitter fate of
the idea to lose its royal form and power—to lose
its ‘virtue’—the moment it descends from its
solitary throne to work its will amongst the people.
It is a king whose destiny is never to know the
obedience of his subjects, except at the cost of
degradation.”
G. H. Thring, in the iV. A. Review, urges that
to flood a country with the works of eminent
writers of other countries is to soak the nation with
foreign ideas, and to destroy those patriotic feel-
ings which a great national literature tends to
produce. He says rightly that the importance of
this point has not been sufficiently understood,
and quotes a concrete instance.
A deputation of the citizens of Hungary—a country 7
still outside the Bern Convention—appealed to the
Government, and complained that the large influx of
thought from other countries tended to foster in that
country the spirit, the tone, the sentiments of foreigners,
and that the young generations were not growing up Hun-
garian patriots full of the vigour and sentiments of their
race, hut nerveless cosmopolitans. They stated that this
result was due to the suppression of genuine Hungarian
literature, owing to the unfair competition by piracy of
foreign works.
Australia is in the same case. While this
country continues to borrow its literature from
Britain, the emergence of a patriotic national
spirit will always tend to be thwarted or delayed.
Swinburne’s novel, Loves Cross-Currents, is
found to reprint an essay in epistolary fiction
published in an English maga. long ago. The
Times praises the grand manner of the dedication
to Watts-Dunton, and adds that the book itself is
“a terribly pale affair,” closing in “undeniable
dullness.” “Mrs. Waterbrook owned to David
Copperfield,” we are reminded, “that if she had
a weakness it was for Blood. It was a very
reasonable weakness, and one that no write;'
should be without.”
Sir Frederick Treves’ condemnation of alcoho
has been duly starred by the prohibitionists, but
a-priori one would say that Treves is unfitted to
comprehend the whole case. He is a brilliant
surgeon, and the quality of brain that makes him
a brilliant surgeon is antithetic to the qualities
which make a great philosopher. “Surgery is
an exact science ” : a physician needs imagination.
He must rise to generalisations, while the surgeon
may follow details. As G. E. Channing writes in
an admirable study of a doctor’s life in July
Harper's, “ to make a great physician many things
are needed : a colouring of the artistic tempera-
ment, a dash of the gambler’s, a touch of femini-
nity, as well as the solid stratum of cool common
sense at the bottom of all. These eke out the
modicum of scientific knowledge, which is all
mankind has yet wrested from secretive nature.”
An interesting point in Justice Darling’s sum-
up of the libel case Thomas v. Lucy, was his state-
ment that
not think that anything published in Punch
couldTlo anybody the same harm as if it appeared in
a more serious paper.
Thus the doctrine of injury and damages is made
to depend, not wholly upon the libel, but partly
upon the character of the vehicle of libel. Though
the relevancy is not clear, How could there be
a more serious paper than Punch 1

Some Economic and Social Aspects of Public
Health Work, by J. S. C. Elkington, Chief
Tas. Health Officer (Hobart; Govt. Printer).
A brightly-written pamphlet stressing the need of
preventive sanitary work.
Kangaroo Island, by E. H. Halleck (Ad., W. K.
Thomas), with map and illus.
Kangaroo 1., near Adelaide, ranks next in size to Tas-
mania among the islands of the Aus. coast. It was dis-
covered by Flinders in 1802, and by Dowie a century
later. In this 48-pp. pamphlet the writer chatters plea-
santly of its history and hopes.
Queensland. —Annual Report of the Chief Pro-
tector of Aboriginals for 190 A (Brisb., Govt.
Printer).
Dr. Both continues his useful work, and naturally
has become an object of attack for those meaner whites
m the outlying districts who were accustomed to abuse
our black brother. Queensland is now mapped into
aboriginal protection districts, each with a local police-
officer in charge ; and the administration is as thorough
as funds at disposal will permit. It is pleasant to find
Combo praised by leading pastoralists in the Gulf
country for his prowess as a stock hand. “He is just
as good as a white man,” say many of them. That is
what professional pedestrians think, for applications
from individuals wishing to employ aboriginals on the
running track “continue to be received, and are
invariably refused.” At Torres’ Strait the Murrav and
Dauar Islanders own three fishing-boats, and go'shell-
ing on their own account, earning anything up to 3001.
a year clear. Criminal offences both by and upon
aboriginals are decreasing remarkably.—Generally, the
way in which the blacks are being looked after reflects
credit upon Queensland and the officials concerned.
Flashlights on Japan and The East, by Dr. W.
Maloney, M.P., in collaboration with Francis
Myers (Melb., Andrade).
A richly-written gossip about a journey to Japan,
full of information and suggestion—and warning.
Cheerless, I look from my arbiter's seat,
Asking for oxygen each one I meet :
Why must I suffocate here every day 1
Why must 1 poison my young life away ?
iVo one to ventilate, no one to fan,
No one to jrunkah a half-stifled man :
Sniffing miasmas up, sadly I stare,
Child of the atmosphere, driven from air !
THREE POINTS OF VIEW
mSE Japanese point of view is admirably
I indicated in the book of The Russo-
Japanese Conflict, by K. Asakawa,
published by Constable, (7s. 6d.
net). [This book should be read by every
member of the Federal Parliament; and,
incidentally, one member of- that Parlia-
ment should devote himself especially to
Japan. He should equip himself with all
the facts and figures, and open and main-
tain channels of direct information, so as to
obtain an expert’s knowledge of the subject,
kept always up to date. Such a devotee
would do national service. Better still,
perhaps, if the Federal Government gave
the task to a permanent official with brains.
There should be at least one man in this
country to know everything about Japan.]
Asakawa states his case thus : He nni
out that in the last thirty years there hi
been an immense growth in Japanese tral
and industry, coincidently with an enormnn
increase of population. In 1875 the non, i
tion_ was 34 millions. To-day ft j* JJ
millions, increasing at the rate of 600 000
year. This new population cannot be surf
ported by agriculture, since all the good land
in Japan is already under intense cultivi
tion. It must be supported by industry and
trade—-by an increased importation of raw
material and food-stuffs, and an increased
exportation of manufactures. Follow,
that, unless Japanese national life is to bt
paralysed, Japan must have an “open door’
— a foreign outlet. That, says Asakawa is
why Japan is making war—to keep open
the markets of East Asia. Korea and
Manchuria supply her with wheat, rice, and
other food-stulls, and take cotton goods in
return. |||| , |g
If there were a Japanese colony in North
Australia, North Australia could do as much
or more. It is more distant, but it is more
fertile. For the present, Japan is content
with East Asia ; but, as The Japan Gazette,
pointed out on Sept. 17, 1903, while “ Japan
has an overcrowded and increasing popula-
tion, Australia is a vast country compara-
tively unpopulated. Japan is an energetic
industrial nation, sadly needing field,s & for
labour. Australia is a fine large territory,
wasted for lack of labour. Nations that are
growing will assert their right to find room
wherever there is room ; and untilled fields
will be seized by the right of might, if not
thrown open in some other way. People
are not to hold land without making good
use of it.” That is all true. K. Asakawa is
quite intelligible. The Japan Gazette is
quite reasonable. If 46 crowded millions of
Australians held Japan, and four scattered
millions of Japanese held Australia, Aus-
tralia at Tokio might talk precisely as The
Japan Gazette talks. It might tell the
Japanese that they must take up the Yellow
Man’s Burden and occupy their territory, or
the Australians would come from Nippon
and do it for them. What’s sauce for the
Japanese goose is sauce for the Australian
gander.
pm
Britain may care a little about Australia,
but she cares a good deal more about
Britain. Britain, like Japan, depends much
more on industry than upon agriculture;
and, like Japan, must have open markets if
she is to keep alive. She has an open mar-
ket in India, which Russia threatened in the
distance. She has, or wants, an open mar-
ket in China, which Russia threatened close
at hand. Since Japan was willing to try
and remove those threats, obviously it was
British policy to let her. Japan has made a
good job of it, and Britain seems inclined to
be grateful. It was cabled on June 2 that
the British Foreign Minister says there is no
question of ending the Japanese treaty, but
only of maintaining it or strengthening it.
The treaty provides for mutual neutrality in
war ; mutual assistance if more than one
Power attacks ; and common action in regard
to China and Corea. It terminates on 29th
January, 1907, but if notice of termination is
not given on 29th January, 1906, it remains
in force subject to one year’s notice of
termination. One is assured that some
similar treaty will be kept in force by
Britain. Japan has proved herself a power-
ful ally ; she will remain a defence against
Russia ; and she is apparently content to
open the Asian market, or part of it, to
Britain as well as herself. Britain could hope
for nothing better. ,j,-‘
Jt
Australia could scarcely expect anything
better. Since Japan must expand, we want
her to expand in Asia, not in Australasia.
It is true that the greater her expansion, the
more formidable will be her menace to this
country. In 50 years of unimpeded progress,
she may have nearly 100 millions of popula-
tion ; and apparently the Chinese and
Koreans cannot be squeezed much closer.
But the menace of her impeded expansion is
much more formidable here and now. What
is quite clear is that Britain must not be
permitted to break down the White
Australia policy in favour of Japan. As North
Queensland knows, the Japanese are industri-
ally the most dangerous competitors of all
the coloured races. The Kanaka is tract-
able ; the Chinaman is manageable ; but the
Jap wants to own the country, and shows
fight if hindered.
There is no need for exclusion to be offen-
sive. Britain can tell Japan what is in some
ways quite true, that the Australians are an
inferior race who must be sheltered from the
superior Jap if they are to survive. Pnt 111
that way, there is no ground for complaint.
A.G.S.

LEARN FROM OTHERS’ EXPERIENCE.
The Case of
Mr. E. W. OAVIS.
(By a Sydney Keporter.Y
ft is safe to say that the people
who have the most vital interest in
the following particulars are those who
tind themselves in a similar position
to that occupied some seven years ago
by ftlr. lidwurd Walter Davis, painter,
of lienwick-tieet. Marnckville, who
uddressed these remarks to an inter-
viewer :
"It was whilst i was Jiving at
Leichhardt that I was so ill with
painters' colic, and also rheumatism,
and I can give you my word that for
several months it was a doubtful mat-
ter how things would go with me.
both those complaints at once would
be enough to lull most men, and it
is no exaggeration to. say that they
neatly caused my death. Jdy digestive
organs w ere also in a ten inly bad
way, so that, you see, I was suffering
lrom a serious complication of ail-
ments."
"Lhd you seek medical advice, Mr.
Davis ?’
uh, yes ; 1 saw a doctor, and for
some time remained Unuer ms treat-
ment. which, unfortunately, proved to
be utterly' u.-eless. 1 took enough
medicines to do me good in those
days if they had been of the right
kind, but evidently they were not,
and the agony I was continually suf-
fering was simply intolerable. The
rheumatic pains in my legs had quite
a crippling eliect upon me, for i had to
refrain from moving about as much as
possible because of any exertion ag-
gravating my afflictions. JNearly edl
my joints were as stiff and sore as
could be, so you can imagine how dii-
ficult it was for me to do anything ;
but so far as doing anything was
concerned I hadn t the inclination, for
I felt as if the whole world had closed
its doors against me. in my melan-
choly frame of mind I could see no-
thing excepting in the gloomiest of
colours, and all there seemed in this
life was to struggle hopelessly along
against overwhelming adversities. Mad
1 been told that in a short while 1
was going to die i’m sure it would
have been good news, for i was really
anxious to leave all my unhappiness
behind me.”
“ft seems as if you had come to the
conclusion that you were not going
to get better
“Indeed, I. could think nothing else,
because J could not get even a little
relief. My agonies had me worked up
to such a pitch of exasperation that
if anybody came and spoke to me I
could scat cely be civil to them. And
what foaiful nights 1 put in week af-
ter ween ; frequently no sleep from
the time I went to bed till 1 got up
again next day, and I d like to know
who could have got to sleep if they
hail felt as I did, with the sensation
in my stomach aw a everything was
being drawn up into a knot and then
screwed round and round. Combined
with these afflictions 1 had pains un-
der my heart, which caused me more
anxiety than enough, for what chance
had 1 of ellecting a recovery if L had
heart disease as well, and the way
palpitation occurred made me think
that organ was diseased. This was
not all, though, as my chest had
heavy pains in it which' affected my
breathing, and not a day passed but
what .1 was nearly distracted with a
headache. If I took a small quantity
of food—and my appetite was too
poor to cat much —it simply turned
sour in the stomach and gave rise
to fits of belching, and a feeling as
if 1 wanted to be sick would come bn.
Uh, there was no child’s play about
my illness, and 1 otten say what a
lucky man 1 was ever to get rid of
it.”
“That is what I would like to hear
about. Do you mind telling me?”
Aot at all. 1 am only too glad
for it was such a wonderful affair, as
most of the neighbours round where 1
lived could tell you. They knew Low
t su tiered, and when they saw that 1
was being pulled round they were very
curious to know what was the cause
of it, and 1 told them Clements Tonic
was the medicine that was saving my
life, which was the truth. Besides
what I've already stated, I used to
get feelings of giddiness very often,
and my tongue was never clean, but
covered with slime, and in my mouth
the taste was like bile ; but when 1.
had taken a couple of bottles or so of
Clements Tonic 1 was not a bit like
that, and the old feelings of sickness
alter eating were gone. That was a
good deal to be grateful for, but no-
thing compared to what fo.lowed. 1
used to dream a lot whenever I ob-
tained some sleep, and would wake
uPiyiu terror as if ,L had been dream-
ing of murder or Something equally
bad, but Clements Tonic had such a
calming eliect upon me that when 1
slept 1 did so soundly, and felt as
tresh as> paint when I woke up. My
nerves might have had a lot to do
with those restless nights, for beforo
using Clements Tonic they. used to
twitch dreadfully ; afterwards they
were firm and steady, and the little
involuntary jumps i used to give when
lying down were no longer in evi-
dence. i was not the same man at
all when I had been taking Clements
ionic a few weeks. The acute rheu-
matic pains had. been gradually be-
coming less and less painful, and in
I good tune they di d out altogether.
jSuch a thing; might seem impossible,
but, on my honour, it is true, and
when .1 speak of the colic pains I can
say exactly the same, thing—they were
done away with oy Clements Tonic
once and for all. Many of my work-
mates have reaped the benefit of my
experience, for when I have told them
what cured me they got Clements
ionic for themselves, and they have
always spoken of the results in satis-
factory terms."
'‘And how did your digestive organs
fare
Couldn’t possibly have been made
more healthy than Clements Tonic
made them, which means all the dif-
ference between a life of joy and a
life of sorrow, it meant that my eyes
were no Jongvr heavy and dull as
they had been, and it meant that i
could always eat heartily without fear
of being ill afterwards, ’ for Clemen La
Tonic made my internal organs do
their work more perfectly than ever.
That 1 had got very thin could only
be expected, but under the treatment
of Clements Tonic i soon put on more
flesh than 1 had before my illness, and
the additional strength i acquired was
amazing. My wife was also very ill
once, and Clements Tonic did her a
wonderful amount of good, which
shows that it can be used equally well
by either sex, to whom 1- unfailingly ;
recommend its use when circumstances
require."
“And can we make use of this con-
versation ......
“Undoubtedly. You are welcome to
publish every sentence in any way you
prefer.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Till After Judgment Day.
When I am dead my friends can take
And place me where they will;
Beside some sleepy stagnant lake,
Or ’neith a window-sill;
Or where the mild-eyed, hard-hoofed cows
Will trample o’er my chest—
Just anywhere the Law allows
I’m satisfied to rest.
It matters not to me one jot —but one thing do I
pray :
That I may lie quite undisturbed till after
Judgment Day.
Of course I’d like the night-wind’s sighs
To murmur round my tomb ;
I’d like the bright-winged butterflies
To lighten up the gloom ;
I’d like a row of oyster shells
Placed neatly round the plot ;
I’d like an epitaph that tells
Discreetly that I’m not.
All these are merely trifling whims —but one thing
do I crave:
That Judgment Day will pass away and leave me
in my grave.
My conscience is quite free from guilt—
I don’t dread being sent
To join the simmering souls that wilt
In steamy merriment;
But if I must be tried at all,
I’d like it quietly done,
Without grim crowds, in unstarched shrouds,
Grouped round to watch the fun.
It is no merely idle wish that prompts me when I
say :
Please put me where I’ll miss the Blare announcing
Judgment Day.
I never had the slightest taste
For ceremonial state;
There’s nothing in this gaunt World’s Waste
I so sincerely hate.
The sight of Hell, forsooth, is less
Than hordes of flesh less frames
Arrayed in leering toothlessness.
Now where is he who blames
This earnest prayer, which grim despair keeps
urging me to pray ?
Press tight the sod ’twixt me and God till after
Judgment Day.
vie.
BAMBOO.
Land Monopoly.
Takeiheed of the farmers, how they sow,
And the landlords, how they reap ;
Take heed of the produce the cornfields grow,
And the portion the landlords keep.
Take heed of the struggling men who strive
The long wild winters through ;
And note the drones in thehuman hive
Who scoop when the rent falls due.
Take heed of the foam-flecked teams that pant j
Take heed of the sweat-grimed men,
Who’re trudging in when the moonbeams slant
To a cheerless bag-walled den.
Take heed of the slave-like wives who toil
In the dawnlight, noon and dark ;
Of the eager grab for his share of spoil
By the sharp-tusked legal shark.
Take heed of things like these, and know
That ever must these things be,
While the fertile fields where the Lord’s winds blow
To the people are not free.
VIC.
GEO. BRYANT.
The Last Lover.
“ I thirst, my love ! Let the warm blood flow
Until thy turbulent veins are dry.
Red roses lie on the heart I covet,
As strong wine splashes the lips that love it;
To pledge thee here where the deep drops slow,
I hasten, love; for I heard thy cry.
“ What staunchest thou with thy fingers slight ?
That runneth o’er on thy warm, wet breast—
Strange sap to flow from a milk-white bosom !
And ravest thou in thy grief to lose him ?
Who caged a bird for his sole delight—
To fling it forth from a rifled nest.
“ And one who loved thee passing well—
For whom a strand of thy glistening hair
Were bond enow ! In thy pride forsaken,
He sleeps too deep at thy need to waken,
Who would have clung to the bars of hell
To hold thy hand for a moment there.
“ He spurred his steed till its flanks were dyed
And deep chest laboring brokenly ;
A demon chant in the frenzied rhythm
Of pounding hoofs on the plain went with him—
Was thine the fault when the stallion shied
And flung him prone on a fallen tree ?
“ One knelt by thy pillow, passion pale.
Who fled away through the midnight dim—
Dost thou remember the lonely waking
To moaning surf on the low rocks breaking,
And white sea-foam in the schooner's trail ?
Now nought it matters to thee—or him.
“ One stooped to fondle a fallen rose,
Dropped from a fold on thy scented gown ;
Now crushed and low in the dust it lieth ;
For other rose than thy rose he sigheth—
A bud that odorous, crimson grows.
And thine is gathered and soiled and brown.
“ With two white'passion flowers on thy face—
Meet blossoms these for our bridal bed !
Nay, shrink not, sweet, while thy limbs I cover—
No shame should lie between lo' H n"d lover ;
Anl priests shall mumble above the place
Vv herein we two take our joy," JJeat n said.

Imagination*
Astronomers tell us that only 3000 stars are
visible to the naked eye. We have not counted
them, and don’t feel inclined to call those mid-
night watchers liars, but we know ot some men
who always see about 10 million stars in the way
of business opportunities. Optimism is a good
thing provided you don’t get drunk on it and see
double. The most successful business men have
only seen a few opportunities, but they have not
only seen them but taken hold and built on
them. The non-successful man has seen about 10
million, but has never taken advantage of his eye-
sight. That has nothing to do with white ants ;
but Avenarius has. Shrewd, able, calm, calcula-
ting men have built a house which, in the building,
was intended to last a lifetime, but after a few
years white ants have been discovered, and those
slow but sure workers have eaten the foundations
from ijt. They are in the joists, floors, studs and
walls. Had Avenarius, the white ant preventor
and exterminator, been used, not one of those
silent workers would have put in an
appearance. Reader, do you live in a wooden
house? Are you building a wooden house? Are
you fencing ; or building a stable ? If either, write
to E. Rich & Co., Ltd., about Avenarius.
Ruberoid has a mission. That mission is to
shelter the world. Every year brings it nearer to
accomplishment. Its first use was for roofing
cottages. To-day palatial mansions are roofed
with Ruberoid Roofing.

Human Nature.
Walking down Pitt-street the other day, I met
Mr. Andrew Anderson, and I told him that on
the Monday evening previous I had taken a run
out to South Head to look over the lighthouse.
He said : “ Had I known you were going I would
have gone with you.” I replied that the trip must
be quite a common one for him. “ No,” he said,
“ I have been living in Sydney for 10 years, and
have never been to South Head.”
What a peculiarity it is with men not to be able
to enjoy the little pleasures at their doors. Here
was a man who had been living almost on the
tram line for ten years, and yet had not visited
South Head. It is wonderful what a careful
analysis of immediate surroundings will reveal in
the form of neighborhood possibilities.
Think of this with Anchor Fence. If your farm
is not fenced with Anchor Fence, the next farm,
or the one next to it is, for it is difficult to travel
many miles in any part of Australia without find-
ing a block of land sprouting Anchor Fence. A
list of the pastoralists whose runs are enclosed
with Anchor Fence would be a directory of the
most successful squatters in Australia. The big-
gest tree falling on Anchor Fence cannot damage
it. Remove the tree and the fence springs back
into position. It won’t burn, consequently bush
fires cannot affect it. Stock cannot injure it, nor
can it injure stock. Take it all round it is a fence
worth owning. E. Rich and Co., Ltd., are the
agents.

GOING TO LAW.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers pro poses going to the High Court with Premier Deakin on 14 points re the Federal Capital. The Court will
presumably meet on the proposed site of the Capital so as to be able to take in the details of the case,' and doubtless (as there will be two
Treasuries to draw fees from) a strong Bar will be engaged.

Hotv to Address Betters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
o/ficials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 24, 1905.
A FEDERAL BANKING- BILL.
Australia is just now in the curious position of
having John Forrest, of Westralia, for its
Treasurer. There are many things that John
Forrest doesn't know, but his ignorance of finance
is commonly supposed to almost amount to genius.
And there is reason to believe that the large gen-
tleman from Westralia knows well enough that
he is a square peg in a round hole, and only
accepted his present billet because he is in the
habit of accepting billets, and because he didn’t
feel that he would be anymore circular a peg in
any other hole. A few days ago he was asked if
he had any intention of bringing in a Banking
Bill this session, and the alarm and horror with
which he washed his hands of all responsi-
bility for so intricate a measure were amus-
. ing. Yet Australia badly—very badly— wants a
Banking Act constructed on sensible lines; it
wants such an Act much more than it wants a
great many things that loom more conspicuously
on various political programmes. George Turner,
during his last spell at the national cash-box, col-
lected some materials fora Bill, but it wasn’t the
Turner of 1894—the strong man who loved to
seize big difficulties with both hands—who did
this; it was the worn, ageing, perfunctory Turner,
who felt that most things were vanity, and the
ideas of this Turner tended towards bringingabout
uniformity with the least possible disturbance of
existing methods. But existing methods require
to be disturbed a good deal to produce the best
results, both for the banks and for the community.
Unluckily, the Deakin party has put up a Trea-
surer who takes little interest in finance. The
Labor party has very little finance on its pro-
gramme. And the Reid party has fallen back
into an antediluvian condition of fossildom, and
is too much guided by the valuable financial ex-
perience of Bruce Smith to look very hopeful at
present.
• • * *
In suggesting some possible features of a new
Banking Act, The Bulletin leaves out the ques-
tionof a national Bank—the Bank of Australia.
That is another problem. But when the really
National institution comes to pass, it would be
doubtful policy to make it a completely new bank.
It would pr.obably be much better to buy up some
existing banks as a nucleus—not the best ones
which can stand on their own bases, but the
derelicts, which are better out of the way, and
whose existence is a bad advertisement for Aus-
tralia. This would mean taking half-a-dozen for-
lorn concerns off the market, and would prevent
the Bank of Australia making a very serious addi-
tion to a competition which is already much too
great. It would also mean the acquisition, at a
comparatively cheap rate, of a very large business
—a very bad business in the present incompetent
private hands, but one which, with the Federal
G-overnment’s credit and capacity behind it, and
with only one management to support instead of
five or six, would probably become immensely
profitable to the Government. But dealing only
with private institutions The Bulletin’s idea of
the leading features of an Australian Banking
Act would be somewhat as follows :
* * * *
(1) No new bank to be allowed to start with a nominal
capital of less than £500,000 and with a paid-up capital
(or a capital to be paid up in cash within the first six
months of the bank’s existence) of less than £lOO,OOO.
(2) All banks’ accounts to be audited and securities in-
spected, and, as far as possible, valued, at least once a
year by Federal officials appointed for the purpose.
Special audits to be provided for in special cases. Any
bank which has made such losses as to reduce the
shareholders’ funds to less than £lOO,OOO to receive notice
that it must, within three months, call un sufficient new
capital to make at least £lOO,OOO, and dividends to be
suspended until this is done. Any bank which fails to do
this, or which is absolutely insolvent, to be either wound
up or, if the Federal auditors believe it can be nursed
back into solvency, taken charge of by the Government
until it has become solvent, as the Maoriland Government
did with the B.N.Z. The Government to have power to
wind up if, after taking charge, the case proves hopeless.
Any. advances made by the Government during the
nursing process to carry interest and to be a first charge
on the assets.
(3) Banks which are now practically insolvent (if the
State does not take them over as the nucleus of the Bank
of Australia) to be exempted from the winding-up provi-
sion provided that the official audit shows a steady
progress back towards solvency. In case of any retro-
gression, these banks to be either wound up at once or
taken charge of by the State, which may wind them up
later if it finds the case hopeless. When these crippled
banks get back to a solvent condition all the usual rules
to apply to them. No dividends to be paid beyond such
as the institution may be already pledged to until solvency
is attained, and even dividends to which the bank
is pledged to be paid only when they don’t absorb over 60
per cent, of net profits.
(4) Every bank to carry at least 40 per cent, of its net
profits, as certified by the Government auditor, to reserve
fund until the reserve fund fully equals the paid-up
capital. After that, to carry at least 20 per cent, of its
net profits to reserve fund until the reserve equals £l5O
for every £lOO of paid-up capital. Also to carry at least
20 per pent, of its net profits to reserve fund until the
shareholders funds (paid-up capital and reserves) equal at
least 25 per cent, of all liabilities to the public At
least half of the reserve fund to be invested in Australian
public securities (of any State or of the Commonwealth)
on which securities the States and the Commonwealth
must pledge themselves not to reduce the interest, on
renewal of the loans, below 3 per cent.
* * * *
(5) Furthermore the public securities held by each bank
(either as part of !ts reserve fund or outside its reserve
fund) to be sufficient to cover its current note issue, and
sufficient public securities to cover its note issue to be
deposited with the Australian Government. On this
basis the Australian Government can undertake payment
of all notes in case of a suspension. (This and the
succeeding five clauses are inserted on the assumption
that private note issues would continue, which seems
most probable.)
(6) Note issues to be limited only by the amount of
public securities deposited with the Australian Govern-
ment. The Government, in time of crisis, to have power
to authorise the temporary issue of notes against other
forms of good security, and to make notes legal tender
(7) Notes to be compulsorily convertible into gold onlv
at the bank’s chief office in each State. (As this proviso
might make a small branch—say at Berth—liable for the
payment of a huge batch of notes—one quite beyond its
means—issued by the head office—say at Sydney—it
should be further provided that the bank can demand
reasonable notice before cashing large parcels of its
notes issued in another State.) As notes would be practi-
cally guaranteed by the Australian Government no hard-
ship would be inflicted on note-holders.
(8) Note-tax of 1 or 1J per cent.
(9) Notes to be good at their face value in any State as
well as in the State of issue.
(10) No bank to issue notes that are not reasonably
clean and in fair order. J
(11) Every bank to hold not less than 40 per cent, (or
possibly 50 percent.) of its cash reserves in notes issued
by the Australian Government. (This would give the
Government command of, say, £8,000,000 in cash.)
(12) The Australian Government to hold a gold reserve
equal to 25 per cent, of the paper thus issued (probably
£2,000,000) always on hand to meet the demands of any
bank which, through the reduction of its cash reserves
was in a position to (and wished to) demand gold for a
portion of its Federal notes.
(13) The Australian Government to invest 50 per cent
of the amount received from the banks in return for
paper money (probably £4,000,000) in the purchase of
State securities (of any State), and this amount to accu-
mulate at compound interest, the interest b ing devoted
to the purchase of further State securities. The fund
thus created to answer two purposes—as a security for
the Australian Government’s notes issued to the banks
and as a Public Debt Sinking Fund. (When the State
debts are wholly or partly taken over by the Australian
Government, the Sinking Fund securities would, of course
become partly, or wholly, Federal securities.)
(14) The other 25 per cent, (probably £2,000,000) re-
ceived by the Australian Government in exchange for
notes to be applied to the acquisition of a Federal terri-
tory and the construction of a Federal capital. Any sur-
plus to be put into the Sinking Fund abovementioned
Any deficit to be made good as the Australian Parliament
thinks best, but not by diverting money from the Sinking
Fund. All net rents or net profits derived from the
Federal territory to go into the Sinking Fund.
* * * *
(15) Fifty per cent, extra income-tax to he levied on
foreign shareholders in Australian hanks. (This would
have to be part of a general absentee-tax scheme, and
would necessitate making the income-tax a Federal
impost.)
(16) Every bank which is merely a branch of some
institution having its principal business outside the Com-
monwealth to be required to keep its Australian business
separate from its outside business, to maintain sufficient
funds and investments in the Commonwealth to meet all
Australian liabilities, and to keep a certain proportion of
its capital (according to the amount of its business) in-
vested in the Commonwealth. Its Australian creditors to
have a first claim on the funds and investments in the
Commonwealth.
* * * *
The Bulletin advocates these propositions as a
protest against the tired attitude of George
Turner, who holds that there should be as little
change as possible in the banking system, and the
alarmed attitude of John Forrest, who appa-
rently regards banking legislation as something
beyond a man of his mental calibre. In drafting
them six purposes have been kept in view
(1) To prevent the recrudescence of the bogus or boom
bank.
(2) To make Australian banks the most solvent, solid
and reputable set of banks on earth.
(3) To make the Australian banks also Australian-
owned banks.
(4) To encourage the use of paper-money. Australia’s
extensive circulation of gold is a costly extravagance
on the part of a country which owes over £300,000 000 of
gold publicly and privately to foreign creditors, and is
paying heavy interest thereon.
(5) To use part of the surplus gold—the people’s money
not the banks’ money—in creating a fund for the steady
and continuous reduction of the Public Debt (this fund
should also be largely supplemented out of taxation).
(6) To encourage the conversion of the country’s foreign
debt into a local debt. The proviso about the holding "of
half the banks’ reserve funds in public securities aims at
this, and the same rule should be made to apply to insur-
ance and many other companies.
The most stringent of the proposed clauses are
not new, but are taken bodily (especially the one
which compels the holding of a large proportion
of the cash reserves in Government paper) f om
a Canadian law of many years standing—ft law
passed by a bitterly Tory* but still, in some re-
spects, a reasonably common-sense Government,
there are no Socialistic ideas on the list herein
given, unless it is Socialistic for a bank to be sol-
vent, and revolutionary to reduce the Public Debt.
Ihe proposals are in no sense Labor proposals,
though, in The Bulletin’s opinion, they ought to
be, and though many foolish old bigots take it for
granted, without inquiry, that they are ; Labor
leader Watson has expressed his approval of some
of the ideas here outlined, but the bulk of his fol-
lowing shows no interest in them, no knowledge
of them, and little desire for knowledge. The
scheme is advanced as something which may
fairly be considered without any party throwing
curses at any other party ; without anybody flying
on handle to talk about the Petriana lie,
or the Six Hatters joke ; without Joe Cook snarl-
ing about Socialism, or Bruce Smith getting
tangled up in frantic proposals for the introduc-
tion of Cmnamen. It aims at the stoppage of
waste, leakage and insolvency, at the reduction of
the country’s public liabilities, and at the enrich-
ing of the country and the people in it—including
Bruce Smith and his political brother Pumpkin,
?I S M'Millan, Conroy, Cameron and
M Cay— by discouraging the tendency that the
country s assets have of being owned by other
people outside. And to denounce these aims as
Socialistic is a very bad advertisement for Toryism.

Adelaide to Port Darwin, and Adelaide to Perth, which
are all to be rushed forward. Nothing to be gained by a
larger population if you must concentrate it in the south-
east corner of the continent. Climate splendid on the
Herberton plateau and the table lands of the Northern
territory. There is where I would run the people in pell-
mell from every part of Europe, selecting them carefully
ft the port of departure, and mixing them on arrival, so
that there would be no colonies of particular nationalities
Tree passages, free land, and monetary help if necessary
and, per contra, a land-tax, and two weeks military
training*. Agricultural colleges in every centre.
Defence : Universal service. One vear of either mili-
tary or naval training, and then a fortnight a year after-
wards. J
Factories for big and small arms and ammunition at
Lithgow, foremen being imported from Birmingham, and
the Greusot and Krupr works ; naval yard at Melbourne
as safer than Sydney, for torpedo-boats and, later on
cruisers and battle-ships.
One or two first-class fortresses to be formed in Northern
Australia, and the fortifications of all the capital cities
brought up to date.
Maoriland to be urged to follow on same lines, and
guaranteed help if attacked.
That is what should be done. What we will do, time
alone will show, hut it may be in the way of hiring
Colonel Bell to calm our foolish fears, and restore the
blessed security of the good old days.
In reply to which The Bulletin is with Dr.
Arthur as to the need for land settlement (but
not land settlement only),'and as to the building
of the three great railways—Brisbane to the Gulf
of Carpentaria, Adelaide to Port Darwin, and
Adelaide to Perth, and it also hopes to see these
supplemented by a line from the Gulf to Perth.
Likewise it is with him as to assisted immigration,
as to arms factories, as to universal training, and as
to building our own fleet. For the lest it kicks Dr.
Arthur downstairs just as cordially as ever, on
the ground that, just by reason of the rottenness
of the financial methods by which he seeks to bring
these things about, and the want of an industrial
basis to support the scheme, the whole business
would end in ridiculous failure.
* * *
The scheme is, apart from the supply of a speck
of work, and that not self-supporting work, in
arms and ammunition factories and in yards where
warships are built, wholly a scheme of land settle-
ment. Every country that depends mainly on
agriculture is a poor country—generally a desper-
ately poor country. Australia would be poorer
than the average because its land is not of the
best, the rainfall is uncertain, and because, if it
has to sell its grain to the men who manufacture
for it in Manchester instead of to the men who
manufacture for it in Adelaide, Melbourne,
Sydney, Bathurst, Armidale, Ballarat, Bourke,
Brisbane, Rockhampton, Townsville, and so on’
its produce will be so loaded with charges and
expenses that the business will always remain
poverty-stricken. Even now agriculture only
struggles along in N.S.W.—which is as good a
State as the average—by the aid of stupendous
indirect subsidies. The size of them can be
roughly estimated by looking at one or two items
from the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners’ report
for year to J une, 1904 :
Subsidy to

And this is saying nothing of the fact that agri-
culture in N.S.W. is almost wholly exempted
from local rates. Yet, with all this subsidy,
which has to be made up by charging excessive
rates on manufactured goods, by taxation to make
up the railway deficit, and by misappropriation of
loans, the agricultural industry yields to the
people employed in it little more than half the
amount that manufactures pay to their employees.
And if agriculture grows without any corres-
ponding growth of other industries, the other
industries won’t be able to stagger under the
burden of the subsidy; the parasite will grow
bigger than the host.
* * * *
Neither Australia nor any other mainly agri-
cultural country can bear up under the load of
bringing out, on a very large scale, at the public
cost, purely agricultural settlers, many of whom
would have to be supported for a year or more,
and exempted from taxes for a year or more, in
addition to having their passages paid ; and
who would, under present conditions, have
to be subsidised all their lives afterwards by
having their pioducts carried far below cost of
carriage. It would burst; its immigration scheme
would burst; and its loans would probably end in
financial burst. Canada can try these immigration
devices, for Canada, under High Protection, is push-
lng 1 its manufactures ahead even more vigorous]y
than its agriculture. It is providing a larger and
larger market for its agriculturists close at hand,
and bringing the consumer to their doors, instead
of borrowing money so that it may pay a large
part of the cost of carrying their produce to the
distant consumer; Canada isn’t borrowing • it is
even reducing its debt and building a big railway
out of revenue; & J
* * * *
Australia doesn’t want to borrow any more,
having loaded posterity already with
id UOO.OOO a year of interest on unproductive
debt, and sold and spent over £100,000,000 worth
ot trie public estate, which belongs to posterity as
much as it does to us, it is sheer insolence to pro-
pose to borrow more on the plea that “posterity
has to shoulder some of the burden.” As far as
repayment is concerned, posterity has all the
burden, and all the loss, too. Nor should Australia
go crawling to Britain—already far more heavily-
taxed than we are—for assistance. It is time for
this country to rely on itself. And there is no
need to rake every country m Europe for immi-
grants and make this a polyglot land where half
the people may not have had time to acquire a
national spirit before the day comes when only a
ru v national spirit can pull us through. Aus-
tralia doesn't need to rope in Croats, and Bosnians,
and Basques, and Bohemians, and the like. Britain
fo,^nnn nyea i> to the United States alone,
about .L50,°00 emigrants, all of the British race,
and ail persons who paid their own passages and
paid a fee for admission (after rigid examination)
to Uncle bAMS highly-protected country. Aus-
tralia cam get most of these and many more like
them with a little assistance, and provided it has
work to offer them. But experience has shown that
while there is nothing to offer except a poorly-
paid agricultural industry, dependent for its ex-
tension on a market 16,000 miles away, this
country will get practically no immigrants except
those it pays for, and the ones it pays foi
will speedily commence to drift away again. It is
a case of begging and borrowing and pouring
water into a sieve. Queensland alone has spent
within a fraction of £3,000,000 of loan money upon
immigration, and trusted almost entirely to the
land—the blessed old land—to support the new
arrivals, and Queensland is a poor object
lesson to-day. But Australia, as a solvent, honest
country where every industry was expanding, as
lu. j* case with the United States, could get mul-
titudes of immigrants in addition to those it has
to pay for—immigrants who would be good wage-
earners, tax-payers, and bearers of a share of the
national burdens from the week they land.
* * » .
So The Bulletin respectfully kicks Dr. Arthur
downstairs (to use his own words) as a man who
has gone back in haste to an exploded superstition,
who has, backslid on the great question of national
honesty, and who is using the need for popu-
lation as one more excuse for resuming the
old loan disease in a worse form than before.
1-his country can’t raise enough or borrow enough
to buy all the population it needs for its defence,
to give the new arrivals land, to keep them till
their land becomes productive, to build railways,
for them—especially as they would have to be
exempt from taxes for a long while and wouldn’t,
help much to pay for the railways, and especially as.
the land revenue would have greatly diminished
through giving away the soil gratis; and finally it;
can’t afford to carry their produce over these rail-
ways at far less than cost. For on railways that
are built out of loans, and that have heavy interest,
to pay, cost is a serious item. The immigration-
policy which is based wholly on settling people on!
the land in a country situated as Australia is lasts-
just as long as the loans last, and then dies. Then,
there is the debt, the incredible railway deficit,,,
the smash, and the exit of the new population!
Nothing but the establishment of an abundance
of wholly self-supporting industries, of a much
better paying and less precarious class than agri-
culture in a drought-cursed land, will meet the
case. Australia is in deadly danger and wants
salvation. The trouble is that Dr. Arthur’s
scheme doesn’t offer salvation—it only offers the
old bankruptcy repeated.

The N.S.W. Lands Scandals.
W hen The Bulletin last reviewed the proceed
ings before the N.S.W. Lands inquiry Commis-
sion, Mr. Willis had been shown to have received
£34,928 4s. 3d. in fees, generally for getting land
gazetted and leased as improvement areas— i.e., at
a very low rent and under practically the least
onerous of all conditions of tenure. But very
little direct light had been thrown on the issue as
to whether this money was paid by squatters and
financial institutions because they thought that
Mr. Willis had wonderful naturalpporersw r ers of per-
suasion, or because they reckoned that he would
have to disburse a good deal of it in order to secure
for them what they wanted. That issue is
really the kernel of the inquiry. Was Mr.
Willis paid so enormously for his magnetic
powers alone, or because it was understood that
he was, to a great extent, only the channel
through which the money flowed'? The Com-
mission has, so far, not attacked that problem.
But the evidence since the beginning of August
has been marked by many sensational develop-
ments. Out of the investigation of the J. B.
Stephens leases a criminal charge has been made
by the Crown. The proceedings of the Com-
mission have been enlivened by the appear-
ance of Mr. Pilcher, K.C., as cross-examiner.
A remarkable case investigated, which in-
volved some new features, was that of the
Willie Improvement Lease. The Willie holding
was occupied by W. C. Hill. On his urging it
was thrown open for improvement lease. He
tendered. J. Reinhardt also tendered at a
higher price. W. C. Hill was not given the
option of coming up to that price, though it had
been previously stated before the Commission
that the tenant was given that Option as a matter
of fixed policy. J. Reinhardt secured the lease.
His name had cropped up before in the Lands
Department in connection with W. N. Willis.
In this lease matter he does not seem to have
appeared at all. A solicitor was appointed
by W. N. Willis to represent Reinhardt,
and this solicitor received the notices and
carried on the correspondence with the Lam's,
Department in re Reinhardt as directed
by Willis, but not without some protests.
Shortly after Reinhardt got the lease W. ]\ T ~
Willis transferred it for a consideration of £Tb7T
to W. C. Hall. The money was paid to Willis.
Lvidence brought before the Commission pointed'
to applications being made in several cases for
land rights by persons who, if they had any real
existence, never appeared, and had no other ad-
dress than c/o a land agent. That Mr. Willis
was not the only land agent of big fees i# beg a..


ning to be clear. Senator Millen bagged one fee
of £lOOO. Petek Close, it lias been shown,
had £6OO, £9OO, and £1250 out of different trans-
action*. Various Members of Parliament have

bers and ex-members of Parliament who have been,
so far, mentioned before the Commission as having

Land Agoncy.
The public, without being unduly suspic.ous, can
conjecture that the so far disclosed heavy pay-
ments made by improvement lessees to land
agents were partially disbursed in quarters not
yet fully disclosed. But the public so far does
not seem to be seized with any proper idea of the
vast financial interests involv d in this “improve-
ment lease ” business. The closing of lands —con-
tained in expired grazing leases—against closer
settlement, by granting improvement leases, has
been excused on the ground that valuable im-
provements had been effected during the tenancy,
and that these areas were necessary during the
drought for the salvation of the few sheep
remaining to the tenants. But there can be
no excuse for blocking settlement by the
granting of improvement leases —in the teeth
of the original tenants and of applicants
for small areas—to outsiders and strangers.
Indeed the value of any excuse which might be
made is discounted in advance by the fact that
some at least of these persons have made no other
use of their leases when acquired than to hand
them over to others whose dummies they were,
while others have traded them away, almost
in open market, It is a matter of notoriety
that one lease (which has net yet come
before the Commission) changed hands for an
equivalent of 14s. per acre —equal to a sum total
of £35,000 on an area of 50,000 acres. If it be
supposed that this lease were obtained on the
usual land-agency terms of Is. per acre, the profit
accruing to the temporary holder would be
£32,500. In the last resort the public has to pay
that sum—and a good deal more. Rentals for im-
provement leases seem to vary between 3d. and 4d. ;
per acre per annum. Take 4d. as the figure.
Witnesses have appraised much of the land let on
improvement leases at a sale value of from £1 to
£3 per acre. The capital value of land leased at
4d. per acre is more nearly akin to ss. per acre.
Of land worth £1 per acre, rental should be 16d.
per acre per annum ; of land worth £3, rental
should be 4s. per acre per annum. Therefore the
lessee who rents 50,000 acres at 4d., land being
really worth lGd., pays a total rental of £833 6s. Bd.
instead of £3333 Os. BcL, and thus saves £2500 a
year. If the land be worth £2, he pays 4d. an acre
instead of 2s. Bd., and therefore saves annually the
difference between £833 6s. Bd. and £6666 13s. 4d.
If the land be worth £3, his saving lies between the
payment of £833 6s. Bd. and £lO,OOO per annum.
Should he have a 21 years’ lease (some have a cur-
rency of 28 years), the first-named difference would
represent a loss to the State and a saving to the
lessee of £52,500 at the end of that period. The
second difference, during a similar term, would be-
equivalent to a loss to the State of £122,500. The
third difference would entail a loss to the State
and a saving to the lessee of £192,500. These
figures make it clear why such gigantic commis-
sions are readily paid for sqpuring improvement
leases-at re-assessed rentals ; why cash advances of
£5OOO are eagerly offered by financial institutions
as soon as the leases are signed ; and why leases
obtained on such easy terms are marketable at a
transfer value of 14s..per acre, As leases to the
extent of nearly 10,000,000 acres have expired in
the Central Division alone since 1894, and as the
improvement lease provision became law a year
later, it is evident that of late the opportunities
of earning high fees and of trafficking in improve-
ment leases have been practically unlimited.
With a possible swindle on 10,000,000 acres, and
the swindle running as high as 3s. Bd. per acre per
annum, it is a very large possible swindle indeed.

Australia’s Life Policy.
Australia looks on from a distance, and is rather
sorry for America, where life assurance business
can get into the trouble that is now raging round
the great Equitable office. But what is Aus-
tralia’s own position ? If it thinks it is protected
because four-fifths of its business is done with
mutual offices it is most likely making a bad
mistake. In theory the directors of a mutual
office represent, and are elected by, the policy-
holders ; in practice they are almost always the
nominees of “the office.” The cases in which a
candidate who lacked the support of “the
office” has been elected a director of any
leading Australian mutual society can almost be
numbered on the thumb of one hand. It is true
that, if things go wrong, or seem to go wrong,
and the policy-holders can be aroused, they can
do what they please'; but “the office” has an
enormous pull, and the chances are that some-
thing very serious—something almost ruinous—
would have to happen before the policy-holders
would be aroused. It is possible, therefore,
for the same things to happen in connection
with a mutual office as are alleged to have hap-
pened in connection with a proprietary office, like
the Equitable. The Equitable, moreover, has
always had to face an annual State audit and
inspection. The Insurance Department is
required to make an independent valuation
of the policy obligations of every office doing
business in its jurisdiction, so that the public may
see whether the sworn assets are sufficient for their
purpose. Further, not only has the value of these
assets to -be sworn to by the directors, but the
American law restricts loans on real estate to 50
per cent, of its value, and the funds may not be
invested in securities not sanctioned by the
Department. Sydney Daily Telegraph had to
admit the other day, in spite of its objection to
State supervision, that the State certificate “told
the policy-holders what was the actual value of
the investments held in trust for them.’ What
the certificate obviously couldn’t do was to tell
them how much better' those, investments might
have been if the directors had sold them to the
society at the price at which they bought
them. Hence, says the Telegraph, “it is
evident that such companies must be placed
under strict regulations, which hitherto United
States legislators have failed to provide.” The
Telegraph reckons that United States regulation
is not strict enough ; yet it is ten times more strict
than Australian regulation, about which the
Telegraph says nothing. This clear perception
of the necessity for virtue in New York is very
touching. The N.S.W. Statistical Bulletin
for July shows that offices doing business in
N.S.W. have outstanding policies (with bonus addi-
tions) amounting to £125,299,647 ; yet, in spite of
this vast business, N.S. W. has no special life-assur-
ance law whatever. There is hardly another matter
of greater importance than this ; yet the N.S.W.
insurer is protected neither by the State nor
by the mutual system upon which he has been led
to rely as a sufficient safeguard. Moreover, the
enormous importance which has been attached to
bonuses in Australia has constituted a danger
of its own. It has prompted directors
to use up all, or nearly all, their re-
sources and prospective resources in making
additions to policies, leaving little or nothing by
way of reserve against anything unexpected in the
way of calamity. Reckoning in the paid-up capi-
tal of the proprietary offices and every other form
of reserve, the offices doing business in N.S. Wales
had, at the end of last year, only just 3 per cent,
more than enough to cover their bonus arid policy
obligations—only 3 per cent, to provide for pos-
sible unfavorable mortality experienoe, as well as
for slumps in securities, &c. Even allowing that
the present rate of earnings is higher than the
assumed rate, the margin of 3 per cent, is still
uncomfortably small. It is so small, in fact, as to
call loudly for a regular official assurance that it is
a safe margin—moreover, that it is a margin
which actually exists.
The Sleepy City’s Tram Syndicate.
The seemingly endless trouble over Adelaide’s
tram system has crawled on another hair’s-breadth
The position up to a week or so back was that the
Government had given the companies notice to
come to arbitration as to the price to be paid for
the lines, Parliament having authorised the
Government to buy, not with the object of run-
ning the system itself, but with the fool notion of
skurrying around and finding a syndicate to do it.
Tortoise Town jobbed away its tramway rights to
syndicates in the first place, and got about the
most awful system in the world in return ; and
for years past the syndicates have been huge snags
in the way of getting anything better. If any city
on earth ought to feel violently ill when t am
syndicates are mentioned it is Adelaide; yet
the 8. Australian Parliament was so incredibly
foolish that it decided to buy one lot of syndicates
out to let another in—to get rid of syndicates it
knows in order to make room for a syndicate it
doesn’t know. What it will eventually cost to get
rid of even the first lot of syndicates—the present
lot —is beyond calculation so far. Yet they are
a comparatively modest crowd—you can’t ride in
an Adelaide tram, behind an Adelaide tram-horse,
as a regular thing, and wear much frill or cultivate
extravagant ideas. Yet, though the existing syn-
dicates are a comparatively modest lot, the arbi-
trators had only got on the door-mat of their job
when they began to dispute as to whether the syn-
dicates are entitled to be paid something for good-
will; and as they couldn't agree, the matter
was carried by about 18 barristers and nine
solicitors to the Pull Court. The Act which a
former Parliament gave the syndicates says that
the Government may take over the business
(there are four separate companies) at the end of
seven-year periods; a later Act says that it may
take them all over at the same time. The Pull
Court decides now that, though they may be all
taken over at the same time, it doesn’t mean that
that are not to be on the oid seven-year basis so
far as profits are concerned ; wherefore, the
Government must pay not only the value of the
aged lines and the comic-opera rolling stock,
but also the present value of the profits
of the current seven-year period ; and it
happens that that period has only just begun.
Probably it is good law; if it is, it is only
another warning to 8. Australia to have no truck
with any more tram syndicates, tram syndicates
being things that are liable to fly up and hit a
sleepy city long after it thinks it has got rid of
the nuisance. The arbitration will, of course,
have to proceed on the Pull Court’s basis ; but the
obvious duty of the new Government is to ask
Parliament what it thinks of the syndicate busi-
ness now, having had a little more experience of
the difficulty of getting rid of the beast once it is
allowed to coil its tail firmly round the premises.

A "Way They Have in Newfoundland.
A brief sketch of a place where cod comes from :
They must be extraordinary people in Newfoundland.
They are our brothers, of course ; and the “ grand old
flag” flies over them, and all that sort of thing, but for
all that, they don’t seem able to govern themselves
worth a cent. The art of Government with them, to
judge from their recent history, appears to consist mostly
in selling themselves, at a very low price indeed, to
syndicates. The story, as told in a recent number of the
London Speaker, is, or ought to be, very interesting to
Australians. We know a little about syndicates and their
methods in Australia. If some people had their way, we
should know a great deal more. We should have a syndi-
cate railway across Australia, from north to south. We
should have the best part of New Guinea governed by a
syndicate. We should have syndicate tramways, and
syndicate irrigation works. Perhaps the Newfoundland
story will help Australians to realise what they may
expect when the syndicate business gets properly planted
here.
In 1893, one R. J. Reid, a Canadian capitalist, entered
into a contract with the Newfoundland Government to
work the railway system for 10 years, in return for land
grants. In 1897, the -Government which entered into
this contract was defeated over it. But in 1898 a Bill
was carried granting to this same Mr. Reid vastly wider
privileges. In return for a payment of 900,000 dollars a
year, and certain royalties, he took over the telegraphs,
the local sea service, a Government dry dock, and the
freehold of any Grown lands he might select. In fact, the
only thing’s he doesn’t seem to have taken over were air
and light. Perhaps these were overlooked. The Bill was
supported by a Government which included Reid’s stand-
ing legal adviser (though the fact that this interested
person was Reid’s legal adviser was not discovered at the
time), and was rushed through the Legislature at railway
speed. The Governor protested to Chamberlain, then
Colonial Secretary, that the price was far too small, but
Chamberlain, although he admitted that the contract
was “an abdication by a colony of some of its most
important functions almost without parallel,” did not
think that the Imperial Government was entitled to
interfere.
The leader of the party which opposed this astonishing
contract was Robert Bond. He managed to defeat the
Government over it, and was returned by the country
with 32 supporters out of a House of 36, the Reid contract
being the only issue at the election. Bond’s Government
then bought back from the gentle Reid his rights over the
railways and the Crown lands. The most extraordinary
part of the story, however, is still to come. In January,
1905, the Bond Government, which had spent hundreds
of thousands of dollars in buying- back the country from
Reid, entered into a contract with the Ang-10-Newfound-
land Development Co., promoted by the Harmswortiis,
by which the co. obtained perpetual rights to 3000 square
miles of country for purposes of wood-pulp and paper
manufacture. This concession included about 2,000,000
acres of the best land in Newfoundland, with forests,
rivers, and lakes. The syndicate obtained full mineral
and quarry rights, was exempted from all municipal taxa-
tion, and had its machinery imported free of duty.
It obtained complete rights over the timber and game
within its area. It was empowered to control the occupa-
tions of all the residents. It might buy out any owner
within 70 miles of its own boundaries. The consideration
for these privileges was the magnificent sum of about Ss.
per square mile per year, swamp and barren lands ex-
empted, 2s. per 1000 ft. of sawn timber (nothing whatever
for timber converted into paper pulp for the Harms-
worths’ use), and 5 per cent, of the net profits from
minerals. Tne total sum thus payable, it has been said,
amounts to about the rent of a draper’s shop in a London
street.
The Bill embodying this contract was rushed through
with extraordinary rapidity, in spite of the fact that the
matter had never been before the electors at a general
election. It appears, indeed, that the cit.zens were strenu-
ously opposed to the whole project. It is stated that the
second reading of the Bill was carried in the Upper House
mainly through the “ influence ” of the paid agent of the
co. What the “influence” maybe, we are left to con-
jecture. It was certainly a pretty powerful one, to judge
by the rapidity with which the measure moved. And all
this has been done by a Government which got into office
solely by virtue of its opposition to a much less expensive
steal. Truly, they are remarkable people in Newfound-
land, and supply a valuable lesson in the art of “ en-
couraging an influx of capital to develop the magnificent
natural resources of the country.”
Westralia’s “ itfo.”
With more enthusiasm than it has shown over
anything for a long while, the Westralian
Assembly has thrown out the Daglish Govern-
ment’s scheme to buy out the Midland Railway
Co. for £1,500,000. But what does the Assembly
propose to do now ? It has simply decided that it
won’t do something ; and Westralia isn’t in such a
state of health that it can afford to do nothing.
It can’t pull through by even deciding vigorously
to be a negative. The position is that the Mid-
land Railway is a large foreign octopus which
undertook, 16 years ago, to give an efficient railway
service and develop the huge areas which it got by
way of concession ; it has provided some sort of a
service, but it has done absolutely nothing in the
other equally important matter. Spreading out
over 2,350,000 acres, everybody admits that it has
barred settlement instead of developing it Also
pretty well everybody admits that it ought to be
got rid of. Four years ago it could have been got
rid of for £1,300,000 ; but even that price was
thought excessive, and the deal didn’t come off.
Since then the State has been borrowing money
and using it to boom the State’s great natural
resources—in other words, it has been borrowing
money to boom the foreign octopus’s property,
and tiie foreign octopus consequently now asks for
£1,500,000. The railway undertaking might pay
4 per cent, on £900,000 ; the other £600,000 repre-
sents the land-grab ; and it is alleged that, at the
price the State is charging for land, it couldn’t get
within about a quarter-of-a-million of that much
even if it sold the lot. The whole trouble is that
Westralia boomed its great natural resources (with
borrowed money), and forgot, or merely declined,
to put on a stiff graduated land-tax, with
something special to reach the absentee, in
order to make those great natural re-
sources available. And if the Westralian
Parliament now leaves matters where it has
dropped them, the State won’t be any better off.
In fact, if it proposes to go on booming the great
natural resources, and doesn’t put on the heavy
graduated land-tax and the absentee surtax, it
might have done a good deal better had it made
even the bad bargain which Daglish provisionally
fixed up. In that case, at any rate, when it
boomed again it would be booming its own pro-
perty and not an absentee’s. Westralia needs a
policy now, and not merely a negative or a string
of negatives; and it is up to those who have
thrown out Daglish’s one vigorous action-—with a
considerable hole in it —to supply that policy. To
merely yap out “No,” and leave the foreign octo-
pus where it was, won't save the country.
Premiar Morgan’s itfeglected Duty?
It is fully time for all good and patriotic Aus-
tralians to once more bail up Premier Morgan, of
Queensland, and ask him when he is going to do
his duty in the matter of bringing to book that
virulent slanderer of Australia, Horace Tozer,
Agent-General. The ordinary member of the
Stinking Pish party can’t be directly brought to
book because he holds no official position, is not
directly responsible to anybody, and is often too
vague in his si aiders to be easily nailed down to
any distinct statement. Tozer is the remarkable
exception, and Tozer’s case seems to be the one in
which it is possible for an Australian State
to effectively expose the falsehoods and slan-
ders of the Stinking Fish gathering, and to do
it in a way that will fix the attention of
England on the exposure. A little while ago
Tozer was reported to have puolicly stated
that the legislation of Australia was so nefarious
that it was preventing the investment of from
£20,000,000 to £40,000,000 of capital in the
country. If this amount is the capital of
absentees, then it is much better for this country,
which owes a great deal too much already, that it
shouldn’t be invested, but that is not the question
at issue. It is the duty of the Queensland Govern-
ment ta demand particulars from its hireling
Tozer, seeing that it is the only Australian
Government which can get at Tozer. It should
require from him a list of the sums which make
up this £20,000,000 or £40,000,000, the names and
addresses of the people who own these sums, the
businesses in which the money was going to be
invested if it hadn’t been for “Socialistic legisla-
tion,” and, if possible, the dates when the schemes
of investment were formed, the dates when they
were abandoned, and the particular items of legis-
lation which caused their abandonment. If
Tozer was not simply slandering out of his
own imagination, he must be able to supply these
facts, or at least a good many of them, and he
should be invited in a most peremptory fashion to
supply them. If he fails to do so, or if the
alleged facts he supplies fail to stand investiga-
tion, he should be discharged from his position as
Agent-General, he .should be declared by special
Act or resolution unfit to re-enter the Queensland
Legislature, and the reason of these doings should
be published all over England Premier Morgan
is certainly understood to have informed Tozer
that his language was doing serious injury to Aus-
tralia, but that was a weak proceeding and wholly
insufficient. Tozer, no doubt, was doing injury
to Australia, but the question at issue was
whether he was doing injury to Australia by
courageously telling the truth, or by meanly and
disgracefully hawking round a parcel of lies. Sup-
posing Tozer, the one Stinking Fish person who
can be officially called to account, were convicted
of gross slanders, that proceeding would also con-
vict many Stinking Fish persons who can’t be
directly brought to account. If Premier Morgan
doesn’t take this chance—he being the only man
in Australia who has such a chance —he will fail
badly in his duty to his native land.
