The “ White Ocean ” Policy.
G. H. Reid has broken out with a new series
of misrepresentations in regard to the clauses in
the Australian Postal Act Refusing mail- subsidies
td any vessels employing colored labor. At last
elections, scorn of this “ white ocean” policy was
a trump card in his hand. The facts as to that
clause, as shown in the official records, are :
July 10, 1901: Senator Glassey moved, in the Senate,
for inclusion in the Postal Bill of a clause forbidding the
grant of mail subsidies to any but white-labor boats. His
motion was defeated.
July 25, 1901: J. 0. Watson, in the House of Repre-
sentatives, moved the adjournment to call attention to
the increasing employment of colored labor on subsidised
mail steamers. Edmund Barton, on behalf of the Govern-
ment, said that the Cabinet would use its best influence
to prevent any employment of colored labor that could be
avoided on mail steamers. G. H. Reid announced that
he was heartily with the Ministry in any efforts which
might be made to diminish the employment of colored
labor “on board ships related to us under postal con-
tracts.”
Aug. 14,1901: W. M. Hughes, in the House of Reps.,
moved an amendment in the Postal Bill to practically the
same effect as that proposed by Senator Glassey. Decision
was postponed. Sept. 4 : Alfred Bearin' proposed that
other means rather than a direct prohibition in the Act
be adopted to achieve the same end, with which he had
every sympathy. After discussion, the amendment of
W. M. Hughes was finally accepted by the Government
and carried without division. Neither G. H. Reid nor his
deputy, W. M'Millan, ncr any member of his party, voted
against the proposal.
Outside of the official records there was a little
comedy. The Barton Government at first was
against the inclusion in the Postal Bill of a clause
prohibiting mail subsidies to colored labor boats.
It sympathised with the object, but disagreed
with the means. In those days the liKiD-ites had
always their ears to the Labor caucus keyhole
listening for some suggestion, by agreeing with
which they could oust the Government. The
“ white ocean ” clause seemed to be just the thing.
The Government was against it, the Labor party
in its favor. The RkiD-ites prepared to vote with
the Labor party. The Government heard of this
and upset the plan by agreeing to the clause. It
was on a White Ocean that Reid once hoped to sail
into office. Now he has the impudence to stump
the country condemning the clause which he and
his party supported, and to an extent instigated.
Sydney D.T.’s financial authority has been inves-
tigating the local electric tram system—as yet a
baby—and finds that the up-keep charges have
been quite double those of British and American
systems. He promptly draws this conclusion :
The Australian Government workman has, we know, to
be considered. He wants high pay, short hours, holidays,
overtime, and he won’t stand piecework, or pay by results,
on any terms, as he has to do in America and England.
The efficiency of labor here consequently must be put
down at less than one-half in making comparisons.
That is certainly the most important factor vvhich we
have to contend against, and it has to be recognised that
for a Government department to attempt to deal with
its employees on commercial lines would be an outrage
on the privileges of the Government service.
Which is mostly gratuitous assumption. Labor
charges in U.S.A., for instance, are probably at
least as high as in Australia. But a little further
on in the same article the writer admits :
Since the above was written we have been informed
that out of working expenses since electrification heavy
rails have been substituted for the old permanent way,
and entirely at the cost of revenue. The very narrow
groove in opr tram rails is also a source of expense, as it
requires special cleaning, and makes a heavy demand on
the rolling stock, while the wider grooves in other
countries reduce these charges materially. The heavier
currents supplied to our motors are also a source of
expense. And the air-brakes have been costly, but the
electric brake now being adopted will be less so. With
our gradients we must have exceptional brake power, and
our exceptional conditions at many points have to be
admitted.
So there intrude these special reasons to account
for the high up-keep charges of Sydney trams
(1) The replacement, out of revenue, of the permanent
way—a very costly undertaking.
(2) The narrow-groove rail used, which makes the track
safer for cycles and wheeled vehicles, but is far more ex-
pensive to maintain than a wide-groove rail.
(3) The heavier current to ensure greater speed.
(4) The superior brakes to make the service safer.
(5) The extraordinarily heavy grades.
There is not the least doubt that these five factors,
taking into account the enormous expense of the
rail replacement (a temporary charge which will
soon vanish), account for. the great bulk of the
extra up-keep charges. But the D.T. doesn’t
withdraw its mean slander that it is the Australian
workman who is responsible, and that his efficiency
is less than half that of the European or American
toiler.
Cameron, of Tasmania, wasted the time
of the House of Reps, recently with a story about
the grievous destruction of trade by reason of the
Sea Carriage of Goods Act. That Act makes ship-
owners responsible, like any other carriers, for
damage if the goods in their charge are destroyed
or depreciated through negligence, and it was
rendered necessary through the action of shipping
companies, which had gradually altered their bills
of lading till at last they took no responsibility
whatever, and could destroy everybody’s goods
with impunity. Cameron had a vague, ignorant
idea that this Act was a device to benefit the
worker instead of being a law for the protection
of the exporter and importer, and under that im-
pression he raved at large and spilt imaginary
facts around like water. It was only when he was
fallen upon with a crash by Dugald Thomson,
Glynn, G. B. Edwards, Knox, M ‘Williams,
Kennedy, Lee, and J oe Cook that Cameron fully
realised what a failure he was.

It is comprehensible that a Foreign-trader may be
anti-Australian, Tory, pro-nigger, prOrJap., etc.,
but it is not often that such a person, being a
white Britisher, a boy of the bulldog breed, a
citizen of the “Empire on which the sun never
sets,” openly advertises himself as a coward, and
advocates the abasement of a white man’s country
before the Asiatic. Yet, on Aug, 7th, an Aus-
tralian legislator, one Senator Pulsford, sent to
Melb. Argus a long article, from which that paper
quoted excerpts with enthusiastic approval,
urging Australia to “retrace her steps in regard
to the denial to the Japanese of admission
to Australia, in the light of recent events which
have made Japan one of the most powerful of
world powers. ’ Putting aside the compara-
tively trifling misstatement as to “the denial of
admission to the Japs”—there is no law specifi-
cally barring out Japs, and as a matter of fact
they seem to be entering the Commonwealth
pretty fast —it is hard to imagine anything more
spineless or contemptible than the tfpectacle of a
' “ son of the dominant race ” thus grovelling pub-
licly before Asia. Pulsford’s plea is simply
one of terror because Japan has become a “ world
power.” The reason why Australia isn’t also a
world power is want of population. It can’t get
population for want of work to employ population,
and it can’t supply the work because the
Pulsford crowd persuades it that if it makes
things for itself they may cost a farthing morg

l SYDNEY. A
Few journalists in Australia were more
widely known and respected than Mr. James P.
Dowling, who died suddenly of pneumonia this
week. As editor of the Sydney Mail, Mr. Dow-
ling had a view of the responsibilities of jour-
nalism which is, unhappily, rather rare in Aus-
tralia now. A man of broad and generous
spirit, he took it as his duty to know the
mind of the community, and to be acquainted
with all its different interests. The Mail did not
perhaps give as much scope as was deserved for
his talents. Originally a working miner, Dowling’s
first experience in journalism was with the Fair-
faxes Jand he concluded, a few months ago, a third
of a century’s service with that proprietary.
When the alleged bones of Paul Jones
were brought from Paris, the other week, for re-
burial, a good many of the excited populace
shouted “Vive Jones ! ” They saw a procession,
and hearing that it was on account of one Paul
Jones, they wished him health and prosperity.
Touching George Ousted Reid’s continual
wailing; why doesn’t he go forth into the middle
of a large and lonely night and erupt one cat-
scaring bleat and have done with it? Or, better
still, why does he not apply D.C.L. Whisky to the
afflicted part internally ? No man who takes
D.C.L. to his bosom in well modulated enthusiasm
can long remain afflicted. Borrow soon ceases to
be his bedfellow if he takes a nobbier of D.C.L.
for a “ night-cap.” Poor old George must adopt
the “night-cap custom.*

“ Jack Shannon,” the last of the Andrew
Mack plays at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), was put on
for four nights only, and these included a super-
fluous three. “Jack” is wretchedly poor melo-
drama, in which the author seems to have relied
upon his memory for the plot and upon a pump
for the humor; and the way in which that old
pump creaks while the action of the play is stopped
to allow the “spontaneous” Irish wit to flow forth
is something sinful. Having got the hero (who,
of course, is A. Mack) into the usual difficulties
by the usual means, including the usual substituted
bank-note, the conspirator can’t think of any better
way of getting him out than of making the heavy
father wander into a room while his son, duped by
the dark villain, confesses to the hero that it
was he who robbed the safe and did the
other bad things with which the hero is
charged. Then, when the confession is made and
the good and basely-accused young man is shown
up as something extra-special in the way of
righteousness, the heavy father is observed for the
first time, standing in the middle of the room.
“ Then you have heard all?” . . . “ Yes, all.” . . .
“ Bless you, my children ! ” And you go home to
a snack of cheese or to Paris House for some-
thing that costs more—which depends on whether
you have your wife with you or somebody who
isn’t.
Seen once, Andrew Mack shows you just
about all that he can do. At least one judges so
from his Sydney season. Perhaps it may be the
fault of his parts; at any rate* he has'been the
same Mack, the same young, rollicking Irish lad,
in the whole four —making love in the same
quizzical way, meeting much the same difficulties,
in the same way, grappling with villainy in the
same way, making much the same excuses for
singing much the same songs, and finding the
same apparent difficulty with his collar whether
it was Tom Moore’s collar, or Shaun the Post’s, or
Jack Shannon’s. An easy, confident, brisk actor,
who gives a good account of Irish blarney, who
warbles himself close up to the affections of the
audience, and then is invited to come closer to the
public heart because he is so fond of the children.
The actor who dandles the stage-kiddy on his
knee, and gives her a pick-a-back, and is called
“Dear Mr. Jack,” and told that he mustn’t go
away—that actor has his battle half-won. “ Jack
Shannon’s” stage-kiddy, Maisie Posner, has a
good deal more than usual to do, and so far as
the elocutionary part of the business is con-
cerned does it specially well. Miss Lovett
has little to do beyond expressing an unshaken!
confidence in J. Shannon through three acts, and
she expresses it quite satisfactorily. E. Heron,
who had achieved great popularity through
various absurd extravagances (playing “comic
relief,” to whom ordinary laws don’t apply) pre-
sented a much more real character in the servant
O’Donoghue ; and for the rest, with one or two
trifling exceptions, Mack’s support was wooden—
heavy bluegum, mostly. For this Thursday and
Eriday “ Tom Moore” will be repeated - consider-
ably the most striking play in the collection, and,
when you have forgiven its historical inaccuracies
worth seeing twice. After that, Miss Nance
O’Neil and “Marie Antoinette.”
** * *
The Great Thurston will close his season
at Sydney Palace next Saturday night, after a
highly financial run of eight weeks. Some new
tricks are promised for this week—in fact, the
capacity of Thurston for producing new tricks is
only equalled by the capacity of men, women, and
children for being tricked. This remark is meant
in a complimentary sense, but it is one of the
things that might have been expressed differently.
** * *
Sydney has witnessed so few productions
of the home-made comic opera that the production
of “The Coquette,” at the Palace Theatre, for
six nights and a matinee (beginning August 28),
should excite interest. The book has been written
by W. J. Curtis and Jno.T. Hunt, and the music
is by W. Arundel Orchard (conductor of the
Sydney Liedertafel). New scenery and costumes
have been prepared. Mr. Orchard will conduct
a large orchestra and full operatic chorus, and Mr.
W. Curtis will play the comedy role of Jeremy
Pymble, an insurance agent. Miss Minnie Hooper
is arranging the ballets and incidental dances.
The State Governor, the Admiral, the Chief
Justice, and other citizens have consented to “The
Coquette ” being placed under their patronage.
** * *
“Mrs. Dooley's Joke ” has been developed
this week at Sydney Criterion, and by any other
name it would be as funny as it is now. It once
called itself “Mrs. Goldstein,” and in the Gold-
stein days Mr. John Sheridan was a Jewess. Now,
as Mary Ann Kelly, alias Mrs. Dooley, he is a
luscious old Irishwoman, beautifully dressed, and
bediamonded like the masthead lights of Togo’s
fleet. There are some things in this world that
never die; cats and mules are imperishable, and
matter is indestructible, and the old root joke that
is founded on the mother-in-law defies the hand of
Time. It and the joke about the nigger and the
rooster were the two jests that Noah took into the
Ark so that humor might not wholly perish off
the earth. The mother-in-law joke is a good
deal frayed, of course, but it is still capable
of drawing an audience. The one that came to
the Criterion on Saturday to see Mrs. Dooley play
her joke made a great din of welcome over the
impersonator. “ The kind of woman lam under-
stands min,” says Mrs. Dooley, winking towards
the front row of the stalls. The audience responds
with a whirlwind of laughter. The joke begins
to crack with a club scene. A crowd of gay young
blades in evening dress sharpen their wits on each
other and puff cigarettes. Many of the club “men”
are girls—and these do most of the puffing and
strutting. No one in Heaven or earth ever saw
club men with such hair as these masqueraders
wear. A Paderewski crop is understandable, but
a damsel in satin knee-breeches and coat, and
posing as a man, yet wearing a hideous, smooth
erection of hair-frame-work is enough to make a
motor-car shy. There is a long list of hard-
working characters in the piece. Miss Maud
Amber and Winfield Blake do some interpo-
lated turns, and the Joke, which is rather wild
and incomprehensible all the time, closes on
Flemington Racecourse. Next Saturday the
Broughs take possession of the Criterion.
** * #
“A Sailor’s Sweetheart”—a melodrama
new to Sydney—will be staged at the Royal on
Saturday by William Anderson’s co. It promises
to be of a better type than that phantasmagoria of
outcries and sensations, “Her Second Tune on
Earth,” and it could hardly be of a worse type
without a special effort that would be heard all
over the city. It is generally possible to guess
the character of a bellowdrama by its title.
“Jack’s Love ” is generally reasonable and idyllic,
and has a tendency to gather up the shells by the
seashore, and to wander through flowery lanes,
and scratch the nose of the spring calf as it moos
at the lovers over the hedge ; and there is no great
strain put on the public’s credulity until the last
act, when the hero is saved from burning at the
stake in the centre of Africa by the opportune
arrival of a detachment of British tars on a motor-
car. But “The Blood-stain on the Hearth” or
“ The Worst Wolf in the World” gives warning
of a reek of horrors and a general turning over of
restless bones in the tomb. “ A Sailor’s Sweet-
heart” should be sweetly reasonable, provided the
sailor doesn’t pei’sist on shivering his timbers or
throwing one leg in the air sideways while he
hitches up his baggy pants.
The Tivoli bill has been furbished up con-
siderably; and it was getting to want a coat of
something. Yet there is nothing big in the way
of new-comers; it is rather a case of the whole
crowd brushing up a bit. Fanny Powers is back,
popular as ever : but they won’t be able to call her
the marvellous child mimic much longer. A lady
is blowing into the cornet; Milo has gone to Sydney
parks for new poses, and lifts Andromeda, Bac-
chante, and other scantily-draped ladies on to the
shady pedestal in the stage-carpenter’s garden ;
Winton is still reproving MGinty in the usual
manner; and Florence Challis and others help to
give good value for your money.

Last week died William, familiarly known
as “Pete,” Hughes. He came to this country
many years ago with John F. Sheridan, of
“ Widdy O’Brien” fame, as advance agent. Pie
also, on emergency, occasionally played the nigger
steward, and sometimes the nigger wench, Bella.
His sobriquet of “Pete” was derived from a
, habit he had of addressing everybody else as
“Pete,” or else from Pete, the steward above-
meiTtioned. He afterwards devoted himself to
theatrical management, and for a very long time
was under engagement to J. C. Williamson. He
was also very successful on his own account.
When the last Italian opera company, which
numbered among its members the Cutticas, Sisco,
Melossi, Travaglini, and conductor Maffezzoli,
brought to this country by the late Martin
Simonsen, came to calamity in Sydney, “Pete”
took it in hand and safely piloted it overland to
Melbourne, making money for himself and the
artists. There were no frills about “Pete.” His
phraseology might have sometimes shocked those
who talk in the Johnsonese language ; but he was
a kind-hearted citizen, a good friend, and a very
poor specimen of an enemy,
** * *
Most things happening at the Melbourne
theatres when this Bulletin is born into the
world will be faded and gone ere next Sabbath.
The one exception is Bland Holt’s amazing war
drama at the Royal. “ Besieged in Port Arthur ”
will continue until further notice, admired for its
feast of scenery and flow of supers, but dis-
’ respected as an historical romance. Probability
gets no show in the Russian fortress. A mob of
unaccountable peasants are somehow admitted
into General Flogakoff’s banquet-hall when the
ballet is being served. These weird intruders
turn out to be Jap soldiers in disguise,
headed by Walter Baker, Britain’s noblest
son, and seeing that the Muscovite tyrant is
bound to whistle for assistance and wipe the
intruders out, the stage manager saves the situa-
tion by plunging the scene into darkness. Next
time Baker comes to light he is a bogus Russian
priest sporting the hooded cassock of a comic
opera, friar, and in this queer capacity he gives
Fldgakoff further impudence. Any mishap to the
Japs or their white brother Baker is quite out of
the question in Bland Holt’s drama. Although it
is said to have come from London (the brighter
part of the dialogue having been subsequently in-
troduced in Melbourne by Edward Hyson) it
breathes all the sentiment of Cole’s pro-Jap
booklets, and The Bulletin suspects that Cole
aforesaid was the author of the original sin.
** * *
To-night (Thursday) is the last Melbourne
will see of “ lolanthe,” by the Gilbert and Sullivan
Repertoire Co., whose members have served their
three weeks at Her Majesty’s with much credit to
themselves and profit to the management. Howard
Vernon and Kenningham play their old parts, and
Miss Ghiloni this time is the Fairy Queen, instead
of the long-banished peri who rises from a duck-
pond in a dry state. Miss Dolly Castles, dainty
Aggie Thorne, Frank Wilson, Ralston, and Ernest
Fitts are new to this opera in Melbourne, and their
combined resources of youth, freshness, and vocal
strength entitle them to the positions they hold in
front of a charming chorus.
** * *
“ The Cingalee ” brings the Comic Opera
Co. back to Her Majesty’s after an absence of six
months or more. Half a year is a long time for
Melbourne to be parted from its trusty frivollers.
On their re-appearance this Saturday (26th) the
audience will, in a manner of speaking, take them
to its bosom—a good deal of which will be laid
bare for the occasion.
At the Princess’s, the wholesome attrac-
tion of “Quality Street” comes to an end this
(Thursday) night—for the present. The Brough-
Flemming people may be expected to return anon
to repeat what- they have done this trip, and do
other things they left undone, but meanwhile they
have other arrangements. Nellie Stewart and co.
are coming along on Saturday to play a lively
season of 12 nights. A comedietta and a “ roman-
tic farce,” both new to Australia, will make the
opening programme, and Nellie Stewart will
appear in the one and the other. In the other, it
is said, she will figure as a mere man most of the
time.

Adelaide has followed its early view of
11 The Cingalee ” by a late one of the “ Country
Girl,” an unfamiliar play of which the music was

Corporation is talking of prosecuting both theatres

Dr. Nansen, the Arctic explorer, has

fishermen begging him to accept the Norwegian
crown. He should be better than a whole row of
German princes,' anyhow.

COLUMN
'r ——
The July issue of N.S.W. official Monthly
Bulletin, compiled by the Government Statistician, throws
some more light on Carruthers’ character as a financial
reformer. In Waddell’s year (1904) the publication says
that the total loan money spent on whollv non-productive
works was £292,480 ; and Carruthers said that Waddell
was so shocking a Treasurer that he would have to take
his place and save the country. Now Carruthers’ own
officers show,- in Carruthers’ own publication, that in
Carruthers’ year (to June 30, 1905) £299,876 was spent on
wholly non-productive w'orks—that is, £7396 more than
Waddell spent. Under these circumstances, what ought
to happen to Carruthers ?
* * * *
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers has stated proudly
that bis surplus—which has now dwindled to £142,000
is the largest the State has had for 10 years. Yet nine
years ago Reid swore that he had *a surplus of over
£330,000, and Carruthers also swore to it, though the
Auditor-General, the Statistician, and the Special Com-
mission appointed to report on the accounts found instead
a deficit of some £600,000. Does Carruthers now say
that he and Reid were both liars nine vears ago?
* * * ,
“G.” : The Victorian Government has seriously
discounted the value of its recent sensible doings, by the
decision to sell the g'rounds of the Kew Asylum in resi-
dential blocks, and to use the money so obtained as an
endowment fund for the maintenance of the insane. Bent
does not explain how he intends to obtain as much
interest from the money he will have to invest as the
people who buy the land will get from their tenants.
Neither does he say a word about providing any means by
which future Treasurers will be prevented from dipping
their hands in this special endowment to pay off other
debts pressing at the moment. There is absolutely
no way of guarding against any future Treasurer, with a
truculent manner and a servile majority, from “blew-
ing ” the endowment for the insane in political two-up.
A land endowment is a different thing. The Kew Asylum
grounds are, in point of position, the very pick of'Mel-
bourne. From the time of their being opened up thev
will probably fetch not less than £3 a foot, and with the
railway, which forms part of Bent’s scheme, they will
form an ideal residential quarter, high, healthy,
clean, out of the dust and smoke of the city, and still
within seven minutes’ train journey. The proposal to
shift the asylum gives the State a magnificent opportunity
to do what the more advanced municipalities of the world
are doing. By subdividing the area in a proper manner
and erecting villas, Bent could keep his brick works
going for three years and make a big profit. When
the work was completed, Bent would have a ready made
suburb, the houses of which he would be able to‘ fill on
five years leases by tender. If such a scheme were
properly and economically carried out, instead of receiving
3 per cent, for his lunacy endowment fund, Bent
would probably be able to obtain nearer 8 per cent.,
and besides providing for the care of the State’s lunatics
he would be able to buy up the Gillies debentures, issued
on the security of these grounds, from year to year. The
handing over of the grounds to private syndicates means,
instead of a homogeneous suburb of the best hygienic
conditions, the erection of architectural eye-sores, and
the possibility of a grasping syndicate piling up slum
tenements on its particular slice.
♦ ♦ dfc
M.C. : Mr. Sandford says the iron industry
would give employment to 30,000 men. But what Aus-
tralia has to glue in front of it is that, to accomplish thi -,
High Protection on all the secondary processes of iron
manufacture is called for. About 70,000 men are em-
ployed at the British blast-iron furnaces ; so our local
trade would have to equal nearly half that of Britain to
give employment to 30,000 men' in producing merely
primary products. The germ of the idea—so far as
greatly enlarging the scope of employment is concerned-
lies in Protection for the manufacture « f nails, rods, bars,
wire, and galvanised goods. Comparatively few hands
would be required to produce the pig-iron consumed in
Australia ; but the trade would be of immense advantage
as it would form the basis, under Protection, of a great
industry, or, rather, a dozen industries. American iron
ore, of about the same assay value for iron and phos-
phorus as most of the local ore, fetches from 12s. to 16s.
per ton at the smelters ; and the low price of iron ore,
and also of pig-iron, precludes any hope of much wages
being earned through the primary business of iron pro-
duction. Some of the secondary manufacturing, includ-
ing the galvanising, would, on the other hand, distribute
over £1,000,000 per annum in wages—if it had sufficient
Protection.

The Waihi Mine management (M.L.), is making
extensive additions to the milling plant in order to be
in a position to deal more effectively with the sulphide
ores which will for the future be the product from their
lower levels. Concentration will be a special feature in
the treatment, and the company accordingly, before
deciding upon the type of concentrator to be adopted,
made exhaustive tests of the different machines pro-
curable in the market and finally decided to adopt the
Wilfiey Concentrator, and have placed an order for 25
tables with the Austral Otis Engineering Company, South
Melbourne, sole manufacturers for Australasia. This
firm has just completed and shipped the machines to the
mine.* #

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coaled steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sig'n of decay, and may last as long again. Spiial
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making’ boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discriiition of cast or wrought iron work.*

Tin has touched £152, but the market could not
maintain the price, which has fallen below £150 again.
In the commercial metals no other market is so erratic.
For a day or two in 1888 £170 was quoted; that was
during the silver excitement —formerly tin and silvei
usuallv moved more or less together. Next year tin was
down to £75. Forecasts of the future of the market seem
utterly worthless ; the same yarn regarding, scarcity of
supplies and enormously increased demand has been told
during every tin boom, and the enterprise that figures on
maintenance of present prices flies in the face of all past
experience.

The irregularity of the profits and the bulk of the recent
writings-off are explained by the fact that the society puts
to contingency account in the first half of the year half
the estimated amount of the year’s rates, and writes the
amount off when the rates are paid. The progress seems
satisfactory and the position solid. A comparison :
1901. 1904. 1905.
Deposits £35,763 £29,297 £30,490
Foreclosed Properties .. 71,938 61,519 60,741
Advances 20,875 27,813 26,208
Cash, &c. 2,169 4,370 9,288
Net Rents p.c. of Capital
Value .. .. .. £5 13 6 £6 10 3 £6 1 6
The fact that a steady profit is shown on properties sold,
coupled with the very satisfactory net rental realised,
indicates that the writing down a few years back was
sufficiently thorough. The capital is £47,797 ; so that the
borrowings (£30,490) are still not heavy. Apparently the
average rate paid for deposits is 4 p.c., and received for
advances 8 p.c., and that is a sufficient margin to make the
business attractive. On the society’s own funds (capital
and reserves) the ordinary business profit shows a fraction
that doesn’t matter under 6 p.c.

The directors remark that “ the acquisition of the busi-
ness of the late Mr. George Adams will help to increase
the profits ” ; but the purchase is not shown in the pre-
sent accounts. The accounts are silent as to depreciation.
The freeholds are very extensive—standing at very
nearly twice as much as the capital (£108,000)
—and it is a matter of much concern whether
the writings-off are sufficient to cover depreciation. There
is nothing- on which to base an estimate as to whether
they are or not —it is not even certain that anything at
all is written off. So far as current operations are con-
cerned, substantial progress seems to have been made;
expenses have been considerably reduced, much more
than counterbalancing a small shrinkage in gross profits
from sales. But the load of debt is very heavy, and the
doubt whether any or sufficient allowance is made for
wear and tear of plant and premises, &c., makes the load
look even heavier than usual.

Broken Hill and Suburban Gas Co. (N.S. W.):
Added to Re-
Profits. Dividends. Reserves. serves.
1904 .. £2401 6 p.c. =£1694 £707 £5636
1905 .. 2986 6 „ „ 1694 1292 6928
The co. is 17 years old, and apparently the total written
off buildings, mains, and machinery is £4556. The cost
was £31,753, so that in all 14 per cent, has been provided for
depreciation—only 14 per cent, for 17 years’ wear and tear.
It is pretty certain, therefore, that the reserves are larg-ely
or entirely nominal. All the same, the recent additions
have been very satisfactory. The better showing in the
matter of profits in the. past year compared with 1903-4
was almost wholly due to coke sales. Fuel is worth
nearly as much as silver at Broken Hill, and the revenue
from coke was more than one-seventh that from gas. All
but £2831 of the co.’s capital (£28,229) and reserves are
invested in the business. *

the figures probably don’t mean much. As something is
only very rarely written off plant, &c., which stands in at
£BO,BOl, the reserves are most likely paper reserves only ;
and the reported profits not all real profits.

and the voice of the charmer that asks for an increased
div. should be thrown over in the deepest part of the
harbor any dark night it is heard.

The co. has £145,041 of capital and owes the public
(mortgages, &c.) £142,744. It has properties of the book
value of £269,417, and other assets total £1277. During
the year the city property item has been reduced by
£13,563 ; the loss was £8165, so apparently £5398 was
netted for it. At that rate of realisation the assets are
worth £lOB,OOO. Whether, however, the property that
was sold was a particularly bad bargain in the first place
it is impossible to say. The directors say that it was.
Two or three years ago Richardson and Wrench valued
the properties at a price which left the co. with £40,000
odd ; and if that valuation works .out all right, then the
past year’s sale must have been something unusual in the
way of disaster. But whether the money is there or not,
the waiting to realise promises to be a pretty tiring pro-
cess. On the average book-value of property held during
the year, the gross rents (£10,739) show about £3 18s. per
cent. ; deducting ground rents, repairs, taxes, and insur-
ance, the return was £2 13s. 6d. per cent. And, as before
pointed out, the co. is carrying £142,744 of debt, which
costs 44 per cent. The co. is now calling up £1 per share
(there are 16,038 live shares), and with the mortgage re-
duced a trifle it expects, to get its money for 4 per cent,
instead of 44 per cent. ; and that will be some small help
to a concern that needs such heln badly.

This, by the way, is the industry into, which it was
recently announced English newspaper man Newnes and
others had been induced to put half-a-million or so, the
thick end of which was going to the owners of the shale
properties. There was for the moment some mighty
crowing over “ the return of prosperity,” and the daily
papers said how pleased they were to observe that capital
was once more forthcoming to develop our great natural
resources; there wasn’t a word of warning about the
hopelessness of any such absurdly capitalised concerns in
such a business under present conditions. If they export
their shale oil, they have to compete with the products of
works run at much lower labor-cost; there is a very limited
local market, and that is subject to the same competition ;
and the candles produced by this co. have, even in the
Australian market, to bump against foreign goods pro-
duced in countries where,in some cases, the workman is
paid only about half as much as he is here.

The figures for the half-year are excellent; gross earnings
from commissions, etc., amounted to £4617, against £3848
in the first ha f of 1904, and £3409 in 1903. Profits are
divided at the end of the year.
The troubles of the big Equitable life-assurance
office illustrate how an apparently good law is liable to
spring up and hit you. It isn’t the Equitable’s fault,
primarily, that it isn’t a mutual office —it is the fault of
just such an Act as that. For 50 years it has been the
law in New York (and England has much the same law)
that no new life office may start without depositing
£20,000 with the Government; and that provision, which
looks just the thing to prevent wild cats getting their
claws into a confiding public, has had the effect of prac-
tically barring the purely mutual office. The law pre-
vents the public being scratched at the start all right;
but, as it turns out, in some cases it only postpones the
scratching till the claws grow longer and stronger.
V * * *
The sort of thing that is liable to happen to
policy-holders in the 1.0. F., or in any of the “ fraternal ”
insurance affairs, has been happening to the people who
went into the Mutual Reserve Life Co., in England. An
insurer lately sought to recover on the g-round of mis-
representation ; he had been told in 1898 that he would
only have to pay Bs. 4d. every two months, but the assess-
ments grew till they reached 16s. Bd. Then he kicked.
But Justice Barwell told him to save the kicks for him-
self ; his only course was to drop out. Still, the court
gave its own opinion of the “fraternal” humbug by
refusing it costs with great scorn.

From the half-year’s g'ross profit £22,478 has to be written
off for depreciation. The price realised for lead was £ll
6s. 4d., against £lO 14s. in the May-November half-year,
and £lO 6s. Id. twelve months ago. Silver has been Jd.
better than in the previous half, and IJd. better than 12
months ago. Compared with the first half of 1904 the
improvement in the lead market has been responsible for,
say, £35,000 of the extra profit and the better silver mar-
ket for £14,000 ; together £49,000. Practically, therefore,
the whole gxtra profit has come out of improved markets,
assuming that the ore treat ed has continued of the same
grade. In addition to the ore shown above as treated,
61,786 tons of slimes were re-treated —roasted and either
smelted or prepared for smelting. This is an increase on
all previous records, and accounts for probably the whole
of the apparent increase in the metal contents of the
ore. The extractions of metals appear to have been
slightly improved, but costs have been rather heavier;
and practically, except for the improved markets, mat-
ters remain just about where they were 12 months ago.

Announcement that B.H. Prop’s past half-
year’s profit per ton of ore was 9s. lid., against Bs. sd.
previous half, and 7s. twelve months ago, needs explain-
ing. It works out at that if only the crude ore is taken
into account. But, then, in addition to crude ore, 57,301
tons of slimes were smelted last half, against 47,103
previous half, and about 40,000 tons in the half-year
before that ; and this stuff is supposed to give a profit of
over £1 per ton. Besides, 12,851 tons of zinc concentrates
were produced last half, against 8104 tons previous half.
The extra profit hasn’t thus by any means all come out of
the crude ore which has been passing through the mills
and smelters.

When the B.H. Prop, issued its prospectus, on
June 29, 1885, it looked a fairly cheeky proposal. Of the
16,000 shares, the promoters took all but 2000 ; they were
offered for £9, all being then issued as paid to £l9. The
promoters took £3OOO, leaving the co. with £15,000.
Years afterwards, when the co. wanted to he quoted on
Bull’s Exchange, the odd £1 was called up and at once
returned. The provisional directors were George M‘Cul-
loch, of Mt. Gipps station (on which Broken Hill stands),
Bowes Kelly, Harvey Patterson (still the chairman), the
late K. E. Brodribb, W. Jamieson, W. A. Horn (an Ade-
laide broker), J. W. Bakewell (an Adelaide solicitor), the
late W. R. Wilson (the one man in the crowd who knew
much about mining), and S. F. Hawkins, a sub-manager
on M‘Culloch’s station. Bowes Kelly, Patterson, and
Brodribb were squatters up that way. Charles Chappie,
then of Silverton, now of Adelaide, was secre-
tary. There was a shaft down at that time to
150 ft. and two crosscuts through the lode ; a sample of
five bags assayed 118 oz. “The whole width is believed
to be highly payable,” said the prospectus. They work
Boz. ore nowadays and make their profit from it. Still,
in case the 118 oz. stuff shouldn’t be payable, the generous
promotors threw in half-a-dozen other leases—one of them
at the Nine Mile, five or six miles away, “ traversed by a
strong galena lode, giving fair assay results, and which
may in itself prove a valuable property.” Three or four
claims were worked for a few years at the Nine Mile,
but the district has long since been abandoned. In spite
of the added attraction of a claim at the Nine Mile, the
2000 shares took a deal of pushing. One of the best
“ barrackers ” the show had was J. S. Reid, who then
ran the long-since-defunct Silver Age at Silverton, and
didn’t have to trouble about running a way-back weekly
paper much longer. Silverton, which is now a township
consisting of a deserted brewery, an elaborate gaol which
hasn’t been used for years, and a large number of semi-wild
goats, was then the hub of the Barrier ; Purnamoota and
Thackaringa were the fields (they now support about a
dozen men each), and Broken Hill was an outsider. In
fact, Charles Rasp, the Mt. Gipps boundary-rider who told
M‘Culloch it was worth pegging out, reckoned that it was
a tin mine ; it was the.-black, rough ironstone capping of
the vast lode that deceived him. M'Cullooh, who had
the biggest original interest, and stuck to it (for many
years, at any rate), has been living in England for 15
years ; his hobby now is pictures. Rasp settled in Ade-
laide.
* * * *
The first shipment of ore from the Barrier to
Europe hasn’t been delivered yet—and it s just 30 years
since it left. P. Green was a storekeeper at Menindie, a
township on the Darling which was more or less flourish-
ing in the days of Burke and Wills, and 60 miles or so
from the field. Occasional travellers from way-back
brought in slugs of galena ; the black stuff that overlaid
the richer lode didn’t attract the non-professional eye.
At last Green went out and pegged The Pioneer, at
Thackaringa, got out six tons of the stuff that looked
brightest, and put it on a river boat. Something hap-
pened to the boat, and the Barrier’s first shipment was
thrown overboard. That damped Green’s enthusiasm ;
and it wasn’t till 1881 that any more ore was sent away
from there.
* * * *
B. H. North will be the first Barrier mine to
put the De Ba\ay zinc process to practical use. This
system, first mention ;d 12 months ago, is a Melb.
chemist’s invention, and depends on flotation of metals.
Some Block 10 people were early interested in it, and it
looked as though that mine would have first show ; but
the North has fixed up an agreement, under which the
De Bavay Co. will put up a plant to treat first 150 tons
and then 1000 tons a week, the co. selling its tailings on a
percentage basis, exact terms of which have not been dis-
closed.
* * * *
The Smelting and Refining Co. of Australia,
nominal owner of the Dapto (N.S.W.) works, is being
wound up, and the Consolidated Nickel Mines, Ltd.,
mostly of New Caledonia, takes the concern over. The
Smelting Co.’s record has been about as bad as it could
be. It was a blunder to put the works at Dapto, in the
first place ; and that was followed by something worse
than blunder. When Kalgoorlie was gouging out its mar-
vellous stuff, these works made a good contract for the
ore ; on the strength of the phenomenal output (of some-
body else’s gold), shares were boomed to over £2. It was
to pay the co.’s for this gold and let them declare prompt
divs., that the Smelting Co. got heavy advances 'from
Dalgety’s, who eventually shut down on the co., and then
the bailiff walked in both in London and at Dapto. Some
of the methods used to work up the 1902-3 boom smelt
worse than a butter scandal. After the rich Kalgoorlie
stuff was done, output slumped,.,of course; and then the
co., in its struggle for ore to keep its works going, tried
to knock out some other smelting concerns, and got badly
mauled itself. The part of J. Bull that bought at £2 and
over will probably curse Australia for taking it down ;
though the fact is that it was Bull eating Bull all the time
—Australia was simply an onlooker from first to last.

* * * *
Golden Pole, Davyliurst (W.A.), is still the
shuttlecock of the market. Main points of contention
are tonnage of ore reserves, possibilities of 400 ft. level,
and success or failure of residue treatment process.
Nat. Harper, who is now consulting engineer, is opti-
mistic on all these points, and estimates ore reserves
at 50,000 tons, or two years’ supply. This is nearly double
what he estimated them at six months ago, since when
no new level has been opened, although old levels have
been extended, and 14,000 tons treated. On face of it,
one of these estimates appears haphazard. As to treat-
ment of residues, the refractory nature of the ore is
admitted, and the residues have been allowed to accumu-
late owing to this. The erratic nature of the reef makes
it difficult to estimate reserves, and the other two points
in debate can only be solved by time. It is noticeable
that the grade of ore is gradually falling. In May it was
worth 119 s. per ton, with a profit of £B4OO, whereas in
July it was worth only 108 s. 6d. per ton, with a profit of
£7500, only enough to meet current div. This is, of
course, exclusive of residues, worth 30s. a ton if the gold
can be saved.
* * * *
North White Feather, Kanowna (W.A.), con-
tinues to improve its position. During July co. treated
1900 tons for a profit of £lBOO, and, from present appear-
ance of mine, it looks as if this rate of profit could be
maintained for some time. It is, however, useless to
overlook the fact that reserves of ore are far too small to
give any real security, though this drawback is being
remedied by degrees. Co. is over-capitalised, and is not
in strong hands.

WHISKY
certainly as healthy a resting place, and some-
times as comfortable, as man or woman could
desire. It gives a wide outlook on life and
broadens the mind. Getting up is good exercise,
and getting down strengthens the muscles. In
some places whole communities of people sleep in
the trees, using ropes of vines as ladders. As
near as New Guinea such habitations are common.
There are no pigs or usurers up trees, and very
few anti-Socialists or people who take secret
commissions. Where there are wild beasts trees
are indispensable ; where there are larrikins and
brass, bands they are useful; and even where there
are none of these, trees, regarded as sleeping
abodes, are far superior to the average humpy.
Some years ago the writer recommended a squatter
to burn the shearers’ huts and make his men camp
in the trees along the river, but, like the police,
lie thought it against the law, and stuck to the
old pig-stye habit of housing his men.
. * * * *
Experience of the St. Vincent’s Hospital
(Melbourne) art union impresses again the evil of
the system which leaves to “ private charity ” the
upkeep of hospitals, and seems to necessarily entail
crooked methods of stimulating that “ private
charity.” St. Vincent’s organised an art union
with money prizes. That was in itself illegal.
The prizes bore a shockingly meagre proportion to
the value of the tickets sold, so that the public got
nothing like a fair gamble for its money. To crown
all, a swindler so manipulated the drawing that
all the big cash prizes fell to him. These
latter incidents are, after all, only of importance
in calling more marked attention to the facts
that the lottery was illegal, and was so conducted
as not to give anything like a fair gamble. It is
pitiful that the nuns of St. Vincent should have to
adopt such methods to enable them to carry on
their hospital work. The State should take
over all hospitals—employing, maybe, present
organisations such as St. Vincent’s as the French
State and municipal hospitals employ the Sisters
of Mercy—and put an end to these art-union scan-
dals. An even more unpleasant art-union
scandal than the St. Vincent’s was that of
Mt. Kembla (Sydney). Ostensibly to help the
widows and orphans of the Mt. Kembla disaster,
an art-union was organised with motor-cars as
prizes. Nearly £3OOO worth of tickets were sold,
and all the money was absorbed in expenses, etc.
Not one penny reached the Mt. Kembla fund.
» * * *
The N.D.L. steamer Prinz Waldemar, on
its last trip, brought 23 Chinese stowaways as far
as Sydney. The din that ensued is still ringing
in the company’s ears; but on its very next
voyage the ship managed to leave Kobe with 14
stowaways on board. One morning a young
Japanese girl was found strangled on the deck.
The officers, in their stolid German way, regarded
this discovery as suspicious, seeing that no women
were legitimately on board. After a search, 13
brown girls, all in their teens, were found hidden
in the lifeboats, cn route to Hong Kong for the
brown girl’s usual purpose. It appears that this
illicit traffic to Hong Kong and other ports is
carried on to such an extent that hardly a vessel
leaves Kobe or Yokohama without an attempt
being made to send out Japan’s best-known
export. The story of the strangled girl is still
wrapped in gloom.

Father Hays, the latest teetotal crusader,
has hardly reached his home yet, and already the
bottom is dropping out of his good work. A Mel-
bourne woman, who sued her husband lately for
maintenance, assured the bench that “he is good
to me when he is sober. We both went to one of
Father Hays’ lectures and took the pledge. A
whole lot of us went, but the men have all turned
back, and I was the only woman amongst them.”
♦ * * *
Melbourne Telephone Exchange stands
convicted of having knocked off business for a
while in the middle of the other day, in order
that the entire staff might get itself photographed
in a group. The group photograph is a pet vice
with every sort of organised body of Melbourne
folk, who can get the picture reproduced in
Paunch or elsewhere. Cricketers, footballers,
rowers, golfers, bowlers, tennissians, croqueteers,
&c., &c., have got it badly. The employees of
Brown’s factory, Jones’mills, and Smith’s empo-
rium are willing subjects for the photograph
fiend, and why shouldn’t the Telephone staff
figure as a band of heroes? Their group photo.,
which caused all the subscribers to be “rung off”
for 20 minutes or so, is being officially treated as
a public outrage because it fetched a com-
plaint from the Fire Brigade headquarters, but
as regards ordinary Telephone ratepayers the
Exchange might as well be closed half-'an-hour
every day during the luncheon period. One may
certainly get an answer to a ring, but the answer
(from the Exchange) is always the same. The.
number asked for is “engaged.” You may ring
ten several times at intervals of three minutes and
the answer is ever—“engaged.” On subsequent
enquiry from the subscriber whose number you
couldn’t get “on ” to, you learn that he also was
unable to ’phone to anybody at the particular
time when the Exchange adopts an obstructive
policy. Perhaps the Government will “issue
such instructions as will obviate any similar occur-
rence in the future.”
* » »
Victorian Law has just made an exhibition
of itself in an unusual fashion. A Sydney girl,
visiting some Melbourne friends, developed mania.
She was not supposed to be dangerous and needed
little more than care and attention to ensure her
recovery, so her relatives were sent for. In the
meantime the local police asked that she be re-
manded —on the usual charge of “insulting
behavior.” But the P.M. decided that there
was no evidence of riotous behavior, and
that if she were non compos she must be so charged,
examined and sent to an asylum. This was done.
A few days later, her brother came from Sydney
to take her home, and learned that, under Vic-
torian law, when once the State has put a lunatic
in its foul, antiquated, overcrowded asylum, he or
she cannot be surrendered until completely cured,
or dead. So the brother had to go home leaving
his sister in an institution where hardly anybody
is ever cured, and where the neglected lunatics
are piled on top of one another for want of space
and breathing room.
Launceston Examiner, starting out to.
curse Industrial Arbitration, the other day, re-
marked :
Mr. Want, the N.S.W. Attorney-General, has reluctantly
acknowledged that it is practically impossible to compel
men to work if they are minded not to. Anyone with
any pretensions to perspicacity would have recognised
that from the outset.
A wise newspaper ! But, incidentally, a leader-
writer without pretensions to perspicacity, per-
spicuity, perceptivity or peripateticism, but with
just a little permeability or horse sense, could easily
ascertain that Mr. Want is not N.S.W. Attorney-
General. It is an unconvincing gentleman who
preens himself pompously on his perspicacity and
proudly peers prophetically into the future, and yet
is as shockingly ignorant of the political present
as the platypus or the porcupine. Such a party
ought to go and peck with the poultry or precipi-
tately pursue the pig across Paphlagonia.

BROWN’S SATIRE IN A CITY CHURCHYARD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is proposed to build a market on the site of the old
Melbourne cemetery.
Here lie the bones of pioneers,
The strug'glers of the early years,
Who came to gather in the gold,
And gained a six foot bed of mould.
They left the over-peopled North,
With courage and with tears went forth
To where the flowing leagues lay fair
To which the Son of Toil was heir.
They dreamed an ample ardent dream,
Pursued an ignis fatuus gleam,
Of gold, across the ocean flung
They followed—they were bold and young.
A large and loving continent,
Benignly smiled in blandishment,
And whispered “ Wealth ” in undertones
She gave a place to lay their bones.
They clenched their empty hands and died,
Thej' cursed the mocking gleam that lied,
Or ’chance they prayed that underground
The fortune of “ Deep Peace ” be found.
They gathered peace, the grasses grew
Above their bones, and ’neath the dew
It seemed a calm neglected spot
Wherein the monuments might rot.
O skull of Yorick ! empty long,
A theme for philosophic song,
There in that niould dissolve the hearts
Whereon men come to build their marts.
Ye rude forefathers, where’s your right
To crumble on a building site ?
How dare ye cumber Beal Estate
While flurried market gardeners wait ?
Off with your too immortal bones,
A new race ail this city owns.
Your deathright and our birthright clash,
We know no sentiment, save Cash.
For old Mortality or young,
You’re dead, and cannot use your tongue.
The dead must give the living room
To barter victuals o’er the tomb.
Perhaps your souls, in their abode,
Are not in anybody’s road,
But having a perpetual lease,
Ad infinitum rest in peace.
\ ic.
J. BROWN.

WHAT UPSET HER.
MISTRESS : “Aren’t you well, Mary?”
MARY : “No, mum.”
MISTRESS : “Taken something that hasn’t agreed
with you?”
MARY : “ Yes, mum—this situation.”
Fresh appointments to N S.W. public ser-
vice have, by decree of Carruthers, been stopped
as far as the public examinations created by the
Public Service Act (1895)are concerned. Vacancies
in the junior ranks are generally tilled by recruits
from the ranks of pupil teachers. This has brought
about an anomalous position. When the embryo
State’school pedagogue reaches the dizzy height
of £4O a year he is subjected to an examination.
If he passes he is entitled (on paper) to an increase
of pay,(some day), and is set on the thorny road
whose goal (in theory) is the Under-Secretaryship
of the Department. Should he fail, he is trans-
ferred to the clerical division of the Public Service
in some other Department at the minimum of
£5O, an immediate rise of £lO, with the right to
a definite system of increments. Experience
shows that those fortunate enough to fail at the
test are doing much better than those stupid
enough to pass, and it is rumored that pupil
teachers have begun to realise this, and intend to
reduce failure to a fine art for the future.
The evidence in the case of young
Farrelly, charged in Melbourne with larceny from
Reuter’s Cable Agency, threw some further light
on the methods of commercialism. Reuter’s
manager said that when a message was handed in
it was coded (a charge being made for coding),
and the sender charged then at schedule rates;
but “ afterwards the message might be re-coded ;
if it could be reduced to one word we would be
justified in doing so. We make all the profit we
can.” Reuter's paid Farrelly at first ss. a week ;
he rose gradually by shillings and half-crowns to
335. a week in July, ’O4. In three of the cases
on which he was committed Farrelly was charged
with pocketing trifling sums, this re-coding differ-
ence. It is not a wise company that plays such
tricks on the public, and then employs a 335.-a-
-week clerk to work them. And people are already
beginning to wonder if it would be any more
honest for the co. to pocket the difference than
for the co.’s clerk to do so.
* * * »
It is considered that bylaw 36, lately
gazetted by N.S.W. Railway Commissioners, im-
posing a £5 penalty for throwing empty bottles
out of carriage windows, will press severely upon
itinerant temperance lecturers. These are terribly
thirsty men, some carrying, even on short journeys,
as many»as four bottles of cold tea and three of
lemonade, and, if not allowed to throw the empties
out of the window, the accumulation will give un-
charitable people room to scoff. A movement is
on foot to induce the Commissioners to withdraw
the bylaw.
“ Gr.H.” : I witnessed a tragedy in a
chiffon-emporium last year. Mrs. Blank, wife of
a citizen who paid a
few small brown coins
in the £ a few years
ago, drove up in her
gorgeous kerndge and
requested to be shown
some passementerie.
The shop - pedestrian
provided the stately
Mrs. Blank with a
chair, expressed his
approval of the way
God was making the
weather, and then re-
tired. Presently he
branched off to bow to
Mrs. Dash, of Toorak,
who had driven up in
the Stanhope main-
tained by her pore in-
solvent husband. Mrs.
Blank arose from her
chair, and the shop-
walker, thinking that
she wasleaving, handed
the chair to Mrs. Dash.
Mrs. Blank, however,
on second thoughts,
sat down again where
the chair had been but
wasn t. She struck the
floor with a great noise,
and the managing-
director rushed out of
his office with the idea
that the shop-walker
was drunk and had
knocked down a
wealthy customer. He
found Mrs. Blank in a
state of dishevelment
on the carpet, whilst
Mrs. Dash looked on
with loud applause.
The Blanks and the
Dashs are in two differ-
ent sets —though the
two husbands filed the
same kind of document
in the same year —and
they hate one another
a good deal. This was
a year ago. The inci-
dent was recalled to
my mind when I recog-
nised the shop-walker
last week sweeping the
doorstep of a poor little rag shop. His life’s
career ended, I presume, the moment Mrs. Blank
struck the floor. A shop-walker sacked with a
curse instead of a character has as poor a show as
a captain who has lost his ship and taken to drink-
ing in waterside public-houses.
Bananaland seems to have abandoned all
hope of ever exterminating its prickly-pear, which
now holds undisputed possession of tens of thou-
sands of acres. The new Land Bill proposes to
give the worst of the infested lands free to occu-
piers on long leases, but this concession will pro-
bably only attract men to .take up the best patches
on the areas not yet thickly over-run. In some
districts the pear, in a dense, impenetrable mass,
stands 20ft. to 30ft. high, and if it were not for the
spines, a man might walk on the top of it, or,
possibly, drive a dray there. Some clearing re-
cently carried out by the State cost £lO per acre,
and if the cleared land is not quickly put under
crop, the evil will soon be as pronounced as ever.
The man who can discover a means of economi-
cally getting rid of the prickly curse will be a
benefactor. The Bulletin, by the way, knows a
man with a scheme for destruction by electricity.

“ J.P.” : This is the “ less” age —horse-
less carriages, wireless telegraphs, workless men,
hopeless women, godless parsons, mannerless chil-
dren, worthless politicians, faithless statesmen,
and toothless inhabitants.
Concerning
telephones
and what you
pay for not
getting what
you want: —
The number
of ways in which
you can pay for
a telephone ser-
vice, and not
get what you
want by any of
them, is amaz-
ing. Generally
speaking, how-
ever, you get
more for your
money in Aus-
tralia than in
any other part
of the world.
In London, Pri-
vate Enterprise
supplies for £l7
a year what in
SydneyandMel
bourne you can
get for £9 (or
£5 in the case
of a private
house); or if
you don’t like
to pay £l7, you
can pay £5 sub-
scription and a
penny per call.
New York
charges are ap-
palling—£l2 a
year for 600
messages, and
thereafter on
the basis of
about 4d. a talk.
Private Enter-
prise in tele-
phones takes
care not to hit
Private Enter-
prise in tele-
graphs too
hard ; for the
two have, to a
great extent,
only one pocket
in New York.
What London
pays £l7 to Pri-
vate Enterprise
for, Glasgow
gets through its
municipality for
£5 ss. ; and
there aren’t
many services
in the world
cheaper than
that. In Stockholm, Sweden, the annual subscription is
£4 Bs. XOd. ; but there is an entrance-fee of £2 15s. 6d.
Tasmania has the cheapest service in Australasia, the
tariff getting down to £4 10s. for a private house ; and S.
Australia, where they charge £lO for business telephones,
has the dearest. In Switzerland they have a sort of sliding
scale ; the longer you have been ordering the breakfast
sausages by ’phone, the less they charge you fordoing it,
till you can almost order them for nothing.
* * * *
Justice Homburg, of S. Australia, has
begun to play tricks with his liver. In the Ade-
laide Criminal Court, during the winter sessions,
he sat one day from 10 in the morning till past
midnight; next morning he was at it again at 10
and sat right through till 3 o’clock without a
break. When the hungry jury, after hearing
what looked like a hostile summing-up, filed out
and then promptly filed back again, and said
“Not Guilty,” Judge Homburg couldn’t believe
it. “Of course I must accept your verdict,” said
’sonah; “but” (to the accused) “if the jury
had known your record, the verdict might have
been different.” Nobody told the Judge that if
the jury hadn’t gone without its lunch the verdict
might also have been different. And yet it’s just
as likely. The previous day, a man, who had been
following an intricate case for about 10 hours, had
to fight for his liberty shortly before midnight;
and midnight was striking when a fagged jury,
which had been sitting since the morning, had to
decide whether the man was guilty or not.
* * * *
Ballarat appears to be a place where you
are liable at any moment to be assured on the
highest official authority that you are dead and
have been in that state for some time —if you
weren’t born that way. Recently a mining in-
vestor was served with a notice to the effect that,
being dead, his name was evidently on the electoral
roll under a misapprehension that the Absent
Voters provision extended toanotherand a better—
or much worse—world ; and now the local registrar
has been officially called upon to furnish particu-
lars regarding the death of a solicitor whose dead-
ness up to that time hadn’t occurred to the person
most interested in the matter. If this sort of
thing goes on, when anybody at Ballarat happens
to remember that he is alive, it will become neces-
sary for him to go to the paper and advertise to
that effect.

A condensed love story by M. Lloyd :
“ What will Gustave say when I tell him I have a billet
at $2 a week ? ” mused Elizabeth, waiting for the train.
“ Will he say, reproachfully: ‘ Why talk of mercenary
things ? Sordid girl! You have no billet outside my
arms.’
“ Will he say, jealously: ‘I cannot spare you. I want
you all to myself. It will be the crowning joy of my life
to take care of you and shield you from toil and care and
worry and billets.’
“ Will he say, rapturously: ‘Billet? Hush, beloved!
Words are vain sounds. My sight is so ravished by your
dear face that I can hear nothing. Ah, this blessed
night! ’
The train arrived.
“ Gustave, I have a billet at £2 a week,” said Elizabeth,
thrillingly.
“ Is it permanent ? ” said Gustave.

Thusfar the published explanations of how
the Melbourne St. Vincent’s Hospital lottery
swindle was worked have been quite as unsatis-
factory as the swindle itself. The first statement
turned upon the allegation that two barrels of
marbles were necessary to the drawing, and this
has not been denied by the official scrutineers—
presuming that the performance was scrutinised
at all. One barrel contained marbles numbered
from 1 to 999, the other was loaded with marbles
representing “thousands,” and a marble was
drawn from each barrel simultaneously, the total
number of<possible combinations being 419,000, it
was said. Nothing was said of the impossibility
of a prize going to any ticket numbered below
1000 if each drawing was to include a marble from
the barrel of thousands. At that rate the lowest
possible combination of numbers would be 1001.
But a second edition of the swindle story was a
flat contradiction of the first as regards both
the issue of tickets and the working of the
fraud. The public now had it that only
50,000 sixpenny tickets were sold (the expenses
amounting to nearly half the receipts) whereas it
had been previously stated that “ about one third ”
of 419,000 tickets were sold. And the story about
the two barrels became shrouded in incomprehen-
sibility by the further discovery that Naughton
had really drawn the five winning marbles from
one barrel which seemingly contained the whole
50,000 numbered in rotation. Or, rather, he drew
the five marbles by trickery from a tube where he
had secreted them. The mystery as to how
Naughton secured all the prizes by means of five
marbles from only one barrel, if there were two
barrels employed in the drawing, is a profound
mystery in itself. “Explanations” are further
complicated by the indiscriminate use of the words
1 ‘ barrel ’or “ barrels ” in the conflicting confes-
sions and reports which have been published. If
the truth has come out to the satisfaction of the
police and press inquirers—where is it? Did it
come out of one barrel or two bari'els ?
* * * *
A Melbourne Age man recently displayed
feverish anxiety to pile admiring epithets on a
much-boomed detective who knew the elements of
his business well enough to search for evidence, in
the St. Vincent’s art-union ease, in the marks on a
blotting-pad. The idea is probably as old as the
invention of blotting-paper. At any rate, the
detectives of Wilkie Collins and other old-time
fictionists followed it out; and many a jealous wife
or husband in real life has sought traces of love-
letters to the third person in the same way.
Surely it is not necessary to gush over a detective
because he remembers one of the most ancient
tricks of his trade.
MES. GRUNDY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Lovely morning, Mr. Jones ! ”
“ Perfect weather, Mrs. Grundy ! ”
Listen to their quiet tones,
While the Voluntary drones,
Out among the old tombstones,
After church upon the Sunday.
For a virtuous name she bears,
And has dainty, modest airs,
And a charming frock she wears
When he humps her books on Sunday.
But where’s Leander Jones,
And where’s Diana Grundy,
When the full moon floods the sea,
Telling of inconstancy,
And the stars appear to be
Less fixed, and more than free
At ten p.m. on Monday?
Laughter rippling on the sand,
Hearts that surely understand,
Earth and sea go hand in hand,
In spite of church on Sunday.
“ Lovely morning, Mr. Jones ! ”
“ Perfect weather, Mrs. Grundy ! ”
They can’t disguise their happy tones,
After church next Sunday.
In vain the Voluntary drones
Out among the grey tombstones,
E’en the best ancestral bones
Tire of eternal Sunday,
When o’er them sweet Diana Grundy
Whispers to good Leander Jones,
“ Ten p.m. on Monday.”
N.S.W.
D.


Charles Heidsieck

It was sworn, at an inquest on a gas-stoker
in Melbourne, lately, that “there was an inch of
coal-dust on the floor of the stoke-house. There
was coal-dust and grit in deceased’s eyes, nose atid
mouth. The lungs were distended and congested,
the heart enlarged and dilated. The back of the
throat, bronchial tubes and windpipe contained a
quantity of black dust and grit. . The cause of
death was suffocation from the inhalation of coal-
dust.” Verdict accordingly. No remarks about
the gas co. which allowed that inch of coal-dust on
the floor of the stoke-house.
