A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 22, 1905.
My Dear Arini.
The first beauty-parlor in Sydney run by a man
is in full sesthetical swing in Linden Court. The
man beauty-cultivator is assisted by'a pretty
woman. If they could only get patrons to believe
that the lady had been before “treatment” a
hollow-eyed, freckled apparition with excavations
in her cheeks and lines on her neck, no shine on
he*- hair and too much shine on her nose, they
would do nearly as well as if they were land
agents. Said that members of Parliament are
extensively patronising the parlor. They don’t
mind one of their own sex helping them
attain polished, pearl-like finger nails, to wave at
the Speaker when they rise to develop the great
natural resources of the country, also rippled and
essenced hair like that with which Macaulay says
Charles I. fled from Nasebv, sharply-defined eye-
brows, and an absence of dewlap. When the
legislator’s beauty starts to hang upon the cheek
of night like a rich jewel in a Kanaka’s ear the
sooner the ladies’ gallery is extended all round the
House the better.
Rupert Clarke’s yacht La Carabine is again
in one of our sunlit coves of peace. Her
owner acquired a lot of fine pearls whilst among
the divers in Northern waters, but he seems to
have become home-sick, or perhaps horse-sick.
At all events, he gave the yacht over to a captain
(other than his brother) and hurried off to the
Caulfield races. Yachtsmen smiled smiles the
size of carpet bags last week when the S.M.H.
mentioned that La Carabine had passed some
place or other with her fore-topmast gone. She
never had one.
At a dinner at the Australia, given lately to the
potentates of the Prince Alfred Yacht Club, an
appropriate menu was of fine canvas, like delicate
holland, with a tiny seascape painting, and the
word “menu’’was not spelt out in the ordinary
plain fashion but was represented by signal flags
embossed on the document.
Concerning feeds : If your taste has been culti-
vated, how you recall with sentiments akin to
religious exaltation some elegant browse that
refuses to fade into the infinite azure of the past.
The other day a man was talking to me of
Trieste, apropos of the Panther’s visit, when he
suddenly laid his hand on his solar plexus, turned
his eyes to the zenith where the hallelujah is sup-
posed to be, and said, “ Will I ever forget a meal
1 had there, a few miles inland, at a low-built,
vine-covered hostel! They gave me a leveret
cooked with herbs—oh ! ” And he gave a sigh
like the sound of a large engine coming out of a
great tunnel. I believe hypochondriacs suffer the
same way. I remember hearing an impassioned
description from a bad specimen of the tribe of
some unusual symptoms he had under the shadow
of Pike’s Peak, and a certain bottled remedy he
tried at Yosemite. He said nothing about the
scenery. To him it seemed that a mountain
14,000 ft. high was only a favorable site for
symptoms.
The best-dressed woman on the stage this week
is Mr. John Sheridan in “Mrs. Dooley’s Joke.”
His pale-gold satin evening dress, embroidered
with iridescent beads, is a superb outfit, and the
bunch of crimson roses fastened just where
you get appendicitis does not clash with his
carrotty wig. And his hat in the second
act several inquiries as to the whoseness
of his milliner went round to him. Curious
thing—this actor looks much older in his make-up
as a woman than he does as himself on the stage
or in the street. Concerning the “Joke,” there’s
a really truly beautiful girl among the supers.
She hasn’t a line to say. She sits in the back-
ground and dreams or smiles through the dances,
and everyone says she’s more lovely than Mrs.
Maesmore Morris was at her best.
Mr. T. E. Donne, superintendent of Maori-
land’s State Tourist Department, ran through
Sydney last week, on his way home, after seven-
teen months of American andjOld World travel-
ling. He actually has the U.S. brand on his
speech. That shows the personality—no, the
nationality —of America. Mr. Donne was born at
Tooralr, but left that haunt of double-breasted
plutocracy for M.L. while still in lace pinnies.
He has been on the tops of the highest build-
ings and at the bottoms of the deepest mines in
the world, he has bowed before thrones and
presidents and has been affable to the person
right at the depths of the submerged tenth. He
has looked in every direction where finger-posts
point to tourist resorts in all parts of the world,
and has closely examined the manicuring of the
hand on the post and the way it indicated. Some
of the accumulated knowledge he will utilise for
the International Exhibition to be held at Christ-
church early in the new year.
“Narcissus” Count Peretti etc. is resident in
San Francisco as French pro-Consul. San Fran,
is in luck ! The pictorial aspect of Sydney’s
French Consulate faded with the departure of the
Count and M. Bi.ard D’Aunet. The first looked
such a young-romance-in-being, the second such a
romantic ruin. To put it another way one was
a beautiful sunrise and the other one of the best
things possible in sunsets.
Talking of San Francisco, someone sends me
half a page of an American paper showing a
picture of artist Frank M‘Comas—he was a
Sydneysider—cake-waking out of church with his
bride on his elbow. Probably a snapshot. The
kodak can make the normal look very grotesque.
Miss Marie Louise Parrott, who married Mr.
M‘Comas, is described as a Baltimore belle with
money. You may remember some of M‘Comas’
oaks and cypresses painted in Monterey, and ex-
hibited here about four years ago.
Talking—or jerking—some more about painters
(this week the Artists’ Society opens its Spring
show), I read in the memorials of Edward Burne-
Jones that when he was at work on the Blessed
Damozel he used to travel twenty miles a day to
make studies of the cherry blossoms in a friend’s
garden. In view of the fact that a painter friend
of mine will show a sunflower study at the Arts
Society show, I am moved to quote E. 8.-J. :
“ Did you ever draw a sunflower ? It is a whole
school of drawing and an education in itself. Do
you know what faces they have—how they peep
and peer, and look arch and winning, or bold and
a little insolent ? Have you ever noticed their
back-hair, how beautifully it is curled ? ”
Apropos of Mr. Andrew Mack buying a copy of
“Thomas Little’s Poems” in Sydney a friend
writes
One day in a second-hand bookshop in Newcastle I
turned up in one heap of sixpenny books a partially uncut
copy of “ Thomas Little’s Poems,” an original edition of
“L.E.L.’s Improvisatore and other Poems,” and “Gay’s
Fables” with the original woodcuts. A friend borrowed
“Little’s Poems” and lost it. Two or three years after-
wards I was visiting a sick sailor in one of the slums and
found my copy of “Little” in the room. Some of the
leaves had been torn out for shaving papers. I also bought
quite recently for a shilling a copy of “Bailey’s Dictionary”
and (uncut) two volumes original edition of Paget’s
classical work on Surgical Pathology. Who brought
those books to Australia ?
Dr. Knowles, who married Miss Lily Titheradge,
has started practice in Newcastle. The bright
Lily—still fondly remembered as one of “Two
Little Vagabonds”—was by way of being a tall
bloom on the stage, when she quitted it and a
good salary, to marry her (then) medical student.
Returned travellers talk in notes of exclamation
of the dancing of Madge Titheradge, saying that
she’s the most graceful caperer since Kate
Vaughan.
One of the Euryalus’ aristocratic pieces of artil-
lery (Mr. Blackwood) is very ill in a Sydney
hospital, and the Australian girl who is en-
gaged to him, is sorrowful in consequence.
Talking of Australian girls’ engagements to
British men-of-war: Lady Eanshawe has such
a horror of them that report says she takes
to her bed when each announcement is ,
made. To do her churchy Excellency justice, it is
probably the engagements of non-financial officers
to penniless girls that drive her to a horizontal
position. The naval bi'ass-bound Somebody with
little means cannot afford to marry and hope to
rise in the service, unless the girl supplies the lack
of money.
The things Sydney girls are saying about the
Australian and Union Clubs are enough to set
the clubs’ chimneys on fire. It’s all over the
110-ball-this-year announcements. Poverty, corns,
old age and ingratitude are some of the charges
hurled at the clubmen. But the Euryalus men
will give a dance, so there’s still treacle in Gilead,
as the Treacle Bible puts it, if I remember rightly.
Two of the most delightful personalities in
Sydney these days are Bishop and Mrs. Camidge,
of Bathurst. The lady is a huggable prelatess.
How the cold, chaste beauty of the sculptured
figure does draw, to be sure ! That lady who
poses as a marble statue at the Tivoli has a good
audience—mostly male—every night. Audience
could see the Venuses and Dianas she represents
at first hand in the National Gallery, but of course
the Tivoli is more central than the Gallery, and
it’s gratifying to see the “ statue ” come out of her
trance, and somehow the thing that pretends to be
the other thing seems to draw better than the
other thing itself. Also it is so tantalising to
have only a 20-seconds view of a lady mid
next to nodings but a suit of whitewash on.
,Just as those who “have seen tons of
this sort of thing in Paris ” are sizing up the
image as showing the results of the wearing of
corsets, and telling their neighbors that all the
classic antique female statuary, except the Milo
. and Genetrix Venuses, exhibit the figure of a girl
of not more than 18, darkness drops upon the
luminous white form. As living statuary is so
popular, why doesn’t the Tivoli management try
a group on the public. The “Abduction of
Polyxena ” and the “ Laocoon ” would go well, and,
as male perfection of form is more common than
, female—or the male says it is—there ought to be
no lack of models.
Considerable questioning and answering go
on about the lady who lived at a fashionable
hotel, had a private sitting-room, and played
bridge till the morning star faded to palest silver.
Also as to which man she married. For report
says she was married last Wednesday in a riding
habit.
Talking of very recent brides. One of the inter-
esting species, whilst still in the elect stage, held
a private view of her clothes and presents and his
clothes and the gifts presented to him. The
banana-colored and coral-pink pyjamas elicited an
“ O, my !” of great intensity.
At the preliminary canter at Randwick races
last Saturday, the most looked-at pair were Mr.
and Mrs. Frank Gardner. The lady was in an
up-to-date tailor-made.
Miss Eadith Walker is preening herself for a
voyage to Europe, and I’m told, with more or less
truth, that “ one of Die leading frock makers will
be engaged with her 50 assistants for a month
exclusively on the wealthy one’s clothes.”
Somebody arrivals of the week include Mr. and
Mrs. John Potter, of an English steamship-owning
firm—Birt, Potter and Hughes. The visitors are
camped at the Australia.
Mrs. Williams, wife of the Surgeon-General
Williams who did something very tine and large
out in Africa, where the veldt comes from, is, with
her 17-year old daughter, on a visit to Sydney,
and staying at the Metropole. Mrs. Williams is
a Maorilander, and a sister of Mr. Monty Severn.
An advance-Australia girl, Miss Memes, living
at Balmain, has started as a motor-car-for-hire
proprietor. She has a fine, big, olive-green De
Dion, and she herself is a good driver. She does
not, however, drive her clients. A motorman does
that.
The American Business Man’s Magazine,
July, has a good bit by a lady, on Parisian shop-
ping. She is struck by the universal courtesy, so
different both from America and England. Worth
is no longer ahead, except in heavy stuffs and lace.
Beer is the top couturier. Doucet leads in ball
costumes, Paquin in walking dresses and riding-
habits. Denova is the crack tailor. The Magasins
du Louvre continue unequalled, with the Bon
Marche second, and Magasin du Printemps third.
An Adelaide correspondent writes :
“The Cohen-Krantz wedding, at Adelaide
Town Hall on 16th, was a very smart affair.
There is no doubt the Jewish marriage service can
MRS. W. N. WILLIS.
give points to most knot-tying ceremonials in
picturesqueness and in comfort to the victims.
There is no uneasy and self-conscious ‘ .wait ’ for
the bridegroom before a roomful of guests. Nor
does the bride consider it necessary to be the
orthodox 15 minutes late. On the stroke of three
the Cohen party, with the bridegroom in attend-
ance, entered the hall and took up its stand under
the velvet canopy, while the Jewish choir sang
the bridal chorus from ‘ Lohengrin.’ ”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“Lord Dick Nevill is booked for the private
secretary’s post at StOnington. Melba is also due
here presently, with her big son. The two facts
must have muddled the Yankee journalists, who
immediately associated them, and engaged the
songstress to Lord Dick.
“Rupert Clarke has had the cobwebs cleared
from his luxurious flat, and is abiding there for a
festive while. The little pink fairy bower seems,
a woeful waste when used as a bachelor establish-
ment. The curious have gone up in battalions to
view the flat. ! There’s nothing like it in the whole
State of Bent.
“A Paris rag-journal, in dealing with the
question of corsets, prescribes a crinoline filling
for the bust in the case:of flat figures. A plush
lining crinoline filling ! Ironmongery sounds
terrible under the circumstances. At the end of
the skirt, it was formidable enough —but for the
bust, a hoop arrangement would break up the
most sentimental situation. Edwin snatching a
crinoline-busted Angelina to his waistcoat is a
ludicrous notion. No Edwin with any suspicion
of humor could do it a second time. Pneumatic
pads are more reasonable if Angelina must assume
a contour that Nature didn’t give her.
“That Florence Young hies back in a month or
so is a fact. She was not fascinated with America
or the Yank theatrical life. Australia, she finds,
is a better place than she had thought.
“Dr. Jones, the recently-imported doctor for
the insane, has found intervals for singing sere-
nades and falling in love with a Melbournecharmer.
Mamma’s consent was won, and this week con-
gratulations are being showered on the medico and
Miss Kathleen Mahony. The fair party is an
Irish beauty, and was once regarded, more or less
correctly, as an heiress. The engagement had its
origin in a meeting at the Grand. Hotel proprie-
tors should trumpet tidings of this kind all over
the hemisphere. It is an attractive ‘ ad’. ‘’Twas
here they met; ’twas here they married.’ Or it
might be mentioned in the Age : ‘ This is recog-
nised as a good marrying hotel.’
“The Cliveden widow has been at pains to ex-
plain, per post from her Thule eyrie, the allega-
tions of Mr. Charles Atkins, that she and her she-
political league refuse to take any interest in the
Made-in-Australia movement. As proof positive
that she personally furthered Protectionist in-
terests, she advances the fact that on her last trip
to the Cold Country she had several rig-outs for
the tour made by a Melbourne dress-builder, and
did with those solely, to impress the big guns of
London. Patriotism and Protectionist prin-
ciples, she claims, could go no further. Then,
with rigid dignity in her best presidential manner,
the widow winds up, re the statement in the Age
leading article of 26th July, that a member of
the league, ‘ acting as the mouthpiece of the
league,’ said certain things, that there is only
one responsible mouthpiece. And she is it.
“ Mr. Justice Darley’s dame came and went un-
heralded. The dailies drew attention to her visit,
but no one can swear to having seen the visitor in
the Northcote suite. She must have been sand-
wiched in the V.R. squad somewhere, but was
lost to sight owing either to the blinding glitter
of Dame Northcote’s emeralds or the heavy
phalanx of aides keeping the draught off their
Exes. There’s no missing our Chief Justice’s
dame in any proud flock. She stands to atten-
tion, and is well within focus of the society-gapers
wherever she goes.
“The State Exes, are taking a trip to N.S.W.,
and all the visits to schools, gaols, asylums, and
creches come to an end for a term. Governor
Talbottle lias settled it in his gubernatorial mind
that a round of the public institutions and a word
with the big beadles in command constitutes a
vice-regal’s entire duty. After having performed
such with an unbending spinal column for two or
three months (with a few balls and other painful
and back-bending distractions thrown in), he is
off on a jaunt to the Macarthur Onslows’ place,
with a brief visit to the Darleys to follow.
“ The pictures of the Rev. Robinson become a
plague. They have a forward position in several
big shops in the city, and, what is much worse,
fall out of Her Majesty’s programmes into the
heathen’s lap at the show, much to the heathen’s
disgust. While all this camera posturing and
‘My photograph’ craze goes on, some R.C.’s
wonder why Archbishop Carr, the suave and
strict, says nothing. The Rev. Robinson in
homoeopathic doses is refreshing, but as a deluge
he is a trial.
“The John Forrests have splashed out into
expensive entertaining. Last Friday they spree’d
the High Court Justices and prominent Federal
politicians, with the L. L. Lewises and the Ross-
Sodens, at the Grand. This week the Labor
phalanx are to be dined, but ,I have heard that no
enthusiasm in accepting is evinced by members of
that party.
“The dog show in connection with the Vic.
Kennel and Roost Club drew the usual stragglers
to the exhibition the other day. The she-element
prevailed. And yet the cats exhibited were com-
paratively few. Parrots we;re plentiful and game
roosters to the fore. The Argus Haddons, who
house the best-pedigree’d dogs of the State, pretty
well ran the show, and conveyed the G.-G. suite
(your N.S.W. occasional Acting-Governor’s wife
included) round. The atmosphere was trying. It
always is at a dog show.

rare migrations to these parts. H. M. S. himself

Stanbridge, a Geelong heiress, is decorative. The
Daylesford (Stanbridge) estate is rich enough to

“ The Brough-Flemmings are sociable mummers.
Before the wind-up of the brisk season they are
breaking the farewell biscuit with Melbourne pals.
Miss W: Fraser has a junket of the kind on to-day
(Monday) at the Grand. Mrs. Brough, Miss Day,
and the lesser lights are all standing behind the
steaming tea-urn dispensing the brew with hostly
grace, and will continue to do so until their going.
The usual theatrical star is not a spender to any
extent. She takes all she can get and gives
nothing. She even refuses her baptism label to
the book of signatures unless bribed with half-a-
crown. The cash goes to the Tired Actors’ Fund.
Tales of the thrift and economy shown in particular
by the Yank stars who drift to these parts are in
brisk circulation. The Brough-Flemmings are
different.
‘ ‘ The story goes that a certain popular and
hard-working Australian she-mummer in a leadijig
co. shortly secures the decree nisi from her neglect-
ful spouse, and then commits matrimony with a
desirable Dives, at present away in a remote
quarter of Australia.
“ The Melbourne Mayoress has had’flu badly,
but she bounces once more in the places of the
great, and in the eye of the public. It’s a treat to
see how Mrs. Pleasance jogs in the Mayoral har-
ness, and counts the round of duties a jolly busi-
ness.
“ A mummer writes of the Gaiety Gi.rl in the
Big Smoke : ‘To enter George Edwardes’ crack
corps, one must be born again, and born pre-
maturely. At 23, one is liable to be retired on an
old age plea, without a pension. To please the
chief, one must mouse on to the stage, tread the
plank as if it were a layer of eggshell, and have
dimples and an air of innocence. Otherwise,
there is no chance for you.’ This is the golden
age of feebleness in English theatricals. Every-
thing must be done mildly and gently. The time
is coming when the stage assassin will introduce a
murder to his victim by apologising for the liberty
he is about to take.”
Yours Affectionately, AKENEHI.


RHEUMATISM
Ointments Can Never Cure It.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
Drive Poison From the Blood.
“ 1 ■ PROVED in my case that ointments and
liniments can never cure Rheumatism,”
said John Kennedy, a retired mariner,
well-known in all Australian ports, having
been associated for years with all the big shipping
circles, particularly those of Sydney, but who is
tor the present living at 95 Arthur-street, Dune-
din. “ Nothing but a blood medicine can cure it.
After I had been rubbed sore with every mortal
liniment in vain, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured
me by sweeping the aching rheumatic poison out
of my blood. There’s no other cure for a down-
right bad case of Rheumatism.
“My case was as bad as any in N.S. Wales. I
had my first attack at sea six years ago when
taking a cargo of wheat from Sydney to London.
I had always roughed it at sea, and for a long
time my blood had been in a bad state. Off Cape
Horn we ran into a terrible ice storm. The cold
soaked right into my bones. The Rheumatism
was in my system—but that exposure to an Arctic
storm set the pains going. When every hand was
needed on deck. I had to lie paralysed with pain.
The ship’s medicine chest was stocked with all
the standard liniments, and my mates rubbed me
till I was blistered. But none of these things did
me the least real good. I remained a cripple till
we reached London.
“The London doctor sent me straight to the
Hospital, where I was treated by the greatest
specialists for three months,” Mr. Kennedy con-
tinued. “At last the head doctor told me to go
back to Australia and get a shore billet, for I could
never stand a cold climate or exposure at sea. I
shipped for Queensland, and no tongue can tell
the unspeakable torture I went through on that
voyage. When I left the ship in Brisbane I was
practically crippled.
“ I made my way to the Gulf country and got a
light station job. There I read of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. The other men said they were only
‘ for Pale People ’ —but one chap named Charlie
Morely said that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills had
cured his uncle of one of the worst cases of Rheu-
matism on record. I sent straight for a supply.
After the first two or three boxes I would have
knocked them off —but Morely said that if I
didn’t give them a fair chance to cure my Rheu-
matism, it would be my own fault if I was crippled
for life.
“Sol kept on. Soon the pains in my hands
and shoulders began to ease up. Gradually my
muscles lost their stiffness and I could move my
joints with ease. My general health improved in
fifty ways. My case was so stubborn that it took
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills a few months to cure me
—but in the end they did twenty times more for
me than the best Hospital doctors of London could
do. When I started them I was a cripple—when
I left them off I was a strong, active man. Since
then I have never had the slightest sign of Rheu-
matism. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured me for
good.”
Nothing else could possibly cure John Kennedy,
because nothing else could drive the Rheumatic
poison out of his veins. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
actually male new blood. They do just that one
thing—but they do it well. They don’t act on the
bowels. They don’t tinker with mere symptoms.
They strike straight at the root and cause of all
blood diseases such as anaemia, general weakness,
debility, indigestion, biliousness, headaches, back-
aches, kidney and liver trouble, lumbago, rheu-
matism, sciatica, neuralgia, nervousness, St.
Vitus’ dance, partial paralysis, faii.ng powers, and
the secret blood troubles that ruin the regular
health of growing girls and women. If you are
not sure whether Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are
suited to your own case, write for free medical
advice to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s
Place, Sydney. From the same address you can
order the genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills at
2s. 9d. a box, or six boxes 15s. 3d., post free.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Mrs. Willis told the N.S.W. Lands Com-
mission that, “ all her married life,” she had
obeyed her land agent.
Barton-Parkes is the curious federation of
names owned by the new Anglican rector of
Fremantle (W.A.).
Defendant in a Melbourne maintenance
case: “I intended to drown myself, but I went
to Geelong.” There is a resemblance.
Stated by a Westralian paper that John
Norton, of Sydney, has offered himself as a Wes-
tralian Labor candidate at the next elections,
Mr. Solicitor Robson, newly elected
M.L.A. for Ashfield (N.S.W.), was introduced to
the Assembly by a lawyer and a half—that is, by a
barrister and a conveyancer.
Passed out at Sydney, the other day, aged
94, Bartholomew Rush, a pioneer contractor. He
built the telegraph lines from Sydney to Albury
and from Sydney to Bourke.
The respondent in a Melbourne divorce
case arising from sectarian squabbles between the
parties bears the name of Harris Maloney. It
wouldn’t have a more mixed sound if it were
Moses Murphy or Donald Aarons.
“ Melb.” : The newspaper photos, of
Barristeress Greig were taken before her studies
ploughed into her, as George Eliot said of
Romola. I have seen one of the others.
The shockingly ignorant Sydney Tele-
graph, the other day, in the course of one short
paragraph, referred to “ Bakinin ” (meaning
Bakounin), “Fewsback” (meaning Feuersbach),
and “Kail Marx” (for Karl Marx).
F. A. Byrne, mentioned in the N.S.W.
Lands Commission business, is an old dray and
coach driver, and was for many years manager for
Cobb and Co. He is known over the backblooks
of N.S.W., Vic., and S.A., and owns up to 65
years of age.
Premier Price, of S. Australia, who resolved
to wear all Australian clothes to make his policy
speech in, nearly had to do it without a collar.
That small item of Australian manufacture was
the hardiest of all to get, and one only happened
along at the 11th hour.
Died, at Adelaide recently, Andrew
Guthrie, aged 85, a member of a long-lived family.
He leaves a brother aged 83 and a sister aged 90.
Andrew was an authority on church history, and
taught in the same Sunday-school for 54 years.
Men named Andrew generally teach in Sunday-
schools and live longer than usual.
COMMISSIONER M‘KIE, chief Australian
dancing dervish for Booth & Co.
A N.S.W. State legislator was fined for
drunkenness the other day (first offence), and the
Sydney Evening News, which isn’t on his side of
the fence in politics, characteristically published
the fact with his name. Press custom in Sydney
omits the names of drunks up for the first time.
But the legislator is an unlucky dog. He isn’t
even allowed his first bite.
An old Sydney native died suddenly last
week up Normanton (Q.) way, aged 80 years. Mr.
J. J. Roberts, a pastoral inspector of the Bank of
New South Wales, was a brother of the late Wm.
Roberts, the well-known “Old Bailey ” solicitor
of Sydney, who had totted up 86 years when he
went over. Another brother, David Foley
Roberts, settled at Moreton Bay in 1851, was
called to the Queensland Legislative Council on
its formation, and was chairman of committees.
Died t’other day at Adelong (N.S.W.),
cetat 91, Mrs. Ross, a local identity, known
familiarly as “Kitty ” Ross. Her special claim to
notice is her feat of swimming the roaring waters
of the Murrumbidgee, at Gundagai, during the
memorable flood of some 40 years ago, and her
subsequent holding out in a tree, with her two
children, for a day and a night. Despite her age,
she used to walk from her home to Adelong, and
back (six miles) for her O. A. Pension.
Charles Bonaparte, Boose velt’s Navy
Minister, is grandson of Jerome, Napoleon’s
brother. Jerome married Miss Patterson, of Balti-
more, from whom Charles is descended. Napoleon
tried to get the Pope to annul this marriage, but
in vain, though Miss Patterson was a Protestant.
Then Napoleon annulled the marriage himself,
and Jerome married the Princess of Wurtemburg.
Napoleon 111 offered to make Charles Bonaparte’s
father a peer of France if he would resign the
name of Bonaparte.
The new Speaker of the British House of
Commons, J. W. Lowther, is 50 years of age, and
by profession a barrister. He has been in
Parliament 22 years and has been Deputy Speaker
for 10 years. He wears a moustache and the
traditionists say that he must shave. Speaker
Peel wore a full beard when elected, but on taking
the Chair he shaved the moustache but retained
the “billy goat.” British tradition is a fearsome
thing, and it can’t imagine itself being called to
order by a man with fluff on his top lip.
Lieut. -Col. A. H. P. Savage, late of the
Royal Australian Artillery, who died in England
last week, was a Sydney native, born in 1850, in
Myles’ Buildings, Cumberland-street, out Dawes
Point way. Myles, who' was a superintendent of
police, erected the terrace, which stands on the
sky-line; it dates from 1842. That part of the
Rocks was then the home of Sydney’s aristocracy.
Lieut.-Colonel Savage’s father was a retired navy
surgeon, and one of the earliest directors of the
A.M.P. Society. His peculiar costume attracts
attention in the Society’s portrait gallery.
“Den ” : Do the Government Departments
receive no notice of deaths amongst public servants,
or is the publication of such notices as the follow-
ing merely another instance of red-tapeism:—
His Excellency the Governor-General, by and with the
advice of the Federal Executive Council, has approved
that William Rossi, postmaster, North Adelaide, be per-
mitted to resign from the public service, owing to ill-
health.
This appeared in the Commonwealth Gazette of
August 12, when Rossi had been dead several
months.
P. A. M ‘Anulty, the Victorian Taxing
Officer, who considered himself an officer subject
to the Chief Justice and not to the Public Service
Board, loses the fight. A somewhat similar battle
was fought by T. M. Slattery, when a prothono-
tary, in N.S.'VV. Supreme Court, the point raised
by him in a disputed case being that he was a ser-
vant of the Supreme Court Judges and not of the
Government. Chief Justice Martin, of that day,
thought Tom was doing splendidly, but the
Government held a different view, and Slattery
walked out —didn’t wait, like M‘Anulty, to be
pitched out.
E. H. Coombe, new Chairman of Com-
mittees in the S. Australian Assembly, is a country
journalist who runs one of the few way-up papers
that aren’t either a Herald, a Star, or a
Recorder ; his rag is the Gawler Bunyip. When
the Convention sat in Adelaide to fix up a Federal
Constitution Coombe was in the gallery as a
Register man ; now the Register pours pints of
gall on him. He hasn’t been long in politics,
only stepping into the place of John Downer
when the latter went into the Senate ; but he has
made good use of his time and is reckoned as good
and knowing a Parliamentarian as the House
holds.
The story of the death per dynamite ex-
plosion at Canning Bridge (W.A.) of Henry Irwin
Blake, son of the Governor of Ceylon, proves to
be much like many other stories. The deceased
was local manager of an accident insurance co.,
and is said to have been £BOO or more short in his
accounts. He cabled to his father for a large sum
of money, and the answer, which arrived the day
after his death, was “Impossible.” The co.’s
inspector reached Perth to overhaul the accounts
while the reply was still being waited for. Then
Blake remembered a promise to take a friend
lib. of dynamite to shift trees with, and he left on
horseback with the explosive. At a quiet spot he
dismounted and blew up. He left behind him a
swift horse, some tangled accounts, a wife, six
children, and some £6OOO of insurance. Also he
left many friends. The pathetic fact is that the
more friends a man has the shorter he often is in
his accounts.
The Austraxian Star (17/8/’O5) put the
photo, of the lately-deceased theatrical manager
“ Pete” Hughes in the middle of a column of in-
coherent romance headed “ He Lived and Lost,”
of which some extracts are appended :
His own Maria had been lost to him for ever, and no
number of alcoholic outings had compensated him for his
bereavement. So he mused as he walked pensively along
the deserted ways of Newtown after dark. The strains of
a sweet coon song, played soulfullv upon an accordeon,
were wafted upon the chilly air, and they recalled
memories of the depth of the deep sadness of the might-
have-been. The impossibility of the “if” fell upon him
like the wofulness of the “ was,” and Peter sighed. Again
the strains of the accordeon broke forth, wildly, mysteri-
ously terrific. . . .
She was merely requesting a Newtown policemen to get
off her foot, and give her room to trip away, but it was
her style that attracted Peter. . . .
The suburban beer-puller was cold, and looked upon the
lovers with disdain. “ Oh, you are like she who has de-
parted from her Peter,” he said ; and she said, “Am
I ?” . . .
Several times did Peter pull out his purse and distribute
its glittering contents. . . .
The gentleman who served the beer refused to take any
excuse, and threatened to fetch the police. “ Fetch ’em,”
said Peter, “ fetch ’em all. Let ’em all come.” . . .
Peter caught her by her luxuriant crop of golden hair,
and dragged her back to justice and to retribution.
And now there are people in Sydney who think
that hash of mysterious raving was intended to
relate some romantic episode in the life of good
old “Pete” Hughes, and was, in some sense, his
obituary.
A LONG CHEAP VOYAGE.
DOCTOK : “ You must go away at once, sir; forget there's such a thing as business, and go for
a good long voyage.”
INSOLVENT PATIENT : “ Well, doctor, I'll leave it to you. Prussic acid or rough on rats. I'm
not particular.”
J. Gr. Smith, correspondent for the London
Morning Post at the war, furnishes this June
number of the American World’s Work with
opinions of English, American, French and German
men of business in China. All such men that he
could find were unanimously against Japan. They
agreed that the Japs would thrust them all out,
especially by their fiendish skill in counterfeiting
trade marks.
That spotted saint, Tremayne Dunstan,
formerly rev. in charge of “ Mr. James’s ” flock at
the Pitt-street Congregational Church (Sydney),
uttered the pious wish the other day that when
the Japs arrived in Australia their first bomb
would fall on The Bulletin office. Well, after
last week, Tremayne Dunstan ought to know
pretty well what bombs feel like. He had the
amazing front to apply to the Bankruptcy Court
for a discharge, and his bomb arrived through the
Registrar, who told Dunstan that he had pursued
a course of fraud and deceit, that he had borrowed
money from various people by false pretences,
that he had taken down at least two women, that
he had in his parson’s clothes obtained the confi-
dence of people and then cruelly and dishonestly
abused that confidence, that he had absconded
under a false name, and generally that his conduct
had been immoral and dishonorable; and having
given the pious cheat and fraud this comprehen-
sive certificate, he threw out his application for
discharge with great enthusiasm. And this, ye
gods, is the sort, of man they pay £750 a year to
to show them the Way to Heaven !
Some Hebrew fragments :
The London Jewish world is treated in Cassell’s Maga-
zine for July. Chief Rabbi Adler is 65. He and Cardinal
Manning were ardent friends, and he was also a great
friend of the late Bishop Creighton, of London. The late
Lord Bute bequeathed £lO,OOO to Dr. Adler, for a Jewish
studentship at St. Andrew’s University. He raised £BOOO
for a charity at one Hebrew banquet. Said L. Rothschild,
“ You are a prince of beggars.” Adler : “No, I beg off
princes.” Said Cardinal Manning to him, “ When will I
see you eat a slice of ham ? ” Adler : “At your Emin-
ence’s wedding.” Particularly good, ‘but possibly the
Rabbi would not have said it if he had reflected a moment.
The next intellectual Hebrew in London is Dr. Caster,
Chief Rabbi, or, more correctly, Haham(wise man), of the
Spanish and Portuguese congregations. Haham is a title
1800 years old. A peculiarity is that he declines to
take fees as Chief Rabbi, putting rich and poor on abso-
lutely the same footing. Such names as Montefiore,
Sassoon, Ricardo, Disraeli, Lopes, are on the books of his
congregation. Sir Samuel Montagu, proper name
Montagu Samuel, is next to the Rothschildren in finance,
and noted for stubborn orthodoxy. During his copious
travels this has often reduced him to a meal of bread and
onions. He never had a day’s illness, and said to Glad-
stone, “It is through following the Mosaic law.” On
the other hand, Claude G. Montefiore, M.A., from Balliol,
Oxford, is the most brilliant of the Jewish Ultra-Re-
formers. The Berkeley Synagogue is their headquarters.
Mocatta is the greatest of all charitable Jews, in propor-
tion to wealth, giving away from £15,000 to £20,000 a
year. Yet he cannot afford to keep a carriage. Mrs.
Bischoffsheim combines the reputations of being the most
influential lady charitable worker and the best bridge
player in England.
“ Barlamb ” : If Attorney-General Isaacs
is the son of an imported Polish Jew it goes with-
out saying that his talent is essentially tricky.
Paderewski, and Cinquevalli (born Brodzky), the
father of modern jugglery, are typical wonder-
working Poles. Benno Scherelc delights in card
tricks and queer feats of piano playing. The
mental and physical alertness of the Poles is quite
Japanesque, and when the genius of the Jew is
thrown in you get a very strong combination.
But if I were a Judge I would have a particular
distrust in Isaac Isaacs’ most impressive legal
arguments. He must have a tricky mind, a
juggling style of reasoning, although, to be sure,
he takes himself with a seriousness that casts a
gloom around him.

“ E.” from Melbourne : A word for Bent.
I like Tommy. There’s some human nature about
him. His instinctive democracy is almost Whit-
manesque, and it’s a pleasing sight outside Parlia-
ment House to see the Premier arguing with a
ragged newspaper kid and telling him “I’m as
good a man as you, anyway.” Also Tommy
doesn’t deal in Stinking Fish. He believes strenu-
ously in his country —“ the finest place on the face
of God’s earth ’’—where everything is better than
anything anywhere else, including Tommy him-
self. And bis contempt for convention, and his
readiness to oblige with “ health and song ” on all
possible or impossible occasions, form a strong con-
trast to the icicles of Irvine and the ponderous
platitudes of the average politician. Then, though
Tommy’s theories are rotten or rather mixed, his
practice is good. He’s quite a sound Socialist,
and is doing Socialistic work on unsentimental but
plain business lines. He has also hung up a sur-
plus, so on the whole we’re getting along all right
with Tommy, and we wouldn’t change him for
Joey anyway.

“Loco.” : Be following the flight of bees
(B. 13/7/’O5). Was watching two or three bees
hovering around a blossom, when my view was
blocked by a swagmau who asked snappishly if I
hadn’t seen a bee before. I told him I had seen a
few, and, with visions of wild honey in my mind,
offered him great wealth (Gd.) if he could locate
their nest. He agreed. Presently one of the
insects rose, and swaggie got on to his back wheel
from the jump. I followed swaggie. That insect
kept strictly to the left side of the road, which was
covered with stumps and heaps of stones ; but
whether he was falling or rising the tattered pur-
suer kept his eyes glued to the small speck. After
three-quarters of a mile of this the speck slowed
up near a manure heap on the side of the road ;
then, in disgust, steered across to a patch of tim-
ber. We fell over the two intervening fences—
and watched the insect calmly settle on a dead
sheep. That swagman gazed hard and thought-
fully at the blowfly for fifty-nine seconds ; then
said —“You cow 1”

“Quidam”: “8.5.” (8.27/7/’O5) expresses
sorrow that boomerang-throwing is not adopted as
a branch of athletics. Let me tell him that a lot
of sorrow would be expressed by other people if it
were. He says it would soon arouse interest in
large schools. It would—especially among doctors
and undertakers. It is easy enough to propel a
boomerang forward, but it takes a very experi-
enced person to predict where it isn’t going to hit
on the back-flash. When you observe a nice sharp
crescent-shaped piece of hardwood coming back at
you viciously from the clouds, whirling round like
a spinning-jinny, and you don’t feel thoroughly
certain whether it is going to strike you or some-
body else (it’s pretty safe to hit someone), it gives
you a sort of nervous feeling. Once had a Queens-
land friend who reckoned he was pretty skilful
with the weapon, but, on the second exhibition,
its homeward journey was a little too high, and it
passed over us and the various bits of scenery we
were concealed behind, and went through the only
stained-glass window of a little country church.
Squaring up for that sacrilege, and subsequent
thrilling adventures in company with that
bold Queenslander and his boomerang, convinced
me for life that the boomerang and civilised man
must be content to live apart.
“ Jimmy the Ringer ” writes :
Re Rodier and his rabbit remedy: It is now about
13 or 14 years since he advanced his theory of checking:
bunny, and ever since it is doubtful whether either the
man or his scheme was ever considered except with dis-
trust or derision. Pastoral dead-beats have found in both
a gleam of sunshine to illumine the gloomy intervals
between their doleful rnaunderings about what they were
before the rabbit ruined them. Others treading in the
same direction can only be headed off their monotonous
growls about banks, the Government or the Labor party
by having Rodier drawn across the trail, while the theory
has become the standard joke of pastoral fledglings who
were busily engaged in the destruction of the knees of
their pants at marbles, while Rodier was out after bunny.
That there is a humorous side to the question is unde-
niable, but to thoroughly appreciate it and get the full
flavor of the joke, one has to go into the career of the
man himself.
Rodier went to the Cobar district about the poorest of
the unfortunates who thought there was a fortune in
squatting in this fly-blown Eldorado. He took up a 10-
mile block. He probably' couldn’t afford more. And it is
one of the worst in a district where few, if-any, can come
under the heading- of good. He was there when the
rabbits came. He was there when they were at their
worst (in probabl.v the worst rabbit-country in Australia
to handle). And he is there now. Of his contemporaries
in that long-back time, they who are left can be counted
on the fingers. And, further, Rodier is one of the few—
the very, very few— who are sound financially.
This is where the humorous part comes in. Could
there be anything more ludicrous than to see “ the
lunatic” Rodier, who has held his run despite both
rabbits and drought, being held up to ridicule by sapient
f dlures battling round for a billet “on their uppers,” or
to consider Rodier as the stock joke of nominal owners
who cannot spend thirty shillings on a fowlhouse without
permission ? I appeal to any'one. Could there be any-
thing funnier ?

“ K.K.” : I am about exhausted with dog
lies. Isn’t the noble bow-wow a much over rated
animal ? A man near me owned a dog which, so
he said, could ‘‘do anything but talk.” We were
out riding. My friend had to dismount, and when
he re-mounted he left his whip lying on the ground.
“ Tiger ’ll fetch it,” he said. We rode off, leaving
Tiger to fetch it. He didn’t catch up to us, and
next morning my friend came round to tell me he
hadn’t fetched it (or himself) yet. So we saddled
up and went to look for him. We found the faith-
ful excrescence lying down steadfastly regarding
the whip, which was lying where it had dropped.
The whip weighed three ounces, and the dog was
big enough to carry' lcwt.
“Davrich”: The medical knowledge of
the Australian aborigines is limited. They have
only three cures of
any standing, viz. :
“Budgelum y,”
“Mindewarum-
bing” and “Yacat-
barbylo.” The first
is an ointment made
of ’possum, emu and
kangaroo fat, g,nd
is used for cuts,
sores, bruises, etc.
The ungUent is
never used except
in win t e r—t h e
blades disdaining
(or else being too
lazy) to use any-
thing in summer.
The second prepara-
tion is a composition
of boiled eucalyptus
leaves and native
honey, and is drunk
in cases of severe
cold, dysentery,
etc.; and is some-
times drunk all
round, when any
bad sickness is about
the camp. The third
is made from the
mistletoe which
grows on gum, box
or coolibali trees,
mixed with a plant which is known to the blacks
as “ waranbungly,” and which whites designate as
“sourgrass” or “acid plant.” This last is used
for ordinary, uncategorical ailments. I don’t
think there are any others.
“Nym”: A lot of unnecessary fuss is
being thrown about the premises over the seeming
extinction of the native cat in certain quarters, as
if the vandal would ever be missed ! One healthy
“ native ” of the spotted variety, and only as big
as a ferret, will work more cussed damage in the
dead poultry line in an unsuspecting community,
than four foxes. Saw a strange sight last June,
which proves that the vermin isn’t extinct in the
dead timber areas along the Big Trickle. Old Bill
the Stutterer pulled up his fishing lines for the
season, sticking the rods upright in the bank,
with the hook on each string baited. He intended
gathering up the tackle that evening, but lingered
with the talent at Moo San’s shanty cursing the
Government. Bill left in the gloaming, but came
back hurriedly, looking scared. He stuttered, so
we couldn’t wait to decipher his sentences, but
followed him down stream, duly armed to meet
the Tantanoola tiger that sometimes lurks in the
bottles out back. Our targets proved to be five
native cats, each of which had taken the defunct
frog on a hook, and had been caught. Bill
wears a vest made of those cats’ skins.

“ Werrimee ” : Writing of national postage
stamps in the S.M. Herald, Ernest Favenc
reviews a variety of subjects in the animal, bird,
floral and historical kingdoms that offer themselves
as suitable emblems. Three of them, the kangaroo,
emu and black swan, are already in use. Among
the others he recommends (the emblem must
be something common to all the States, and exclu-
sively Australian) are the ’possum, dingo, cocka-
too, brolga, ibis, jabiru, bustard, water hen, peli-
can and eaglehawk ; with the wild duck (name not
specified), spoonbill, tortoise and flying fox as
adjuncts. All these are good, pictorially con-
sidered ; but no mention is made of the kooka-
burra, one of the most notable and picturesque of
Australian birds. He is widely distributed,
species of him being found in all the States,
including Tasmania ; and with a snake in his huge
bill he would look exquisite on a national stamp.
Certainly he would be better than cockatoos, the
heads of which resemble too closely the giant
parrots of other countries ; or the swan and the
pelican, which are not peculiarly Australian;
or the eaglehawk, duck, water hen, crane,
or tortoise, which have representatives too
closely allied elsewhere. The koala, perched
on a limb with a young one on its back,
would be another striking emblem. The animal
is now becoming extinct in many parts, which
adds to its claim for recognition. The flying
squirrel is more picturesque than the ’possum,
but there are squirrels further afield ; and the
’possum and our wild dog must be rejected on that
point also ; while the scrub turkey would not be
unique without its mound. The broad-headed
wombat, on the other hand, is distinctly Aus-
tralian, and is widely distributed. The platypus
is omitted by Mr. Favenc as not being common to
all the States. It is, or was, found in Tasmania,
Victoria, N.S.W. and Queensland (am not sure
about S.A.), surely sufficient to include it as the
most unique representative of Australia. No
other part of the world can produce anything ap-
proaching the famous ornithorhynchus paradoxus
—and very soon Australia won’t have anything
more than the memory of it. All considered, our
best emblematical subjects in the bird and animal
line are the kookaburra, platypus, and kangaroo ;
with the emu and the flying-fox as adjuncts. The
emu is not distinct enough (on a stamp) from the
ostrich and the American rhea for premiership.
But it is rumored that the frantically picturesque
and original Federal authorities are just going to
put Edward Vll.’a countenance on the stamp and
be done with it.

“ Nulla ” : Re that noble bird, the eagle. I
want to know where the nobility comes in. He’ll
kill lambs, and, providing they don’t see him
coming and beat him to the nearest fence, hares
and such small game, but he doesn't do his
slaughtering in a more dignified manner than his
little brother, the sparrow-hawk. Certainly, he
has a very impressive bill, but a usurer has that,
and no one ever alludes to Ikey as a noble animal.
In my opinion, the eagle is an over-rated, big cur,
that can be moved on by “ willy wagtail,” and
kept moving by an argumentative “ soldier bird,”
and until I hear of him assaulting a land-agent or
somebody of that sort, I will continue to class him
as a ruffianly harrier of defenceless things half his
size, with as much right to put on “dog” as the
unspeakable crow.
“ The Prooshan ” : When I was a boy
30 years ago, peach trees would flourish
with very little help from the orchardist.
The curly aphis and other aphides, too-
numerous to mention, didn’t woiiy the life
of the peach out in those days. Now, it’s-
a mighty hard matter to rear a peach at all.
Out of 14 young trees planted a few years
ago by a neighbor only two survived ; the
rest fell victims to blight. Well, I saw five
splendid healthy trees yesterday in an
orchard at Batlow, and asked their owner
how he managed to grow them. “Oh,” said
he, “quite simply. See that stuff growing
round each tree ? ” I then noticed a bunch
of “old man,” a plant well known in the-
country on account of its “ old-man
smell. “And what does that do?’ I
asked. “Aphides don’t like it. I never
have any trouble to speak of with my
peaches,” he said. On inquiry I found that
it was his old mother’s idea. I have found
that old mothers are pretty rich in good
practical ideas.
* * * *
A party of five horsemen camped on
the Long Plain, about four miles from the
Glory Hole (Yarrangobilly Caves), about 20
years ago. Having heard that horses had
a way of mysteriously disappearing there-
abouts and finding lodgings in the famous
Stable Creek in Lobbs’ Hole, from whence
no traveller returned, save at the direction
of him who drove him to market, the party
decided to bell a fidgetty old mare (they
only had one bell), confident she would make
such a lively exit if disturbed by a thief that
the camp would be raised. After turning in,
the wind rose, but still they heard the bell at
intervals, and one man kept the fire going
and listened to the tinkle until relieved in
turn by a mate. “They’re all right; I heard
old Flirt’s bell just now,” said the last watch.
When morning broke they went round be-
hind the scrub, and found the bell swinging
on a twig that swayed gently in the wind,
and that was all they did find. Every hoof
was gone.

The following is an extract from a
Nature study lesson given in a Victorian State
School to fourth and fifth class youngsters :
The Vicia Paba (broad bean): In a transverse section
of the square stem is seen (1) a medullary cavity, (2) pith
of the medulla, (3) vasculous bundles separated by medul-
lary rays, (4) large cells containing chlorophyll (cortex),
(5) epidermal cells pierced by stomata. Cambium con-
sists of thin walls filled with protoplasm. The xylem is
formed of spiral cells, etc. The bud has three layers of
primary meristem, but they originate outside the cortex
(exogenous).
The lesson goes on and finishes with such terms as
periblem, dermatogen, plerome, etc. The poor,
ill-fed, ill-clothed, and over-worked cocky-children
cannot work mentally the price of a dozen eggs at
l|d. each, and when they have this sort of thing
thrown at them it leaves them stunned, gaping,
and ignorant.

“ O’Fipp ” : Be music and milk-production
(“ Madcap,” B. 27/7’05)
A German friend of mine, farming’ at Wuk Wuk (Vic.),,
has trained his cows so well that he simply places the
buckets in a line, strikes a few chords on his zither, and
the cows range themselves over the buckets. Peculiarly
thrilling music follows, and the milk is given up without
manipulation. He was puzzled to find why the process
d’id not work with one of the cows, but discovered that
she was deaf. Pitted her with artificial ear-drums, and
she has since been as easily influenced by the music as the
others. Friend reckons he has saved £239 10s. Bd. in
three years in milkers’ wages. I have grasped the idea
with a feverish hand, and my patent bail, with phono-
graph attachment, will soon be on the market.
PEACE AND WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Men round the pub. door talk of tricks,
Of Reid and Cent, and polities ;
A “ two-up ” school is up the lane
And filled with thoughts of sordid gain.
Sma’l children n the gutter pla,\—
In childhood’s muddy, cheerful w.t.y ;
And women on their front gates lean,
Unmindful of.a house to clean ;
Peace has stretched out her shapely han I
And rested it upon this land.
But hark 1 a rending tears the air,
The place is suddenly astir.
Men from the pub. with flushed cheeks run,
As at the sound of foeman’s gun ;
The two-up school, with frantic shout,
Rush from the lane and join the rout ;
The children from the gutter shove,
Neglectful of the dirt they love ;
The front-gate gossips join the throng
And with it madly tear along.
The cause of all these trampling feet
Is two dogs fighting up the street.
VIC.
C. C. HUTRLSH.

TOWING THE KID. —(Seen down by the Murrumbidgee.)
“ Valak ” : Re tracking bees from water
(B. 3/8/’O5). I have taken scores of wild bees’
nests, and always found them from water, but I
did not try to put salt on their tails, or anything
of that kind, and then chase them home. By
getting the sun so that it reflects from the bee’s
wings, you can line a bee for 15 or 20 yards after
he leaves the water, and although he circles a bit
when rising, after he lights out for home in
•earnest, he travels as straight as a solicitor to
his fee. We would watch a bee from one hole
and get his direction, then go a little higher or
lower till we found another going in a similar
direction. The intersection of those two lines
would be pretty close to the nest, and we merely
had to examine the trees in that vicinity. I have
. got as much as 3cwt. of honey out of a nest, but
lest Mr. Carruthers may think this a solution of
the unemployed difficulty, I hasten to add I have
also got considerably less. One of my most pain-
;ful memories is that of finding a nest in a huge
white-box tree about Oft. through at the butt.
From the dark appearance of the opening
we opined the'nest was old ; but as the bees were
•entering about 40ft. up, too high to cut them out,
the tree had to come down. About a hatful of
honey is a cheerful reward after hours of slogging
with an axe. There is a lot of difference in bees.
Some come out to look for trouble with the
first chop of the axe, and they are not happy till
they find it either. I have often had to light a
fire to windward of the tree before I could even
•cut the tree down. On the other hand, some bees
start straight in to eat the honey. I have heard
of a nest on the side of a hill, which smashed up
-as it fell, and the honey ran down the hill a
•quarter of a mile into the creek. I can’t corro-
borate this, but perhaps some other Aboriginaliar
•can. As far as chasing a bee to its nest is con-
•cerned, some time ago I saw where bees and
.homing pigeons were let loose together and the
bees were home first. It seems to me that the
man who can keep up with a bee is wasting his
time chasing them. Chasing records ought to pay
better.
Some more old reminiscences by a man
who was recently contradicted :
Apropos “ Thurla and Burrowang ” (B. 17/S ’O5). It.
I). Meagher's father did not succeed “ Constable” Suther-
land at Cowra. The officer at Benni Creek, four miles
from Cowra—sometimes confounded with Oorowa—was
Sergeant Andrew Sutherland. The story told was that,
-on May 1, 187:1, two men rode up to the hut of a farmer
named Horan and made an enquiry. While there Sergeant
Sutherland appeared and called one of the men, with
whom he held some conversation. The second man had
/gone into Horan's hut and the sergeant called him out.
It was alleged at the inquest that the sergeant said that
if the man did not come out he would shoot. The boot
was on the other leg, for as Sutherland demounted the
■other shot him dead. The news was brought to Cowra,
An arrest was made but nothing came of it, and Suther-
land is still unavenged.
Sergeant Denis M’Cartv succeeded Sutherland, and
about the year 1885 M’Carty asked the writer if he
would like to see the murderer of Sutherland, be-
cause, if so, the man would be on view at the police court
that morning. The police believed that the two men
were myths, and the police also well knew who shot the
-sergeant, but could not sheet the charge home. The man
.In custody had not been in Cowra from the morning that
he had brought the news of Sutherland’s death until that
week. He had been knocking about the pubs for five
-days when M’Carty warned him to, leave before midnight.
As he did not, he was arrested as a vagrant, although he
had a fixed residence in the neighborhood, and, after
•evidence, was sentenced to two months in gaol. The
magistrate expressed wonder at the audacity of the man
in putting in an appearance at Cowra “ after what had
happened ” On leaving the dock, the accused said that
it served him right ; he had been warned not to come in
contact with M’Carty. As M'Carty stood on the court-
house steps, a flash bushie warned him that the
accused man might have a shot at him when he came out.
The sergeant grimly replied* that he would have only one
chance.
The widow Sutherland married an hotelkeeper at Mur-
rumburrah —one Marina, an Italian, who had fought under
•Garibaldi, and who was one of half-a-dozen left standing
in his regiment after an engagement.
“Dave H.R.”, in anger and contempt :
“Gip” (B. 10/8/’O5) is sarcastic anent some bushmen
who, he alleges, visited the city to transact business, and,
instead of accomplishing their object, huddled themselves
up in a room on the 111th storey of a pub. (or something
like that) away from the roar of the trams and ’buses,
and the city’s din, and the ’arbor, and the corybantic
scurry. When “Gip’s” bushmen came out they tied
handkerchiefs on to verandah-posts, and hit horses in
intermaxillary spots to see how old they were, and gazed
about them like a stone idol debating whether he’d com-
mit hari-kari or cable the ’Erald and Yellowgrapii to say
that the Japanese had just won a great victory, and that
the eneniv was terribly routed, and that they were going
to win another victory to-morrow, and their chief poet
was going to make an epic poem in praise of an alliance
with England. Now, I knew some city men once
who came to the bush. “Gip” may have been one
of them—they looked like dictionaries and exaggera-
tion, and tiiev meandered in a blimey fashion up
to a shed where shearing was in process. The shearers
asked them to come in and have something to eat ; and
thev came, and they were very welcome. (It is nice to have
something to eat when you’re hungry, even if there are no
serviettes, and the quadruped is in a tin dish.) After tea,
the “pedestrians” who had no blankets were provided
with wool sheets and packs to make a bed, and every-
one went to sleep or to the pub. seven miles away. One
of those who went to the pub. happened to return about
3 a.in. in a state of undoubted sobriety because the pub.
had been burnt down before he arrived there. He caught
the city visitors quietly absconding in the ebonit.v.
Thinking (seeing they were out so early) that they
were poets and not responsible for their actions,
or else had some nefarious designs against the stars he
hailed them. They seemed as if they had to catch a train,
but three other men coming up at the moment they were
caught. In their swags was found a miscellaneous '
collection of razors and watches, sleeve links and linen,
and culinary circumstances: and they fled for the city with
despatch, pursued by various cattle-dogs, and boots and
accelerations and objurations.
* » * *
Australia still imports oak and hickory for
spokes of wheels and the like, under the notion
that these timbers are the best on earth for resisting
heavy strain ; but recent experiments at Mel-
bourne University proved Victorian blue gum far
and away superior. A blue gum slab 30in. long,
sin. wide, and 3in. thick, stood a breaking strain of
27,05011). Another piece that had been part of a
skid for five years, and had then been made into a
spoke, withstood a crushing strain of 85731 b. per
s'], in., while samples of oak and hickory only
g:ive a resistance of 38671 b. and 40651 b. per sq. in.
respectively. Truly the importing madness is a
very insane lunacy indeed.
“Yarrum”: Not generally known that
the octopus, or starfish, as fishermen generally
call it, makes a bed of shell for itself handy to the
rock it lives under. It gathers together with its
tentacles all the bright shells within a radius of
yards, and camps upon them until the approach
of danger, when it skedaddles under the adjacent
rock. I can now, by looking out for the nest,
alwavs tell just where there is a “starry” to be
had for bait.
* * * *
“C.H.S.”: Re length of Australian
snakes. In Nov. 1890, at Top Camp, Ashburton
River, W.A., I saw the two halves of a snake
which, when placed end on, measured 28 feet from
head to tail. This snake was killed close to a
waterhole in a narrow rocky gorge by three diggers,
who dispatched it with stones from the high
banks. Before giving its final wriggle it vomited
a half grown-wallaby, which, no doubt, was the
reason why it didn’t quit the spot in haste when
the trouble began. New proverb: “A gorged
snake never wins the Melbourne Cup.”
* » * *
“Drifter”: Visited a poultry farm near
Fairfield t’other day, and found the boss trying
to palm off a young hen as a rooster on to a
Chinaman who wanted only “ plumpee young
loositer.” The Chow, much amused at his per-
sistence, showed his yellow fangs in a cunning
Asiatic grin, and, pointing a skinny finger at the
hen, cried: “ Whaffor ! That fella no loositer.
Him littee girl! ”
‘‘Jimmie Pannikin”: Recently met
Peter, the Paroo poet, at Tilpa, on the Upper
Darling. In return for a small kindness, Peter
presented me with two wild turkeys and three
pomes. I helped —largely helped—to eat the
turkeys, but the poems being still uneaten, I
send them along
“ 0.P.” on the amateur farmer
The rich man says to the poor man, “ Go on the land,
young man.” But the poor young man is frightened he
can’t come oil again, if he does that. His clothes wear
out, and if he comes hack to step on to the pavement they
see he has no socks on, which is no good ; and he has to
walk backwards so the patch on him won’t show. Some
young men who don’t know what farming is like goon
the land when the rich man says so, then they are sorry
afterwards. When he doesn’t know what to do, the
young man picks the land to go on where there is no rain.
He gets more land for his money there, and then he is
flash. He gives the place a big name, then he starts to
build his house and make the fences, and do the farming
with his white shirt on. Then the neighbors come, and
say it is a fine day, and they don’t think it will rain much,
for the wind is in the wrong quarter. And the poor chap
tries to be sociable. He has read about farming in the
weekly paper, and he wipes his face with a handkerchief,
and he talks about ensilage and drainage, and the other
things which are out of place. Then the neighbors think
he has no sense, because he is not a farmer, and they
poke fun at him, and tell him to put the cabbage in the
wrong way up. And he ends just like the other farmer,
for the Government gives him seed wheat, and he eats it
all for supper ; and when he has eaten it he gets off the
land sudden-like.
9 Hr * *
The proposed society which is to take
under its care the fauna of N.S.W. may do a lot
of good if it goes the right way to work. The
young Australian is developing a passion for the
promiscuous destruction of bush life, and the
average old Australian is too busy to whack sense
into him. A society which would fix up close
seasons, set apart reserves, and move the law
along when, wanton destruction took place would
be worth encouraging.
THE LONELINESS OF A GREAT CITY.
JOK, THE COOK: “Jest bin to the city to see the
wonders. Walked four days. Thousands.
Not a face I knowed. Never was so lonely
in all me life. Dashed glad to get back ’ ere.”
He was a bush liar and then a parson.
“ When I was a youth,” he purred, “ I lived on a
station. Dad’s hobby was polled cattle. He kept
no others. One day, while rounding up the cows
on old Billyam, the stock-horse, I heard a thud of
hoofs and a muffled roar. Looking round, I
beheld'an immense black bull coming at me. And
its mouth was open. I jammed tbe spurs into
Billyam, and we went. He followed for four
miles, and then it was bull or railway gate. I
took the gate. As our hind legs cleared it, I felt
the bull’s breath on my ear. We had to chop
down that gate to get out the bull, his horns were
wedged an inch into the wood-work.”
At Wanaaring
Folk are daring—
They swim the wide and waste Paroo,
And risk a nip
From fierce bunyip.
Which sometimes bites men through and through.
At Port Pooncarie
The folk are chary
Of asking you to have a drink ;
But for old times’ sake,
Many nips they’ll take,
If you will only find the chink.
At Yantabulla,
When men are fulla
Than ticks have any right to be,
Mates just pump them out—
Thus a drinking bout
Lasts weeks and weeks, believe you me.

If the parsons who barrack for unre-
stricted admission of colored coons to Australia
got their wish, they would probably, after a time,
imitate the action of American sky-pilots. In
various U.S. churches the “black trash” are
ordered to keep away from -the communion-rail
until the whites receive the sacrament. Mostly,
however, the black trash are ordered to keep out
of the white trash's church altogether.
The Paraffin Paint Co. have in Malthoid
Roofing a product that promises to be a world-
beater. There are forty portable roofings manu-
factured in America, and inexperienced judges
take it for granted that they are all more or less
the same, and that one is practically as good
as another. This notion is hopelessly wrong.
Malthoid is a special combination of a pure
mineral and wool felt, and no other roofing uses
the same combination to begin witii, and no other
roofing has had twenty years of unrivalled success
to finish with. There is only one portable roofing
in the world that can be described as supreme, and
its trade name is Malthoid.*

Goose all over, really ill;
Wheezing, sneezing, shrill and high,

Oblivious to the world’s affairs,
All its sorrows, pleasures, cares,
Useless suffering, why endure?
Take Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*

ANNEXED.
The legalit y of the Australian flag and seal is being questioned in England. The distinctly national Australian emblems of the emu and kangaroo
were annexed, it appears, by the first Lord Rosmead (Sir Hercules Robinson) when he was raised to the peerage. Permission had to be given to him by
the Imperial officials. because the emu and kangaroo were not “on record” to anybody.— Melbourne Age. J
