POLITICAL
POINTS
What is the reason for the dank, mouldy,
silence of that “ people’s watchdog,” Sydney
Truth, in regard to the N.S. W. Lands scandals ?
‘ ‘ Politic us ” : A recent Bulletin para-
graph re the action of Messrs. Bell and Denham
on the Betterment Bill (Queensland) is sadly in
error. Both of those Ministers spoke most
effectively in favor of the measure. The para-
graph did them a grave injustice.
Last Monday Sydney Herald and Tele-
graph both announced that the first train that ran
in Australia was that which left Sydney for Par-
ramatta on 26th September, 1855. A very casual
glance at Coghlan’s statistics would have shown
them that they were both wrong. The first train
in Australia left Melbourne for Port Melbourne
on 13th September, 1854.
Premier Carruthers, of N.S.W., says he
has discovered that in the Don Dorrigo scrub 100
acres will keep a family, and that there is room at
Dorrigo for 2500 additional families. He promises
that the scrub shall be thrown open within the
. next 12 months. But some wild persons have
been talking of bringing out millions of immi-
; grants and mentioning the Dorrigo scrub as if it
would hold them all.
Sydney Telegraph, rallying the Ashfield
electors to return another supporter for the N.S.
Willis Reform Government, published lists to
show that, in a House of 90, Carruthers has 43
followers, the Labor party 25, and the Waddell
party 20. Thus, on its own showing, Carruthers
holds office with a minority following. As to the
deadly wickedness of doing that, see Telegraph
files when Watson was Australian Prime Minister.
J. H. Garruthers twitted the Labor party
recently for having supported the Government
which was in office when the chief Lands scandals
occurred. Whereupon M‘Gowen dug out of
Hansard this quotation from Carruthers, violent-
ly approving of Crick’s land administration :
So far as the Minister’s administration was concerned,
he might say at once that he approved of it more heartily
than he approved of the administration of any other
member of the Government.— Nov. 5, 190%.
Some months ago two “ gaps in the law ”
were pointed out to the N.S. W. Attorney-General.
One (re Slattery) where property was endangered
owing to want of protection from trustees ; and
the other where the lives of infants were at the
mercy of the amateur midwife, whose unsupported
certificate was sufficient warrant for the burial of
the unwelcome baby. Steps to rectify the first
matter have been taken with great energy, but the
other missing link is still missing.
PLEASE REMEMBER AUSTRALIA.
Is the High Commissioner to supersede the six Agents-
General, or is he simply to compete with them?— Age.
Ought to have a tine effect if we can gel a good
stand for them to borrow at —say some
Piccadilly corner.
A discussion in N.S.W. Parliament on
having another public holiday to be called “Em-
pire Day” was appropriately enough “counted
out.” The idea is to knock off work for a day,
and spend some borrowed money on crackers and
flags, and talk about “the Empire.” Members
yawned arid' went out to see about a dog, and the
quorum bells rang, and the dog was reckoned of
more importance than the Empire; and that
ended the matter.
Sydney Telegraph, in its blind rage
against Australian manufacturers, can’t even add
two and two correctly. The other day it had to
put in this painful explanation :
In our leading article yesterday on the subject of the
duty on harvesters it was stated that the duty on the re-
valuation arbitrarily fixed by Mr. William Lyne would
amount to £l6 ss. The doubled duty of 25 per cent,
should, of course, have been calculated on the invoiced
value of £32, and not on the fictitious value of £65 fixed
by the Minister for Customs. The duty on each machine
at £32 would thus be £B.
One Bill in Queensland Premier Morgan’s
programme for present State session (re the
better housing of shearers and sugar workers) is
sadly needed. In some of the plantations up
North the white man has to hang out amongst
surroundings that the Pacific Islanders’ inspector
would promptly object to if the occupants were
Kanakas, but the white man, having no inspector,
must put up with them or be sacked. And when
he is sacked be reads in the Piebald Press that
“ White labor on the sugar plantations is utterly
unreliable.”
Queensland is finding
out every day now what a
fool it was when it hoaxed
itself for years into believ-
ing that it had a “ business-
man Government,” and that
the bottom would drop out
of things if it made a
change. For years the old
crowd tried, or professed to
try, to get the ocean-going
mail steamers to go up to
Brisbane and carry away
produce; and it failed.
Yet what that crowd tried
for years to do and couldn’t,
Premier Morgan has
brought off in a few months.
And he did it without offer-
ing anything special in the
way of a bribe to the Orient
people ; he was simply able
to show them that Queens-
land was living in a sober,
respectable, hard-working
way, and wasn’t existing on
loans and fireworks, and
that people were producing
things to export instead of
loafing on borrowed mil-
lions. The Queensland but-
ter business will probably
be the first one to look up
as a consequence of the new
arrangement, and it has al-
ready looked up something
considerable.
Dawes Point, to the
west of Sydney harbor, or-
dered to be set apart for a
public park, was once the
only place where imported
packages or parcels could be
landed. Imports landed at
any other wharf were seized
by the authorities and
generally confiscated. There
were not many people then
to look after smugglers, and Governor Macquarie
simplified the matter by making all importers
unload at one wharf.
Joe Carruthers has given N.S.W. due
notice that when he leaves office as Premier “wild
horses will not drag him back.” Which is a very-
safe statement indeed. There are at least 16
cases in N.S.W. alone of a defeated Premier coming
back to office, but not one of these Premiers was
dragged back by wild horses. They just returned
in the usual way. And it was the same in all the
other States. Nor is there any record of a once-
defeated Premier coming back to office drawn by
wild oxen, or by the wild ass of Persia, or travel-
ling behind any other remarkable animal. More
than that, there is probably not one man in Syd-
ney who, when he leaves his office at night, would
be dragged back there in the morning by wild
horses. The tramcar is much safer.
It is announced that all the beautiful
scenic effects on the Tasmanian stamps, and the
swan on the stamps of Westralia, are
to be done away with by the Aus-
tralian Government, and this coun-
try reduced to the old dead level,
with the countenance of Edward
VII dabbed on the corner of its
envelope. No reason is assigned
for the change. Of course, if it
does any good to Edward VII to
have his face on our stamps, he
should certainly have his face
there. The man who would refuse
to aid and comfort a fellow crea-
ture, struggling on a throne with
a large family, by putting his head
on a stamp, is unworthy of the
name of old woman. Or if Ed-
ward VIPs head does any good
to Australia, or if it is a more
wonderful work of Nature than
Mount Wellington, or Jenolan,
or the Barron Falls, then again
the new idea is all right. But
if none of these things are so,
then the proposed change reveals the person re-
sponsible for it as one whose artistic sense isn’t
equal to the job of white-washing a board fence.
The coldest remark in Deakin’s reply to
Carruthers re the Federal capital question was
the last one
The papers which were laid on the table of the N.S.W.
Parliament do not disclose any “compact” agreed to
prior to the adoption of the Constitution by “ the people
of the Mother State.”
And Joseph, who had been spread-eagling a great
deal about the alleged “compact,” probably felt
like the slavey in “What Happened to Jones,”
when she put out her hand for a dollar and got a
large lump of ice.
G. H. Heid, babbling at Sydney Ex-
change, puffed himself out larger than usual,
and remarked that “ until we got rid of
the prejudice against worthy immigrants
we would not have removed the biggest
stumbling-block to our progress.” Well, who is
it that has this “prejudice against worthy immi-
grants”—the party that wants to provide them
with work when they land, or Beid and Joe
Cook, who want, the work of making things done
in some foreign country? The Foreign-trade
party is the party of the open-door and the empty
cupboard ; and it’s the state of the cupboard and
not of the door that the immigrant troubles about.
It is difficult to know what Deakin could
do that would satisfy the Freetrade push. Car-
ruthers proposed to carry the points of dispute
about the Federal capital to the High Court for
settlement, and Deakin replied cordially that if
Carruthers would devise some legal process by
which these points could be brought before the
court he was quite agreeable to the arrangement.
Then Wilks yapped about the “ ignominious
manner in which the Prime Minister had gone on
his knees to the N.S.W. Government and asked it
to discover a legal difficulty which could be carried
to the High Court.” Ordinary courtesy is wasted
on the Wilkses of this world. When Deakin re-
fuses to do what N.S.W. wants, it is a Victorian
conspiracy, or an outrage, or something, and when
he cordially agrees with N.S.W., he is cursed by
worms like Wilks for not disagreeing.
WANTED , A HOUSE-BREAKER.
Quite a small panic is created in Melbourne by the audacity
and persistence of the local housebreakers, but the Legislative
Council still stands intact.
Last week N.S.W. Assembly put its foot
down for once, and refused to waste the time of
its members sitting on a Select Committee, to
worry out the petty alleged grievances of two
asylum attendants, who were sacked on a charge
of incompetence somewhere in tne dark ages. Pity
is that the Assembly doesn’t keep in that frame
of mind. For years now, it has been almost im-
possible to fire the most useless or intoxicated
barrow-man off a Government job without Parlia-
ment calling the parties before it and hearing the
case personally at great length.
George Reid on immigration :
If it were only remembered that every individual who
landed at Circular Quay, Sydney, brought work to 50
different trades we might enter on a new era of prosperity.
So we might, if these 50 trades were here. But if,
under the accursed system of low tariff, few local
industries and huge imports, the 50 trades to which
the immigrant who arrives at Circular Quay sup-
plies work are mostly in England or Germany or
America, while N.S.W. has to borrow money to
support the immigrant on public works or relief
jobs, where is the new era of prosperity ?
Premier Carruthers’ statement in N.S.W.
Assembly that Pilcher, K.C., is not retained to
generally cross-examine witnesses before the
Lands Commission, but only to cross-examine when
required, is worse than anything John Haynes has
said against the Government. The Premier and
other Ministers are liable to be witnesses, but Pil-
cher is not given a general cross-examining job—
which would be sure to include them. He only
cross-examines when he is told, and leaves it alone
when he isn’t told. Now when is he most likely
not to be told ?
Mr. Abraham Hoffnung, chairman of
London Board of Directors of Tasmanian G. M.
Co., Beaconsfield (Tas.), of the Mt. Boppy G.M.
Co. (N.S.W.), and of Hoffnung and Co., Sydney,
to a Launceston Daily Telegeaph reporter,
concerning the alleged “driving of capital out
of the country” by Australian Socialistic legis-
lation
It is not so. For a person to make a statement like
that is to stamp him as not conversant with the facts. The
English people have money to invest, and they are
partial to Australian mining, and Labor legislation in
Australia has not, nor will not, deter them from putting
capital into what they consider a good thing.
A lawyer writes :
A landlord and tenant law of a single clause will go
nearer to giving all-round satisfaction than any number of
complicated provisions such as N.S.W. Parliament is now
worrying over. The law should simply give the landlord
power to eject at a week’s notice —in other words, to
stop supplying house just as a butcher can stop supply-
ing meat. If he Wants arrears of rent, let him sue, as the
grocer or baker does, and if he obtains a judgment let
him sell up, but only as a grocer or baker may do. Wor-
thy landlords at present suffer at the hands of tricky
tenants, who will neither pay nor leave, and worthy
tenants are unmercifully harried by harpy landlords, who
use their power to distrain in circumstances in which a
court would refuse its sanction.
“The Great Fight ” that George Reid used
to babble about before Deakin stood from under,
still struggles on in a feeble, spasmodic fashion.
For some reason Reid imagines he is popular with
the women voters of this great country, and still
dreams of coming; back to power on the crest of a
vast female uprising, at the next election. In the
meantime, with Reid out of office and the N.S.W.
State Reform Government slowly becoming not fit
to be s en dead with, the anti-Socialists are in
parlous plight. Their latest idea is to canvas for
signatures to a petition against Socialism. Whom
the petition is to be addressed to nobody seems to
know.
The! N.S.W. “Reform” Government pro-
mises to put a sum on the Estimates to recoup
Richards, M.L.A., and John Haynes their ex-
penses over Haynes’ petition to Parliament
against Richards’ election. Haynes maintained
that grave irregularities were allowed in the
election, and that his defeat was due thereto.
These charges were all held disproved by the
Elections and Qualifications Committee, and the
quasi-Government support given to the claim of
the unsuccessful petitioner is hard to understand.
Richards might possibly claim a reimbursement,
but Haynes’ claim seems a strange one for a noble
and honest and economical Ministry to recognise.
If N.S.W. Minister for 'Lands Ashton is
wise, he will suggest to the secretary of the Lands-
Commission to bring up a few transactions (there
must be some available, surely) in which Mr.
Ashton acted as land agent—just to let the public
see what fees he got, and what was the nature of
the services. The Bulletin believes in Ashton’s
honesty. But it is known that he acted as a land
agent, and the public has a right to see what sort
of agent he was.
The fossilised majority in the Vic. House
of Landlords pointed in all seriousness to the
defeat of Lord Mayor M’Eacharn by the
democratic Maloney as a final proof of Woman’s-
Unfitness for the Suffrage. The Federal Govern-
ment gave woman a chance to return the Chief
Magistrate of the city of Melbourne to his former
seat in Parliament—and she preferred Maloney.
Therefore she is as incompetent to vote properly
as the men who likewise preferred Maloney.
Nothing could be clearer. Everybody who doesn’t
vote against Maloney, the Landlords believe, is
unfit to have a vote. In fact the only use of a vot-
is to keep out Maloney.
Queensland wants more revenue. Educa-
tion Minister Barlow says he could do with-
£loo,ooo instead of £6OOO for new school buildings
and repairs. Some of the schools are tumbling
down. An old hospital morgue does duty at
Toowoomba as a class room. Ipswich crams 100-
pupils into a low-roofed, badly-lighted, ill-ven-
tilated skillion 50 years old. Bundaberg Board
resigned the other day because it could not get aid
to build a play-shed. “I have had that morgue-
on my back for a year and a half, ” lamented Bar-
low, the other day, and he owns pretty broad
shoulders. Other Departments show signs of
inefficiency through depleted expenditure. A
progressive land-tax would set many things right
in Bananaland.
The Prohibition fight in Otago is going to
be hard and bitter this election, and the cold-tea.
section is already straining every sinew till it
cracks. Port Chalmers—about to sell beer again
for two or three months—will certainly vote-
no-license ; and so, almost as surely, will Bruce —
where also, for the moment, a man may get a
drink in a straightforward way. Behind the Pro-
hibition strength in the Otago back-country there
is, as chief cause, neither the love of sobriety nor
the fear of God, but merely an inordinate idolatry
o’ the siller. If there were any possibility of
stopping church collections, the Prohibition dis-
tricts of Otago would vote No--Collection by
tremendous majorities. But democracy is not yet
so far advanced.
Premier Bent’s Bill to Regulate Public
Meetings in Victoria has been tabled. It briefly
provides that “any person who, in or near any
hall, room, etc., in which a public meeting is being
held, behaves in a riotous, indecent-, threatening,
or insulting manner, or uses language to that,
effect, shall be liable to a fine of £5 or one month.”
Also, “ any person present at such meeting may
take proceedings against any other person in
respect of such offence.” It seems comprehensive
enough, but, if it passes in that shape, the next
time Bent tells an elector publicly that he “isn’t,
fit to carry guts to a bear,” he will probably find
himself served with half-a-dozen summonses next,
morning. And then the local honorarium will-
have the pain of discharging him with a caution.
UP-TO-DATE.
“ Now, this Bar abbas was a land agent! ”
Victoria’s hopeless House of Rich Land-
lords last week threw out the Women’s Suffrage-
Bill by 16 votes to 11—this making the eighth re-
jection of the measure. The same old weariful
platitudes and absurdities were trotted out, Ena-
bling actually advancing it as a reason for refusing
woman the suffrage that, though the women pro-
posed to be enfranchised would, as far as the
Council was concerned, only be ratepayers, they
would almost certainly vote the Labor ticket.
Evidently Enabling, Miller, Payne, Manifold and
the rest have a better opinion of their womenfolk’s
voting capabilities than the women have of the
Eats’ legislative powers. But it is a fool of a legis-
lative Landlord who openly advertises himself as so
politically corrupt as to refuse a person the suffrage
solely because he thinks that person will not vote
for him.

With flattering vows allure,
Remember that your truest friend
Is Woods’ Gee at Peppeemint Cube.
When frosty stars gleam overhead,
And earth’s in wintry moods,
The cold attacks in throat and head,
Then you’ll remember Woods.*

U.S.A. Consul Orlando Baker, back in
Sydney, savs there is no unemployed difficulty in
America, as there is “plenty of work in the
country.” Plenty of work is the unfailing solu-
tion of the difficulty wherever it exists, and the
best way of getting plenty of work is for a country
to do its own work at home.

Even John Bull—Jew-driven, careless,
Freetrade John Bull—felt a shiver along his
Spine the other day when figures were published
showing the growing increase in the number of
aliens qualifying as British pilots. In 1888 they
were 35 in number : now they number 200—200
foreigners holding the keys to British ports.
Griffith and Allan, Patent Attorneys of
Sydney, have opened a branch of their business in
Melbourne—(Broken Hill Chambers, Queen-st.),
under tie management of Mr. H. P. Keogh, of
Melbourne.*
Bent’s State brickworks have got so far
that a vote of £9OOO for them, in the Surplus
Revenue Bill, has passed the Assembly, and will
catch the eves of the Fat Landlords as soon as
they condescend to meet. In the Assembly Bent
weakened somewhat, and said that, instead of the
State working the plant, he would let the works
to a co-operative co., which would supply the
Government with bricks at 255. per 1000, instead
of the combine’s 40s. Nothing was said about
terms as to rent, tenure, &c., or as to supplying
the public ; in fact, Bent said as little as he could,
and what he did say wasn’t as good as what he
might have said. Yet he had a lot of encourage-
ment to go straight ahead ; for, in spite of all the
criticism in the House, and the Argus’ ravings
outside, when Watt, the renegade Democrat,
forced a division on the item, he could only get
one member out of 51 to vote with him—Tory
Boyd, whose own coal mines are heavily subsidised
by the State.
A bit of recent S. Australian history.
C. W. Chandler, proprietor of Adelaide Free
Lance, was convicted of libel, and sentenced to
six months’ imprisonment, principally because he
had written down Australian interference in the
Boer war. In Adelaide gaol he conversed with a
number of prisoners on the criminal side, and was
Btruck by the fact that many of them were unable
to explain why they had committed the offences
that had gaoled them. All they knew was that
they were drunk on “old red” wine, and woke
up in the cells. When Chandler was released
he started Adelaide Truth, and one night de-
liberately intoxicated himself on the pinky
stuff. He related his awful experiences in
his paper, and then started to fight the
poisonous adulterations in a weekly column
chronicling the doings of the “Pinky” Club.
Government interference followed, there was an
analysis, and the result was the, at least partial,
destruction of the trade in that kind of wine which
is sold at about 3d. a bucket, and has three sheols
in each bucket. But a robust Adulteration Pre-
vention Act has not yet arrived, and the man who
drugged himself with “old red ” keeps pegging
away.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
It was hardly to be expected that the Aus. ra-
lian Rugger team, fresh from a four days stormy voyage
across the Tasman Sea, would have a chance of bra..ng
the tough material that Wellington, Wairarapa, and
Horowhenui Unions would put against it. Still such a
fall as 23 to 7 is a little more than was expected. Even
the Fall in Genesis doesn’t mention that the apple
knocked out Adam by 16 points. It wa,s a beautiful day
from a Wellingtonian point of view. Fine, the mud not
over the boot-tops, and only a gale-and-a-half raging
straight from one goal to the other. Australia had the
wind behind it the first spell, and when the half-tnne
whistle blew was only one point behind—7 to 8. Irie
teams changed ends, but the wind didn’t. The struggling
Australians disappeared in the mud, and never scored,
while their opponents ran up 15 points.
A turquoise sky, and a breeze that exhilarated
like wine, brought out huge crowds to watch the first-
grade Rugby competition matches in Sydney on hatui-
dav. Play all round was exciting, and sometimes unduly
rough The only change among the leaders was that of
Universi tv and Western Suburbs, the latter, which had
shared third place with Newtown, falling away to fifth,
while the ’Varsity moved up to fourth place. South Syd-
ney which is in the fore-front, fell on Sy-dney District and
smote it hard in the second half. During the first spell
Sydney, although losing, kept its tail up, and reached
the whistle 3 to 8. The first few minutes of the second
spell saw it playing a strenuous game, and then the
team apparently went to pieces, and South Sydney- added
no less than 20 points to the score, while Sydney failed to
break the ice. The final result was 28 to 3. Western
Suburbs played excellent football, but the Glebe crowd
trampled through by sheer weight, and won a fast,
brilliant game by 14 to 7. In its match against the
plucky Eastern Suburbs team University had all the luck,
but, although the score was 10 to 5, it was a well-con-
tested game, with nothing but the figures one-sided about
it. North Sydney went down before Newtown in a match
that was far from brilliant by 3 to 10.
* * * *
Under “Soccer ” rules Pyrmont is still in front
with 20 points. Rozelle has, through its defeat by Bal-
main on Saturday, slipped back to share second place
with Glebe—lB points each. Far behind Ultimo toils
third with 13 points, and Balmain has 12. A fast and
unusually rough game between Ultimo and Glebe on
Saturday ended in a draw with one goal each. Balmain’s
unexpected defeat of Rozelle was by three to two, which,
as the philosophic ape said in his own language when he
pulled the trigger with his foot and blew his whiskers off,
was a pretty- close shave. The most serious discrepancy
was that between Pyrmont and South Sydney, South
going under by four
Saturday saw the finish of the first round of
Victorian League football, leaving the clubs much in the
same position as before. Collingwood leads with 12 vic-
tories in 14 games, Fitzroy follows with 10J, then Carlton
with 9, Essendon with 7, South with 6J—the rest don’t
count. The three ensuing Saturdays the odd numbers
play each other, and the even ditto; then the highest
four battle in semi-finals and final. ’Wood went down to
Essendon on Saturday by 53 points to 66. South’s battle
with ’Roy was the most exciting match of the season. It
started a trifle roughly, but a plentiful allowance of penalty
kicks cooled the ardor of too-robust players. Thereafter
South’s heavier men piled up a threequarter-time score of
7 goals 7 behinds to ’Roy’s 4 goals 7 behinds. The wind was
across the ground, and ’Roy’s chances looked hopeless.
But its lighter and better-trained men outlasted their
opponents” and, struggling round South’s posts, they
potted three goals in succession in five minutes, and the
game was a tie. Then they notched a behind, but within
cooee of the bell ringing, South’s rover “ marked ” 20 yards
straight in front of ’Roy’s goal. He “ placed ” carefully
and kicked—just going the wrong side of the post
by a hand’s breadth—and the game ended in the only
draw of the season. That bad goal shot means that,
instead of playing ’Roy, Geelong and St. Kilda in the
second round, South will now have to tackle ’Wood,
Carlton, and Melbourne, all a shade tougher customers.
It is up to somebpdy to explain why South, making a
draw with the second best league team, should have gone
down by 39 points to 41 to St. Kilda, the worst of the lot,
two Saturdays previously. Last week the same St. Kilda
18 could only score a miserable 23 against Carlton’s 73, so
it could hardly have been the Saint’s good play that caused
the debacle. The 10 Association clubs still meander
gradually towards their semi-finals, which seem certain to
be fought among North, West, Williamstown and Rich-,
mond, though the reversals of form that pervade things:
just now may upset anyone’s calculations.
The public school football premiership of Vic-
toria was decided on St. Kilda ground last Saturday by
the match between Melbourne Grammar School and Wes-
ley College. Former won by 10 goals 6 behinds to 3 goals
7 behinds. The play was irregular and of poor class ; any!
of the better State school teams, though lighter and
smaller, would have beaten Wesley, and perhaps its rival as
well. The match was chiefly interesting by reason of an
unseemly riot at half-time and another at the conclusion.
No sooner had the players retired to the dressing-room
than mobs of well-dressed young larrikins mostly
scholars of the two institutions —swarmed on to,
the playing ground and raised pandemonium. Their
chief object seemed to be to capture the colors'
and caps of their opponents, and incidentally kick
the shins and blacken the eyes of all and sundry belonging,
to the rival school. Eight constables, who had been put
on in anticipation of ,a ruction, were hopelessly out-
numbered, and the argument just had to die away of
itself. Little real damage was done, but the behavior of
these supposedly educated, cultivated lads contrasts
most unfavorably with that of the allegedly mannerless
little nippers who pick up a “godless education” at the
State schools.

Sydney Metropolitan Rugby Union very wisely
passed a long sentence on that peppery player who;
recently plugged the referee! If the offender is still as
keen as ever on Rugby.at the end of ten years, he will be
at liberty to indulge his fancy. The people who control,
football have stronger backs than the crowd that imagines
it rules the cyclists. ,

Football in S.A. is booming again, and specta-
tors now and again get more’than their money’s worth in.
the way of wrestling bouts and scraps. The Adelaide!
Oval match between N. Adelaide and W. Torrens resem-
bled a Bear and Monkev skirmish more than a football
match. In the last quarter the grand crisis began, when
two very angry players got together. Before the umpire
could collect wind enough to breathe through his whistle
most of the field joiried in, and the arena was a mass of
struggling, blaspheming footballer, policeman, and small
b °y. *
* » * *
Collingwood football team has done something
to show Victorian superiority to S.A. under Australian
rules, by visiting Adelaide and knocking out (by a com-
fortable margin) two of the leading - local clubs. And the
week between the two games included a visit to Broken
Hill, where the hard grounds often knock a visiting
team’s feet right out. Several of the ’Woods were disabled
by the time they got back to Adelaide, but the remainder
were equal to ail requirements. #
The averages of the five test matches show one
player on each side at the head of both batting and
1 owling lists—Jackson for England and Duff for Australia.
The results work out disastrously for the Australians, the
one gleam of distinction being that Duff’s 146 was the
highest individual score in the series, beating Jackson’s
144, though the English skipper was not out. Trumper
held the unexpected position of tenth on the batting list,
and was actually- beaten by Cotter. Another unexpected
result was that Noble only held seventh place on the
batting list, and was last of all on the bowling record.
* * * *
When one reads the English cricket news—this
match interrupted by rain, that match abandoned on
account of the rain, and the other one “resulting in a
draw” —it seems absurd to maintain cricket as the
national game. Originally a one-day-, or one-afternoon,
game, it has developed into a three days battle, subject
to the eccentric control of the weather. No wonder that
English professional players are mostly addicted to beer.
Playing in the sunshine, or wasting time whilst the rain
falls, is very dry work.

Australia’s champicn amateur sculler, Tasmanian
Tom Bourke, last week received a cabled offer of £lOO
exes, from the New Westnvr.ster (Canada)- Exposition
people as an inducement to compete in the world’s ama-
teur sculling event there in September. Bourke declined
the offer on account of an intention to try for the
Diamond Sculls at Henley (England) next year. As
Bourke rather easily “downed” ex-champion “Joe”
Daly, formerly reckoned our speediest “’rigger’’-shifter,
experts hope that Bui! will be lamenting the loss of two
valued pieces of wood in the near future. Australia
wants something to make up for those “ ashes.”
* * *
The feasting of champion “Jim” Stanbury is
rapidly- restoring the circumference which it took seven
months to reduce to racing trim, and such swelling up of
a champion athlete doesn’t point to continued success.
Stated that Stanbury is likely to put his winnings into a
pub. and retire from the pastime altogether—bestowing
the just-won title upon a “ young unknown ” oarsman.
At any rate, there’s a big likelihood of Towns challenging
Stanbury shortly for the title, the recently defeated
champion being satisfied that he spoilt his chances by
under-rating Stanbury and failing to take his training
seriously.
* * * *
Extraordinary public interest was manifested in
the Wallace-Continental road race for cycles from
Bathurst to Sy-dhey. At every town along the road
excited crowds cheered the competitors on, while 12,000
people roared congratulations to the winners at Ashfield.
Of the 75 acceptors, 61 started. Victoria, Queensland
and Maoriland were represented, the last by the mighty
6ft. R. Arnst, who, with Larcombe of Goulburn, was on
scratch. While he lasted, Arnst put’Up a tremendous
effort, hurling himself at full speed along a road with
which he was not acquainted, till turning a corner near
Bowenfels, he went clean over a stump or a log-, described
two complete somersaults in the air, and injured his head
so as to require many stitches. This wrecked any chance
he might have had of winning- the race. The limit men
were Robinson of Cooma and A. M. Sommerville. For
over 80 miles Sommerville led, unpaced and alone.
Then M‘Mali on and Carton headed him. Carton
punctured, and M‘Muhon rushed on alone, with
Sommerville close behind. Somewhere between
Penrith and Ashfield, Allsop, of Baulkham Hills,
took second place, and Sommerville, exhausted by his .
long, unpaced ride, fell back third. In this order they
finished. M‘Mahpn’s time was 7hr. 38|min., while the
fastest rider, G. H. Horder, got over it in 7hr. 23min.
The distance is given as 127 miles. When C. S. Orr put
up the record for the Bathurst-Sydney race in March,
1905, he took lOhr. 48min., and is credited with having
done 145 miles. Ashfield is five miles from Sydney, so
presumably Orr and the present crowd of wheelmen tra-
velled by different roads. The race is to be an annual
fixture.
* * * *
J. M‘Mahon, winner of the Wallace-Continental
road race, Bathurst bo Ashfield, was born at the romanti-
cally-named LochinVar, in the Maitland district of N.S.W.,
is 25 years of age, and has been a cyclist only two y-ears.
He is something of a wonder, for when he appeared at the
Summer Nights Amusements’ November carnival he raced
on a banked track for the first time, and took £l2B in
prize money. This was his first big road race.
* * * *
Allsop, the cyclist who ran second in the
Bathurst-Sydney road race, had hard luck. The week
previous he had got over the course in 7hr. 2min., but he
developed~stomach trouble the day before the race, and it
was against medical advice that he started at all. For the
first 50 miles he was in a bad fix. Then he began to recover,
and in the last stage came with an almighty rush, which,
had the race been a little longer, might have brought,him
in first.
* * *
This year the great Dunlop motor reliability
test will be held from Melbourne to Sydney, from
November 14 to 18. The test is to be an annual affair,
and will, unless a new Pharaoh arises, be usually run off
in November. The course is divided into five stages ;on
each a competitor may earn 500 marks, with an additional
500 for a non-stop over hilly- country, including the Razor-
back mountain between Goulburn and Sydney. On the
infamous Razorback the grade runs up to 1 in 6, which is
the sort of thing to break the heart of any motor not in
the very pink of condition. Injuries to ty-res will not
count as delay-s in a non-stop run, the object being to
find out the most suitable type of engine and car for the
Australian roads. Tyres will be tested by- the journey- as
a whole. The Dunlop Co. is going to extraordinary
trouble to make the test a success. Dangerous places in
the road will be indicated by red discs placed about 25yd.
away. Although the test is not a race, a fair touring
speed has to be maintained, but anything like scorching
will lose marks. Already the hum of preparation is loud
in the land, and quite a number of cars (which ought to
be made locally) are being imported to try and carry off
the valuable Dunlop Cup, and the gold medals, and other
prizes. There is a section for motor-cycles as there wes
last year, when only three machines got over the journey.
Experiments have proved that, properly treated, a
motor-cy-cle can go anywhere a motor-car can, but it has
to be handled with more tact.
* * * *
The new Dunlop inter-club shield, which is
understood to be an exquisite creation, was, contrary-to
expectations, won, on Saturday’s final, by- Enfield Club,
which defeated last year’s conqueror, Balmain, without
much difficulty-. Betts and Maxwell, of Enfield, were
first and second. Power and Rodrick, of Balmain, filled
third and fourth places, with Nicholson and Gannon, of
Enfield, fifth and sixth, and Lawson and Tay-lor, of the
suburb of rotting Wharves, at the tail-end. Each of the
four Enfielders wins a gold medal, and the club calls itself
premier arid holds the Dunlop shield for the next 12
months. If Enfield wins again next year, the shield
becomes the property of the club. Only Heaven and the
accountant, who is most likely a Scotchman, know what
it costs the big Dunlop Co. every year for prizes, medals,
shields, goblets and miscellaneous silverware. Hardly a
week passes but one reads that someone has won the
Dunlop whatyoumaycallit, or thingamy, or whatisit.
Entries for the Gortlburn-Sydney road race, which eventu-
ates September 16th, closed yesterday (Wednesday); At
the present time, Dunlop people are going over the course
per motor, making all the necessary arrangements for the
smooth working of the contest. Six wheelers are coming
from Maoriland, and a couple of Westralians, and quite
a squad of Victorians are expected.
* * * *
George Farley, the Australian cycling cham-
pion, has been holding his end of the log up fairly well :n
Europe at time of writing. He had been pitted against
Rutt and Kramer—the latter that Yankee flier of whom
it is said Major Taylor was afraid—in three heats. In the
first heat Kramer beat him by only a length ; in the
second, Farley was within half a wheel of the American,
but in the third Kramer won with comparative ease,
although the distance between them was said to be only
a length. Farley is riding his Australian-made machine,
and it has attracted much attention in both Paris and
Antwerp, where the people understood, from the yells of
the Stinking Fish party, that Australians were a black
race who could make nothing for themselves. When
Farley arrived in Paris he was met by the man who had
engaged bi> i, and, after an ice-cream, the Frenchman
conducted him to a hash-tower. Farley- finds that the
food is greasy. The only difference between that of
Antwerp, in the north, and Genoa, in the south, is that
it is more greasy in the north and not quite so greasy at
the south.
• * * *
The international motor-cycle race, known as
“ the Little Gordon Bennett Race - ',” was run recently over
a 33-mile circuit in the Dourdon district (France). The
total distance was 165 miles, and as machines were not to
exceed 1101 b. weight there were thrills about the busi-
ness. France,'Austria, England and Germany competed,
being represented by three riders each. The winner
turned up in V. Wondrich, an Austrian, who got over the
distance in 3hr. smin. 15sec., or an average of 53 miles
and a fraction per hour. The light machines staggered
at the terrific speed, and the riders expected them to fly
to pieces any moment. The whole of the headlong jour-
ney two Frenchmen hung to the Austrian, till one of their
machines broke down, when the breathless Gaul promptly
mounted another and flew off in pursuit. After 50 miles
he was again close up to Wondrich, but could not pass
him. At the end he was disqualified for changing his
wheel. The English team was outed by tyre and other
troubles, and only- the winner and the two Frenchmen
saw out the whole journey-. One Englishman, Collier,
got round four times, and then blew up, or something.
The winning machine was a twin-cylinder, belt-driven
Laurin-Clement.

will push his jigger in Australia this season. He arrives
in September, so that none of the crumbs shall be lost
owing to negligence.
Maoriland test race to select a team for the
Goulburn-Sydney and Warrnamb'ool-Melbourne road-races
produced a world’s record 0f’275 entries. In Canterbury
the peculiar formation of the Plains has stimulated the
use of the bike to an astounding extent, and there are
probably more bikes in proportion to population between
Waipara and Parawa rivers than anywhere else on earth.

There was some remarkable pistol shooting by
Jack Hamilton at Queensland Rifle Association’s meeting-
last week. Firing with the ..regulation revolver at 20yd.,
Hamilton scored the possible 42, thus beating the
score of 41 by Woodland, of 11.M.5. Euryalus. Hamilton
is a fine specimen of the native-born Aust: lian. ■ As
well as being a dead shot, he is the handiest of aan with
his fists, and was cock of the walk at more ..an one gold
rush. For many years he was in ; Queensland Legislature,
and even that experience does not seem to have put his eye
out. Yet at the time when Hamilton helped to make the
laws, many a politician couldn’t aim straight enough to
hit the refreshment-room door with his own body-.
“J. A.C.” : Be the poker machine swindle. The
wily exploiter, not content with; having ridiculously long
odds in his favor, makes extra sure by abstracting certain
cards, making fours and straights next to impossible. Not
long ago the committee of a sporting club decided to have
a poker machine on its bar, and a man came to fix it up.
Said he : “How many cards do you want taken out?”
“What do you mean? We want none out! Put them
all in !” “Ah, well,” replied the manipulator, more in
sorrow than in anger, “ you’re very foolish !” I know a
man who was making'from 30s. to £2 a day with one of
those machines when the poker craze was more furious
than it is now, and if his thrippence-in-the-slot marauder
were to be examined the result would be a revelation.

M.L. sprinter Machine Gun’s win of Tramway
Handicap (6fur.) at Tatts. Randwick meeting (Saturday)
amply endorsed his Maoriland form. Carrying top load
(10.6), he came from second last (When two furlongs had
been traversed) and landed the prize and bets at 5 to 2
against from Isos, with Picturesque third, in the very fast
time of 1.14. Subsequent statement by starter that
Machine Gun had such a bad time of it in the middle
of the field at the start that Hewitt would have
been unseated but for his great skill, renders the win
still more impressive. Nag’s weight in coming- Epsoin
Handicap (one mile) is 9.12. Machine Gun’s ability to
stay a mile is doubted by some, who have probably for-
gotten that he won Great Easter Handicap (7fur.) well at
Canterbury (M.L.) Jockey Club’s last autumn meeting
with 10.4 up.
-* * * *
Sydney Noses are still at outs with local sub-
urban racing clubs, and though they (the Noses) profess
to be as firm as ever, there are signs of knee-bending-.
Advent of a “ mediator ” and a “proposal” were hailed
with joy, and the proposal was eagerly closed with, but
it subsequently transpired that neither mediator nor pro-
posal had been properly authorised by the other side.
Later, 100 Noses, in meeting assembled, discussed
the situation, and talked of testing the legality
of exacting registration fees, &e. Some counselled
acceptance of the “slightest concession, as there
were six new books now, and there would be 20
before long.” One speaker said, with consider-
able feeling, that “it was galling to see traitors
filling their pockets with money while loyal men looked
on,” and dozens of nodding heads evidenced that a sympa-
thetic chord had been struck. Meanwhile, backers of
horses are, to a man, with the clubs, and bets are being
made that if Mo. doesn’t back down quickly he will be
badlv left.
* * * *
Beilis had almost miraculous luck in V.R.O.
Grand National ’Chase. Jockey Regan tells how, when
Equity fell, his horse was right behind, but seeing Equity
spreadeagled on the ground, Beilis, by an extraordinary
muscular effort, swerved in mid-air, and dodged. Then
Regan lost bis balance, and would have fallen but for the
rowel catching and giving- him purchase to recover his
seat. It is also said that Beilis struck Equity as the
latter was rising, and the injury that resulted ended
Equity’s career.

W. P. Crick waved his arms wildly, t’other
day in Pari ament, and stated that when he was in the
Cabinet he shut up the Sydney betting shops. But that
was only for a short time. The “shops” were soon
flourishing more mightily than ever. Still, it showed
that closure was possible if there was any real desire on
the part of the Government to carry out the law.

remember, Tatt’s sweep prizes have gone to policemen

sumably, when they pocketed the prizes, they were under

got away with the first.”

The Tote v. Moses investigation discloses that
had Adelaide Grand National Hurdles gone to History
his backers would have drawn £240 3s. for each £l, and if
he had finished second their share would have been £BO
Is. Backers would have received .£lO5, £122 10s., and

bastes scored. Never has Isaac Noses in his most careless

* * * *
The Jack Thompson-Dan Hiam scrap at Sydney
National Sporting Club should be a world’s record for
frequent and ineffective punching on the jaw. Both men
were big and strong enough to hit an all-out blow, but
apparently it is neither weight nor strength that really
does the damage. The real secret is the timing, of the
delivery, which accounts for a little batsman lifting the
ball farther by a slight tap than a giant may bang it
when he smites like Hercules. In the end, after much
vain hammering and banging, a light chip on the chin
sent Hiam down in the 16th round, and he had only been
on his feet again a few seconds when Thompson’s right,
swung at a venture at long range, just barely reached the
point of the chin again. This time Hiam failed to rise
within the ten seconds. It was a most accidental-look-
ing fight.
* * * t
Albert Murphy, bruiser, of Auckland (M.L.),
came to a sudden finish at the hands of Sydney light-
weight “ Robbo,” at the Gaiety Hall, Saturday night. It
was a brief contest, lasting less than two rounds, and,
considering the limited time at his disposal, Mr. Robbo
succeeded in putting a beautiful head on Mr. Murphy.
For prompt, artistic disfigurement, it would take a lot of
beating. Twice in the first three minutes the floor
bounded up and hit Mr. Murphy, and in the second round
Robbo showered blows, as George Reid showers promises,
on Murphy’s body. Then, when Murphy tried to protect
his equatorial regions, Robbo’s fist caught him on the jaw,
and Auckland’s pet was a dead bird and horizontal.

Consider the great difference between the English
and the Australian “commercial man”! Lord
Russell’s original Secret Commissions Bill was
the outcome, of investigations made by the London
Chambers of Commerce, which assisted him in every
way to put a stop to the tricks and dodges of dis-
honest and unfair traders. Now similar Bills are
before the Australian Parliament and the Vic-
torian Assembly ; but the Australian Chambers of
Commerce, instead of assisting, are doing their
utmost to hamper the Ministers in charge, and to
get the Bills so amended as to be null and void.
And they are meeting with some success in their
defence of fraud. Vic. Minister Mackey has told
an Employers’ Federation deputation that though
“hundreds of commissions which passat present
mMelbourneareillegal, and though Lord Russell’s
Act makes everyone of such transactions criminal,
he had. decided to make the concession they asked
for.” If his Bill passes Vic. Parliament with the
concessions asked for, Mackey might just as well
drop it into the stove at once. It is a horrible
advertisement for Melbourne that a body which
represents a huge proportion of its wealthy busi-
ness men should send forth a deputation to plead
for a continuance of a system which England finds
Vo dirty and dishonest to be further tolerated.
What kind of effect do these deputationists think
this will have on “the investment of foreign capi-
tal,” and such-like tin gods of Toryism ? Who is
going to send foreign capital for investment in a
country where it is so difficult to distinguish
between the business man who robs you with
secret commissions and ?w..e Bit l Sykes who robs
you with a crowbar ?
Jamieson, one of the Tory members of S.A.
Assembly, is a man with an inspiration. He
thinks the pay of Ministers should be reduced to
£5OO a year to diminish the “scramble for office.”
Possibly if it were suggested that the pay of the
chief managers of big banks sho d l be reduced to
£250 a year to diminish the scramble for bank
managerships, Jamieson would dimly perceive
something foolish about the idea. A Premier, if
he is to be a successful Premier, requires a larger
and more varied knowledge than the great lm.uk
potentate who receives £3OOO per annum, and he
should be paid for the knowledge that is expected
of him. Then he should be paid extra on account
of the precariousness of his position. If Upper
Houses were abolished, Lower Houses reduced in
numbers, attendance made compulsory, pay
doubled, and a rigid law passed to prevent
a member’s pay being cadged by constituents,
it would be something like reform. A still
greater reform would be to abolish the State
Legislatures', abolish the Federal Senate, and run
the country with an extensive Local Government
system and a House of Representatives in which
the private member’s salary was £l2OO a year, with
Ministerial salaries to correspond. And there
also it would be necessary to pass ah Act making
it criminal for constituents to cadge, borrow, or
solicit subscriptions from members,’ or for mem-
bers to give subscriptions. A country may pay
for intellect arid not get it—that is an accident.
But :t country that only pays for mediocrity, or
less than mediocrity, almost always gets it.
S. Australia paid £lOOO a year and got Jenkins.
If it had paid £5OO a year it might have got half a
Jenkins,
■ ♦
Captain John D. Switzer died recently at
Brisbane, aged 81. His career included soriie
naval and military adventures, and some unprofit-
able commercial adventures. With a knowledge
of astronomy and mathematics, gained from IJs
uncle who had charge of the Dublin Observatory,
he joined the navy, and took part in suppressing
the slave trade on the West African coast. Later on
he fought in the Kaffir war. He was also in the
coastguard service. Queensland saw him first ii>
1862, when he plunged into the sugar industry t'tk
Bundaberg in a big way, and like many others ;
wasted a fortune. A dabble at the fishing in-
dustry met with similar ill-luck. Then he joined
the Marine Board as compass adjuster, and taught
navigation and mathematics.
“ Colonel ” Hoskins, of the Salvation
Army, in a speech on the gambling curse :
Two boys bet a shilling' that one would amputate more
of his fingers than the other. One lad had cut off two
members, and the other was about to remove his second
when they were stopped.
The Bulletin has doubts if that comes undAr
the head of gambling. Any boy with the gambling
instinct really developed would have hustled
round till he got at least a half-crown bet before
starting to cut off his feet or his head, or any other
important part of himself.
Renegade Joe Cook is of opinion —re tise
Union Label—that “neither trades unionists
nor any other freemen should be coerced to> pur-
chase inferior goods, no matter by what, weans
they are produced.” The “ coercion,” course,
is Cook’s invention ; the Bill simply proposes that
trades union goods may be labelled, and then
they can be bought or let alone. But it is note-
worthy that Cook has now got so far advanced in
his career as a renegade that he assumes that
trades union goods, made by white men working
under fair conditions, must, necessarily be “in-
ferior.”

any good, as my hands were as bad as
when I began. I also tried many reme-
dies, but none of them ever did me one
cent’s worth of good. I was discouraged
and heart-sore. I would feel so bad
mornings, to think I had to go to work
and stand the pain for ten hours, I often
felt like giving up my position. I would
have to wrap every finger tip separately,
so as to try and keep them soft, and then
wear gloves over the rags to keep the
grease from getting on my work. I had

North End; Kalgoorlie, hitherto known as
Devon Consols South Extended, has got to work, and
seems to have a rosy chance of success. Co. is registered
in Perth with a capital of 200,000 shares of ss. each.
Vendors and promoters cut up 150,000 scrip* between
them, as well as £5OOO cash, leaving- £7500 for working
capital. To curtail exes., registered office is on the mine,
and all directors are large shareholders. Mine is in charge
of Harry Fey, who can be relied on to work thing’s con-
scientiously. Co. has a five-head mill • which is to be
doubled, power being g-ot from local Electric Power Co.
.Mine has practical^ 7 been worked out to 80ft., and yielded
good returns over a width of 20ft. in places. W inze below
SOft. is still in good ore, and pay shoot so far proved is
200 ft. » » «
White Feather Main Reefs, Kanowna, is look-
in"-sick. Ore treated during July averaged only Bdwt.,
and month’s crushing showed a loss of £2400. During
June £650 was lost on treating 12dwt. ore. Reefs at
bottom level have petered out to mere stringers of no
value. Further prospecting is being done to the north,
in shallow levels, beyond where the shoot on which co.
was.floated petered out years ago. Results at this point
only yield 1 to 2dwt., which is not hopeful. Looks as if
mine may have to close down before the year is out.
. * * * *
Burbank’s Birthday Gift, Coolgardie, after long
silence, has rejoined producers, but results are unsatis-
factory. In July 1800 tons were treated for 6000 z., with a
loss of £B5O. If' this can’t be improved on, further calls
are certain. Co. is now in 170,000 shares of £l, paid up
to 18s. 6d., leaving a liability of Is. 6d. This amount can
be called up 6d. at a time, at intervals of not less than two
months. It doesn’t seem promising to continue paying
calls into a concern that only treats 7dwt. ore at a dead
loss of Bs. 6d. per ton.
* * * ♦
Westralia Waihi, Davyhurst (W.A.), for the
mompnt looks more hopeful than ever before. The new
reef found by Manager Watt has been more or less proved
by pot holes to range from 15 to 41dwt., and in width
from 24 to 42in. This reef has been traced for a long
distance on the surface, and carries a much longer pay
ishoot than the three or four erratic leaders previously
worked. If this reef can be located at 117 ft. and 282 ft.,
and maintains its values, position of mine will be quite
a tered. July’s.return shows considerable improvement,
and "results in a profit of £450, which vvip-S out loss
incurred in June.
* * » *
Boulder Perseverance, Kalgoorlie, has shown
marked improvement since Manager Klug took it in
hand. Mine is opening up much better than expected,
and is not nearly done with yet. In addition to pushing
on developments for all they are worth, King is to over-
haul treatment plant, and not without reason. Last
year total costs were 465. sd. per toil; of this 255. 3d.
wentfor treatment alone. Nothing whatever was allowed
for depreciation. Klug is to replace the Yankee furnaces
with others of ' Edwards type. Since he took charge
output has gradually increased, last three months’
returns reading 89070 z., 96790 Z., and 10,121-oz.
* * . * *
South Kalgurli, Kalgoorlie, has at last got its
treatment plant going full swing, and result hardly seems
to justify high price of scrip. From treating 5100 tons
of ore worth 365. 3d. there is a profit of 2s. Id. per ton
only, or £550. After directors’ fees and depreciation are
chipped off, the apparent profit will vanish. It is in-
teresting to note that value of ore reserves is given as
10-7dwt., while extraction is B’6dwt., or 80 per cent.
Unless, therefore, costs can be materially reduced, or ex-
traction materially increased, shareholders are not likely
to finger many divs. Mine is well managed by manager
Morgan, and, as- plant is entirely automatic, Costs may
come down when preliminary difficulties are overcome
and men are properly trained to the plant.

The story went round Ballarat like wild fire
last week that Manager Browne, of the Spring Hill Leads,
Creswlck, had resjgned, owing to the stand his board was
taking over the pegging out of some ground for some one
else near the Spring Hill Leads’ lease. As Browne was
tup countrv on account of ill-health, the story didn’t
fit. Still, it’s quite on the cards that Browne’s resigna-
tion goes in. If fib, it will be well for Browne, as it is bad
enough for him to’ lose health through trying to nurse a
derelict without having to sit in the middle of a bear-
garden to do it.
* * *
New Moon, Bendigo, despite past protests
about the need for a vigorous policy, has let criticism
germinate. Co. has big ore reserves and a big battery,
but instead of using all the stamps for its own ore, has
been doing custom work. Now it is going to re-open the
south shaft and connect it with the main shaft for hauling
quartz. From the south shaft the shallow ground is to
be prospected. But why not review the position further
to see’ if it would not be possible to lay out
the mine in such a way as to double the output? To have
only crushed 271,000 tons since 1892 is nothing to be
proud of in days of progressive mining. As the best stone
has come from sopth of the main shaft, the re-opening of
the south shaft gives scope for enterprise in the direction
suggested.

The ‘management of the State coal-mines in
Maoriland is being; probed, mainly on the motion of the
miners, who allege that some of those who are supposed
to be giving State ownership a fair trial are more
Interested in making it out a failure than a success. The
gre mostly with regard to the Seddonville mine ;
fiqe other, the Port Elizabeth, isn’t seriously attacked.
■One matter, which a board is now inquiring into, is
whether the principal agent for the sale of Seddonville
coal isn’t principal owner of the township alongside
another colliery, arid therefore more interested in booming
the latter than the. colliery which he is agent for ; also,
whether -other officials are not deeply connected with
private colliery concerns. If half the charges are true,
or half-true, it looks as though good old Private Enter-
prise had'decided to get in early and kill off a dangerous
rival by taking the job of nursing the rival in its infancy.
But Private Enterprise in the shape of the
middleman dealer has done most to rob the public of the
expected'advantages. When Premier Seddon first talked
State coal he also talked State coal depots. As the latter
haven’t transpired, shipments have gone into the dealers
hands in the old way; and they have made their own
price. , * * *

from which Bull draws good divs. Float has 100,000

holders, the rest presumably to Alfred Clarke and

about £s,ooo to be called up,” as one guileless North
shareholder ecstatically exclaimed. What the deal really

shares for £2500' in a rattling good prospecting show.
Well “ an old horse for a hard road ; ’ and if the British-
Zeehan folk want the ground, as seems not improbable,
Clarke may repeat his Victoria Broken Hill coup. To
have reconstructed the South and North Broken Hill, and
+o finish with this Colonel North pup, is no small record.

Top-drift sluicing accounts for the lower returns
'from the Pioneer tin mine, Bradshaw’s Creek (Ta,s.).
Average of drift sluiced during past half-year was 2"lllb.
ner cubic yard, but this is sure to increase a little as
lower drifts are reached. But the increase will not be
much, as up to date the average of 1,343,400 cubic yards
treated has been only 2-2891 b. stream tin. Work done by
co, is ft standard to some extent of what ought to be done
at the flriseis Extended owing to similarity of plants;
only Piouger has a big gravitation water-supply, which
helps to keep posts down. To get a ton of 'stream tin at
the Pioneer last year ran away with £39 18s. 7d., against
£2B 7s. sd. in 1903-4. Co. is a prosperous Fatman’s venture,
with a son of old Money Miller as chairman, Gibbs, Bright
and Co. as its business head, and Cecil Ryan as its capable
manager. # # „
The prospect: for the Queensland cotton indus-
try look bright. It was recently estimated that a 15001 b.
per acre crop should give the grower £5 per acre, which
appeared favorable against N.S.W.’s average agricultural
return of £2 Bs. per acre. But some yields at Ipswich are
as high as 23001 b. per acre for the upland variety. Other
returns are equally encouraging in other districts. There
appears to be no doubt that if the Australian Government
gives a bonus the industry will be started on a large prac-
tical basis, and then it should be seen that a high pro-
tective duty is established to start cotton manufactures.
There is a huge market in a country that spends about
£2,000,000 a year on imported cotton goods.

Mining at Winnecke’s Depot, nee Arltunga,
Central Australia, is still sick. Papers said the new
Coorong battery would start in May ; but plant, being
old, evidently suffers from lapse of memory, and forgets
to go to work for about five days out of every six. The
reported 37ft. of water in water shaft has disappeared;
the management is now endeavoring to find it, for it is
wanted badly. Government of S.A. has £2lOO in this
little venture, and its Inspector of Mines has gone up to
inspect, and incidentally help to discover aforesaid
missing water.

Bull has been breathing heavily over some of
the results of the Westralian timber combine. In 1902
•Millar’s Karri and Jarrah Co. absorbed a litter of similar
concerns ; and the expenses seem to have been something
extra special. Shareholders in the Jarrah Timber and
Wood-Paving Corporation have been informed during the
last month or two that, after expenses of liquidation are
met and the debentures paid off, 100 of their ordinary
shares carry one share in Millar’s. Holders of 50 shares
get cash—i's. for the lot. They were £1 shares, and in
the timber boom went to a premium.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Van Biene, the ’cellodrama man, has sailed from Perth
for London, after completing a sad 20 weeks tour
of Australasia. Van Biene’s drama, although free from
horrors, was too luridly sensational for the public he
appealed to, but his ’cello playing, as far as it went, was
very effective. Many concert artists who have a better
knowledge of music and a more cultivated technique,
cannot rival Van Biene as producers of heart-stirring
sounds from the big fiddle. The astonishing thing about
his show was its absurdity. He has travelled on “The
Broken Melody” for 20 years or so, and all the time he
might have been acting a similar character, and playing
the same music, in a quite reasonable drama that would
have framed his performance to far greater advantage.
Why is it that dramas written to display the principal
actor or actress as a variety artist are almost invariably
foolish ? Old theatre-goers can recall to mind many cases
of a versatile “ star ” in a ridiculous piece. Joe Emmett,
Minnie Palmer, Carrie Swain, Charles Arnold (as Hans),
and others. Andrew Mack struck something considerably
above the average in “Tom Moore,” which nevertheless
is not bursting with literary merit. By a strange coinci-
dence, poor little “Pete” Hughes, who had acted as
Musgrove’s business manager for the Van Biene Co., died
in Sydney at about the time when the co. broke up. Pete
was the baldest-headed boy in the theatrical business, and
the most universally popular. The world never saw him
out of spirits. He was said to be a quite illiterate man,
and if so he was somewhat of an argument against schol-
astic education, for he contrived to be an entertaining
companion without it.
There are not many chorus girls who can shake an
independent leg in a theatrical manager’s face. But in
Adelaide, the other day, one of them became enraged
when her four-line part was given to another, and
promptly stated her intention of looking for a job under
a manager who could recognise talent when he saw it.
Wm. Parkinson, teacher of singing, whose death at
Brighton (Vic.) is announced, sang in leading operatic
roles in London 30 years ago. For a number of years he
owned the threatre at Preston, Lancashire. His immi-
gration to Australia was occasioned by failing health.
Harry Shine, comedian, has suffered a complete break-
down, and is now in hospital at Adelaide. He had been
the mainstay of Frank M. Clark’s Folly Co. in the steepled
city.
“ Petramos ”: A. G. Spry, the English funny-man
whom Ijenry Rickards is re-introducing to Australia, is
about the only imported star whose business wasn’t done
to death as soon as he left these shores. Nat Clifford can
never sjng “ The Grand Hotel ” with any marked success
in Australian,again, because pretty nearly every amateur
alleged humorist has had ago at it. Spry’s songs were
mostly in the manuscript, and when he wasn’t singing he'
sat on the manuscript, Frank Harwood, a descriptive
singer, brought out-a few months ago by Rickards, made
a hit with a song called “ Looking Glass,” and he also sat
on the manuscript. When in Adelaide he was approached
by a local mimic who wished to include the song in his
repertoire. The amateur says that the descriptive singer
received him warmly, and that his description of Sheol
was the most vivid and accurate thing that any local mimic
ever heard while flying downstairs, and not caring
whether he struck the bottom with his knees or his ear so
long as he got there.
“ Melb.”: Perhaps “ Trelawney of the Wells,” with its
crinolines of ’62, suggested wheeling Time’s bike ever so
much farther back for “ Quality Street,” with its whiffs
of Jane Austen and Fanny Burney. Please observe that
the “ Quality Street ” ladies trot about like Yum Yum at
the other house. A spectacled amphitheatran beside me
remarks to his spectacled lady : “ Barrie is always Senti-
mental Tommy ; alwavs artificial, hardly ever virile.” So
he is. His plays fade off the stage, and yet have a bright
flowering, for there are snaps of genius. Now Barrie and
Pinero in collaboration would motivate. Barrie’s cup-
and-saucer style is not in the same street with “ Caste ”
and “School.” However, lam graciously soothed for
two hours with mental fare like the deliciously thin slices
of bread and butter grandmother used to cut..
An English stage chronicler states that during the run
of a pantomime at Melbourne Royal, “ some years ago,”
the clown (a native of Victoria) took a benefit. He was
drawn across the stage in a triumphal car by “ a magnifi-
cent team” of 24 tom-cats—not the usual pantomime
variety, but genuine tile-walkers, whose protests and loud
cries of “ Ma-ria ” drowned the music of the orchestra.
Goes any old-timer remember the incident, if there ever
was such an incident ? .
A novel form of Australian Industries Exhibition is to
he carried around Victoria, in the first place, by showman
Erskine Scott, the author of the scheme. Manufacturers
are invited to get their works “biographed” in good
going order, and the Exhibition will thus be sheeted home
to the public understanding in a series of living pictures.
Seeing that factories and foundries are serious subjects
for contemplation, a number of “comic” filmshaving
nothing to do with the industrial progress of the Tory-be-
littled country, will be interspersed between the exhibits,
so that even the Stinking Fish party may find entertain-
ment. And the charge for admission is to be Nothing—
the most popular price possible under the circumstances.
This is undoubtedly the best means of advertising local
manufactures that has been suggested. The public, who
pay willingly for a faked-up cinematograph show dealing
with such deplorable matters as the Russo-Jap war or
King Edward’s Coronation mummeries, will get a lot more
benefit from the forthcoming free Exhibition. The Bul-
letin wishes the new Manufacturegraph lots of luck and
no flickering.

“H. Glubb”: The Federal Copyright Bill vastly im-
proves the position of writers, musicians and artists, but
might be made still better by the inclusion of one pro-
vision on the lines of the American copyright law. United
States copyright of a foreign work is obtained only by
production or publication in the U.S. simultaneously with
production or publication in the country of origin, and
the result has been to give a tremendous fillip to native
literature. With a population as small as that of
Australia, it would be impossible that this provision
should be adopted in its entirety, for London managers and
publishers could not be expected, to produce in Australia
every play and book that is now tried <sn the London dog.
But a modification of the American provision might well
be introduced. Another omission that should be rectified
is the lackof any protection to the man who thinks out
stage business. In juggling and acrobatics there are
hundreds of men who can do anything after seeing another
man do it; and, at present, the other man has to suffer by
the depredations of those impostors who see him per-
form, and then get off to another country to live
on the product of his brains. Imitators of Cinquevalli
are simply encumbering the limited area of Europe to-
day. The law should be so framed as to give protection
to the originator of a device whether he is the direct ex-
ponent of it or not. There is, for instance, a New Eng-
land clergyman who cannot waltz without treading on his
partner’s toes, but who has thought out a frenzied revel
which would make the fortune of La Belle Otero or
Madame Darto. A proper copyright law would enable
the meek and lowly shepherd to copyright his idea and let
it out on license to beauteous and agile damsels (more or
less dressed) who are capable of' executing it properly.
Otherwise the existing and proposed law might, as well
grant a musician copyright on his own music only when
tootling it on his own cornet.

“Den”: Have you ever noticed a stereotyped but
highly popular declaration that' runs through most of
the bellowdramas of the day? For instance; in “The
Night Rats of London,” the unhappy villain says to the
callous adventuress : “ Woman, were it not for the likes
of you there would never be such men as I am,” or words
to that effect, and there is wild approbation from the
gallery. Again, in “ The Face in the Fireplace,” says the
miserable adventuress to the saturnine villain —“If such
men as you never existed there \vould never be women
like me,” and again there is prolonged approbation from
the gods. I suppose it is this tendency to pass on the
responsibility that makes us the -borrowing community
we are.
“ Straubs,” says violinist Heermann, “ is an impression-
ist, and sees the music of an orchestra in vivid and
gorgeous colors. The music of an organ sounds gold to
him. Whilst conducting- on one occasion,, I heard Liszt
correct one of the musicians. ‘ That is not right, he
said ; ‘ you should play that —let me see now —yes, play it
a little more blue.’” Some day, maybe, one of these
greac men will compose and play an immortal green work,
with red stripes, and a white border, and a tassel of blue
music hanging from the centre.
Chefalo, the cyclist who loops the enormous sectional
loop in Wirths’ Circus, lately developed a craze to try
to break his neck off horseback. He has been hurled
through space from almost every dangerous kind of
vehicle that cart be used for trick riding, and when the
motor-car that leaps the chasm, and the bike that loops
had palled, he flogged up his jaded appetite for sensation
by getting across George Wirth’s charger, which has been
specially trained to deposit on the grass everybody but
George. He rode the brute in a procession, and was
dressed as Satan, with red cloak, crimson close-fitting
body-clothes, horns on his head, and electric light stream-
ing from his glazed eyes. The horse d.dn’t know what a
spectacle it had on its back till the procession stopped in
front of a big glass window, and then it saw' what it
was carrying-. There was a terrified snort, and a
devil whirled down the street, with cowboy Captain
M‘Leod in full pursuit 1 With' his' lariat. Devil and
horse were finally lassoed at the other end of
Toowoomba, and brought home in triumph. Pos-
sibly it was all a well-rehearsed little advertisement;
but there was one notable exhibition which Chefalo gave
in the circus itself, and it was not rehearsed. They were
paralysing the South Americans at the time, and Chefalo
cells it like this .“ While I whizz found the loop, this
part that I ascend by is shifted back to form the last
part I descend by. You onnerstan’? Yes ! Well, while
I flash round that—just up there, round like the dam fly
on the roof, upside down—l strain mine ears to catch the
click of that section to know the way all clear to come
out by. It take five seconds, de'y say, by the watch, but,
by gar, it seem five blessed years ; y,es, sir. Well, one
night my ears- not hear that click. My heart stop, my
gizzard come up into my neck and choke me. I turn the
top, I come down. I see the faces like the sea—l see that
dam section stop about 18in. away. I cling to the
handles. I wonder if she yump. Wough ! I soar over
it no I not. 'The back wheel strike the edge, clang,
twang, ten thousand roars. I whizz out into de people.
I hit one man across the stomach. I shoot through the
back of the seats. The ground rush up and hit me hard.
All my hopes want to fly away—apart; you onnerstan’. I
close my eyes. ’Oh, I alii dead. lam dead. Dam it, do
you not onnerstan’ lam dead !’ I say to-the people
w ho crowd roun’ to look at me as if I not give them show
enough. I cannot breathe—my winds is knocked-out of
me an’’they jam the bot-tel of brandy between my teeth
as though I am the horse, and pour it into me. . Then all
mv winds go ; lam choked. I try to get up. No hope.
Both collar-bones broken, and half my ribs gone. By gar,
I chuck this soon as I make my fortune. No good risk
your life for fun. No, sir ! ”
The Employers’ Federation of Victoria is
doing its best to make Australian commercialism
stink all over the world. Even after the butter
revelations, which showed many wealthy Victorian
firms to be little better than sneak thieves, it had
the front to ask Minister Mackey to excise from
his Bill the clauses prohibiting secret commissions,
and insert that —
In any prosecution under this Act it shall be a sufficient
defence to show that it is the ordinary customary usage
■of long years’ standing unadopted by reputable firms of
any trade, business, profession or calling.
A “reputable” firm is, of course, a rich butter-
swindler who has gone a long time without being
found out.
Concerning the anger of some S. Aus-
tralian Tories when they didn’t catch a fish
The old wire-pullers tried a neat little trick at the
opening of Parliament; but it didn’t come off.
The S.A. Assembly always holds a private meeting prior
to the formal opening ; it is convened by the Clerk, and
chooses the Speaker. This time the notices said that the
Chairman of Committees, as well as the Speaker, would
be chosen ; that is to say-, before the House met, and
parties had shaken down, and when it wasn’t decided
whether the Laboritbs and the Liberals would come toge-
ther, the ancient Conservative, Alfred Catt, would be
pitchforked into the chair for the 16th consecutive year.
' But the meeting insisted on stopping short after electing
the Speaker ; and now, when the Tory wire-pullers’ little
scheme for making things nice and comfortable for an old
pal has broken down, and a younger man, E. H. Coombe,
a Liberal, has been put in the chair, the same Tory wire-
pullers are making themselves hoarse snorting at the sin-
fulness of the Government in selecting a party man for
the post. The Register, which shrieks through a column
headed “ The Spoils to the Victors,” winds up by warning
the Government that it has “made a formidable foe in
Mr. Catt.” And this is the Register’s perfect chairman
—the man who, not having got a job he wanted, is
“ made a formidable foe ” ! It must be a deadly thing for
a politician to have as clumsy a friend as the Register is.
F. Leveson-Gower’s recent book of recol-
lections tells how closely the late Duke of West-
minster cuddled his money. The Duke was a
multi-millionaire, mainly through his buccaneer
ancestors grabbing 600 acres in London and a few
square miles of the rest of England. One of his
sons, a midshipman, asked for a watch. “Why
do you want one?” said the ducal parent.
Because' all the other middies have one” was
the reply. “In that case,” said the bloated
Westminster, “you do not need one. You can
always ascertain the time from them.”
The Baker bigamy trial, at Sydney, was a
case with some curious features. It turned on the
question whether there was one sister with a mark
on her back who married two men, or two sisters,
each with a mark on her back, who married one
man each. The second sister, unfortunately, didn’t
appear; people who had seen her mostly didn’t
know she was another sister, and were quite sur-
prised that she wasn’t the same sister. Both sisters
seemed to have worn the same clothes at the same
time. No one had apparently seen them together;
the accused sister said she agreed to personate the
other sister, and that she wrote her letters and
drew her money —in fact, it was an unconvincing
tale all through, and the jury brought in a verdict
that the name of the narrative was mud.
.Chas. Nyulasy (father of Dr. Frank
Nyulasy), recently deceased at Melbourne at the
age of 78, was a cavalry officer in Hungary’s
muddled insurrection of 1848-9 —the War of Lost
Opportunities. The oppression of Hungary by
Austria, and the oppression of Transylvania by
Hungary, were vaguely suggestive of the. boss
who oppresses the cashier while the cashier passes
on the aggression to the office-boy. Naturally,
when the cashier rounds on the bloated proprietor,
he may expect the office-boy to throw the ink-
bottle at him from behind. It was the office-boy
throwing the ink-bottle from behind that made
Mr. Nyulasy an exile.
Among the pitiably small questions which
recently took up the time of N.S.W. Assembly
were—
The alleged use of “ scribblers ” instead of slates in the
public schools.
The hours worked by housemaids in hotels.
The amount per acre paid for burning off timber at
Cataract Dam reserve, whether the measurements were
“horizontal” or “ surface,” and whether a certain book
had been lost on the reserve.
There were about two dozen more such problems
in the week’s record. These things may all repre-
sent wrongs or injustices, just as it is an injustice
when Tommy Jones hits Johnny Smith on the
nose in the street, and a wrong when Brown s
dog chases Thomson’s cat into Robinson’s tree.
But the Assembly should remember that it isn’t
paid to be a benevolent old hen in a poke bonnet.
It is paid to legislate, to deal with great questions,
to right big wrongs, and to be the captain on the
bridge—not the religious crank in the forecastle.
The silken bonds have been seriously
dragged again. Colonel Hoad, on some official
occasion in Japan, wore all his pomp and millinery,
ancl a British officer conceived that one of the
millinery was a millinery that was sacred to some
special brand of Britain’s own officers, and not to
be worn by “ mere colonials ’’-with impunity. He
wrote to the War Office about that millinery. The
War Office wrote angrily to Australia about the
said millinery. And the Federal authorities have
replied in a frozen tone, to the general effect that
Australia will wear the aforesaid millinery if it
wants to.
