“ Gr.H.” does a scrap of description
I blew into the Victorian Assembly the other night.
Everybody was asleep, or had just wakened up, or was
just dropping off. M’Leod and Giilott drowsed on the
Government side of the table, and Prendergast blinked
opposite them. Little Chairman of Sleepmittees Craven
rested his chin on the far end of the legislative deck.
Jack Murray was breathing heavily at the disused chan-
deliers, and Peacock was dreaming on the front cross-
stench. Gaunson was on his feet, and had been there for
ages, I believe. He was talking about the Dog Act just
then. The Ministerial door opened and a large bale
of meat fell into the chamber. It was Premier Bent,
the one live individual, bar George Swinburne, on the
Government side of the House. The'only man in Aus-
tralia who lias reduced his State’s Public Debt threw a
rough witticism at Gaunson, and then fell into a seat.
And David, the extinct volcano, wandered on about this
and that, and talked^—and talked—and talked, and
plodded over flowery meads of vocabulary, and ambled by
flowing streams of cussedness, and climbed mountains of
adjectives, and screamed in obliterated caverns of com-
plaint, and threw himself on the ear of the country
generally.
They cail the Professor of Languages at
one Australian University the Professor of Bad
Language. And he nearly deserves it.

could not show them she was undone.

The Parisian dance of “legs in the air” has reached
London at last, being given as a turn at the Palace. Says
the Express : “ When the eig-ht girls ‘dance’ with their
feet in the air, lying prone, the impression is obtained of
a number of huge pink roses with their green stalks rising
from the bloom. Daring as it is, the dance seems free
from all objection, and the deep green stage, dotted with
little heaps of pink lingerie, is a bit of pure artistry.”

About Mummers.
The growth ot the postcard-collecting craze recalls
“ Dundreary ” Sothern’s claim that the original un-
illustrated variety of postcard came into use through his
habit of writing messages on the reverse side of envelopes
after closing them. Sothern had a mania for practical
jokes (Toole hadn’t the mania, but professed to have it
as an advertising dodge), and used to write on the
envelopes such messages as “ I will bring the five giraffes
with me on Saturday,” or “ How are you getting on with
the cockroaches now?” Another phase of Sothern’s
crude, early-Victoriari mirth was to have his envelopes
stamped with information that led people to think the
letters came from the “Refuge for Reformed Atheists,”
the “Smallpox Hospital,” the “Asylum for Confirmed
Virgins,” or the “ Society for the Promotion of Suicide.”
It was a light-hearted sort of humor, suited to an age
which loved to balance a large dish of water on top of a
partially-open door and wait results.
It has been decided, after a long trial in a Paris court,
that a lady engaged as’ a “star” dancer was justified in
refusing to appear in any dress but the traditional flesh-
tights and brief gauze skirts. She had to represent
Salome dancing before Herod, but the contract did not
say anything about costume, and so she recovered
damages from the management, which wanted to insist
on her looking something like what Miss Salome might
have been.
“ Hiphen ”: The most monumental joke I ever
encountered is at the Coliseum, London’s latest and most
ornate music hall. There is a Royal entrance leading to
the Royal Box, so that the Royal Potentate may not soil
his feet on the carpet used by his muddy-footed subjects.
The said Royal entrance is about 25 yards distant from
the aforementioned Royal Box. Lest Edward VII. should
have to walk from the cab or ’bus, or whatever he came
in, a large loose-box, worked by a gorgeous be-medalled
menial, runs on polished brass tram lines along the 25 yards
of passage, and dumps its Royal burden at the Royal Box.
But the only time Edward VII. used it he brought about
sixteen pals along. The Royal tram refused to stagger
under the load, so Edward VII. stepped down and had to
walk the whole yards, by himself, on foot. The menial
who, by failing to shift the load, had lost his one chance
of shoving a king, tore his hair in the passage, and asked
that death might come and abbreviate his misery.
“O’Fipp”: The Australian “serio” or “serio-comic
artiste” of to-day knows more about singing than her
mother, the serio'of 10 or 20 years ago, and is just as good
a dancer. The old-timer’s turn drove many a good man
to thoughts of suicide-; the modern one merely drives
him to the bar till her turn is over. There was_ once a
serio whose songs did not contain the phrase “ jolly old
pals.” She is now reduced to selling peanuts. Of course,
I would not insult such artists as Peggy Pryde and Marie
Lloyd by classing them as serios. Seriously, why don’t
some of the Australian girls go in for something like
Peggy Pryde’s branch of business, instead of the eternal
“rowdy-dowdy boys” and “silly jay” type of song,
varied only by ditties where “ coon ” always rhymes with
“ moon ”?" Also, why don’t they give an occasional Aus-
tralian song—not the imitation of cockney doggrel some-
times locally written, but original Australian stuff? Of
course, songs by local writers and composers would cost
them more than imported rubbish at the start, but a
capable woman with the courage to be different from the
ruck would surely make up in increased salary more than
the cost of her songs.
“ Snell ” : Consider again the smallness of the Austra-
lian song-birds in light opera. Dolly Castles, Aggie
Thorne, and Vinia Deloitte, Williamson’s three latest
acquisitions, are all tiny, like Flo Graupner, Vi Variey
and Carrie Moore. But the concert-singers bulk ever so
much larger. Evidently there is an impression that lead,
ing ladies in light opera should be light and little.
Miss Celia Ghiloni, the massively handsome young- lady
who endeavors to look like the Gilbertian dame of faded
charms, spent her earliest years at Williamstown, a quaint
seaside suburb of Melbourne, where ancient mariners,
looking and speaking like W. W. Jacobs’ characters, dot
the foreground of the seascape. Miss Ghiloni, as her (u;i-
assumed) stage-name implies, is of Italian parentage. She
sang ballads with vaudeville shows before migrating to
the comic opera stage.
George Edwardes has assured a Pall Mall interviewer
that the London Gaiety is “ the most moral theatre in
the world. I doubt whether any place of business is con- <
ducted more strictly. No one is allowed in without a
special pass. Suppose you had some business to transact,
there would be no difficulty about your admission. But
you would not see any of the ladies. They are kept severely
apart from the men. Ladies dress on one side of the
house—men on the other.” “Like a harem,” said the
ingenuous interviewer, and Edwardes could only reply
that that was “rather an unfortunate comparison.”
“The piratically handsome Rankin.” That was how
M‘Kee, of that ilk, appeared to Clara Morris’s eyes a good
many years ago. M‘Kee Rankin played the Comte de
Varville to her Camille in New York. The Armand of the
cast, Charles R. Thorne, was generally known as “ My
God Thorne.” Miss Morris explains that “ whenever
Thorne was nervous, confused, or ‘ rattled,’ as actors
term it, or uncertain of the next line, he would pass his
hand across his brow and exclaim, in suppressed tones,
‘ My God ! ’’—and delicious creepy chills would go up and
down the feminine spine out in the auditorium. The
male spine is not so sensitive, you know.”
Bendigo now’ prides itself on being “ the canary cage of
Australia.” Natural evolution from the mining cage,
presumably.
Jefferson’s last stage appearance w'as in 1904 as Caleb
Plummer and Golightly in “Lend Me Five Shillings.”
With his autobiography he touches on his first rehearsal
of “ Caleb Plummer,” in presence of Boucicault, author of
the adaptation. Jeff was mightily pleased with himself
after the first act, but Bouci. was disgusted. He said :
“If your ideas are right, mine are all wrong. Don’t act
in the first act ! You show the audience all you can do.
Keep surprises for them, man ! ”
Lionel Brough says University youths, with private
incomes, are spoiling the dramatic profession in England
by acting for practically nothing. Few of these gilt-edged
individuals rise above mediocrity, but their blackleg tac-
tics block the way for men of ability who believe in
decent salaries.
Melbourne is at least musical, even if not always artis-
tically so. On a recent Saturday evening, in Elizabeth-
street, there were three separate performances going on
at one time not more than 20 yards apart, and all looking
for coin from the public. There was a good old grey-
headed gentleman grinding out hymns on a hand-organ,
a German band playing something incomprehensible, and
a blind man singing coon songs to his banjo. For-
tunately, the woman with the cracked voice and the bor-
rowed baby was round at the Town Hall, and was almost
inaudible.
The best information about actors and actresses comes
from florists and photographers. Both tell of a popular
actress who, when she first came to Australia, posed as a
determined man-hater, and was wont to express with
vehemence her detestation of the sex that wears a
top hat. Nowadays the lady is married, although the
matter is kept dark for professional purposes, and she
has no lonsrer a word to say in condemnation of the male
animal. This is remarkable, because marriage usually
aggravates that kind of distemper. Contempt cf man is
bred in the softer sex by fireside familiarity.
Julius Superbus is said to be so vastly impressed with
Australia’s sustained admiration for his legs that he c< n-
templates returning hither with a co. of his own on the
expiration of his present contract.
WHEN THE CIRCUS COMES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once a decade round our way,
Sad old Codville hides its gums,
When ’tis decked with posters gay,
And the tawdry circus comes.
In an hour the big' tents loom,
Monster mushrooms in the Square-
Human moths forsake the gloom,
Tempted by the noise and glai e !
Spangles ! how the spangles sir c,
As our long lost loves bestride,
With a wealth o’ form divine,
Their spry nags in airy prick !
Sadly then into the night,
Dazed and lone at heart we pass,
Peering for Life’s spangles bright,
Into mem’ry’s looking-glass.

Cure, and, after taking that medicine, I was promptly relieved from all pain. Since then, my
health has been most satisfactory.”

trouble in a very severe form. I suffered intense pain in the back and under the shoulder blades,

in every respect.”

When the Alien Feels His
Power.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In 10 years a Japanese will be Chief Justice of Aus-
tralia.—Opinion expressed by a brown visitor.
E'O ! the Jingo -press informs us that we. need
not mind the Monkey—
(Sing huzza the modest Monkey with his
unpretentious ways !)
He’s as harmless and as humble as the wearied,
toil worn donkey,
Not at all the sort of person to set continents
a-blaze!
But the modest Monkey mentions that his bosom
holds Ambitions,
And the accent of the Ape, it seemeth ominously
sour;
And the landscape’s littered over with a stable of
grim monitions—
We shall dance to different music When the Alien
Feels His Power!
Lo ! the Monkey is an insect of the ruminating
kidney—
He’s as silent as a sandbag, but he thinks with all
his might;
He has got the plans of Melbourne, and of Hobart
and of Sydney— :
He may cave Australia’s door in with an axe some
starless night ! "1 .
He is thinking while we’re drinking—h» surveys
the Land of Promise—
Yea, the Monkey eyes the plunder that he hun-
gers to devour;
And he’ll make a mighty effort to extract our
jewels from us—
We shall have to load our shot-gun When the
Alien Feels His Power !
But the Monkey won’t advise us in a formal, lan-
guid fashion—
He’ll remark that he is coming when he’s notice-
ably here;
Late at night he’ll thrust his bayonet and pug-
nacious black moustache in
The bar-room where we’re fooling with our fish
yarns and our beer.
He will not send a message asking gracious
. leave to visit;
He’ll inform us of his presence with a. sudden
leaden shower;
We will hear a roar and clatter ; we will loudly
cry “ What is it ?”
Better set our house in order Ere the Alien Feels
His Power !
VIC.
GRANT HERYEY.
Bill Tarkalson and Co.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" ~X" M T E struck Brody’s station one
\ \ evening about sundown,” said Tar-
kalson, resting his arms on his
knees, and gazing into the bed of red coals with
a smile that had something grim in it. “There
was me an’ Jim Scully an’ Mat Conyers. We ’ad
a pound or two among us at th’ time, but our
’orses wur fair knocked out, an’ the grass-
bearin’ country ahead warnt none too good, so we
wur ready to tackle anything on offer at Brody’s
to give ’em a spell. Conyers was th’ only one of
us who had two mokes. He carried Scully’s
pack an’ his own. I was walkin’ as usual, but I
’ad a pack-’orse, leadin’, Took ’im for wages
owin’ last place I worked.' Le’s try a fill o’ that
new terbacca o’ yours.
“Brody was a fine-lookin’ man at this time.
’Ad the name id’ bein’ a straight goer, but he was
a darned hard nail all the same. We went up to
him with our coats off, an’ our sleeves rolled up
to show our muscle, an’ we kinder took Brody’s
eye. ‘I think I ken put you on to something
that you’ll make good money at,’ he ses. ‘ This
place is overrun with wallabies—’ad to wire-net
my horse-paddock to keep the brutes out. I’ve
just sent a mob o’ blacks out to Lorry’s Lagoon;
but if you go anywhere north-west you’ll be clear
o’ them. I’m payin’ tuppence each for scalps, an’
buyin’ all skins at market rates, less 20 per cent,
for commission an’ carriage. Yer ken get what
rations you want ’ere, an’ th’ amount ’ill be de-
ducted from your account.’
“We cottoned on at once. Scully an’Conyers
’ad a rifle each, an’ I got one from Brody, cheap.
’Twasn’t much of a concern,-but I wasn’t much of
a shot, so it didn’t matter. Scully was no dab
either; but Matthew, ’cordin’ to his own skite,
was a crack shot. Could put a bullet in the bung-
hole of a beer-cask every time it turned up rollin’
down hill. ’Ad been buffalo shootin’ in the Terri-
t’ry, too —shootin’ with one hand at full gallop.
Just a matter of nerve, he sed. W« wanted t’
’ave a few practice shots at a tree next mornin’,
but Matthew wouldn’t hear of it. There was no
sense in wastin’ good ammunition like that. He
was surprised at me an’ Scully,
“’T any rate, he ’ad a proposition to make.
He was mostly bristlin’ with new-fangled ideas at
times like this. We’d gerra supply of opium from
the Chinaman, he sed ; then we’d go out to Lorry’s
Lagoon an’ get th’ blacks to hunt f’r us. They’d
do anything for opium in that part —’specially
when it was on the spot. We’d give ’em a little
tucker an’ terbacca too—not too much—an’ pro-
mise ’em a divvy when we got th’ cheques, I
agreed it was an easy way o’ makin’ a rise. ‘ One
thing certain,’ I ses to Scully, ‘ they’d get as many
in a day as we’d get ’n a week.’
“ ‘ Eggs-ackly,’ ses Scully, in his slow, deliberate
way. He warn’t never very eloquent, but when
he sed anything it was convincin’ like. ’S good a
man as ever I met was Scully. Mostly shaved
clean, an’ his face was splashed with freckles, just
’a jf brown paint ’ad been sprinkled on’t. Con-
yers was a reg’lar beauty in comparison, but he
warn’t half the man Jim was.
“ ‘Wot I don’t like about the bizness is this,’ I
ses to Mat, * they’ll be doin’ all the hard work an’
we’ll be gettin’ the profits.’
“ ‘Eggs-ackly/ sesr Scully.
“‘ I don’t hold to that, 1 I ses* ‘ I believe in
doin’ nie share.’
“ ‘But they’re only niggers,’ ses Conyers.
“ ‘ Eggs-ackly,’ses Scully agin. _ -
“ But Matthew’s forcible logic floored me in two
ups, an’ in the end he had his way ; an’ ai-med
with opium, terbaeca. an’ rations, we goes out to
Lorry’s Lagoon. He spent our last few bob bn a
set o’ books, pens, .ink, an’ other darned things
that we could ’a’ done without.
“ ‘With so many employ-ees, an’ doin’ bizness
on a gi-gantic scale,” he explained, hitchin’i the
blamed ledgers up under his arm, ‘ there’ll be a
lot o’ bookkeepin’ to do—receipts an’ disburse-
ments, an’ that sort o’ thing. We ought t’ ’ave
scales for Weighin’ too, but we’ll get them next
time. I’ll look after th’ books an’ stores, measure
out opium an’ terbacca, check the takings an’ give
receipts, an’ superintend operations gen’ally.
That ’ill keep me party busy. You bein’ the best
'cOok, Bill,’ he ses to me, ‘ you’ll attend to) the
colander department and string scalps, an’ Jim
’ill peg out th’ skins. Ye’ll find we won’t have
much time to scratch ourselves—if th’ hunters are
' any good at all.’
“ ‘ Eggs-ackly,’ ses Scully ; but I noticed there
Was a mystified, half-vacant sort o’ look showin’
between the freckles. * r 1
“We fell in with Conyers’ plans. We mostly
did. Conyers was a Bar won native, an’ to hear
him talk ye’d think he’d been over half the earth.
I yooster try at fust to gerrim bushed about Aus-
tralia, but my ’umble remarks would only remind
him of something in Borneo or Brazil, or some
such place that was a week o’ Sundays off my
track. Besides bein’ able to ride outlaws an’ cla s
wool, he’could survey land an’ navigate a ship.
Learnt ’em at the Yooniversity, I s’pose. He ad
plenty o’push, an’ an impressive way with him
that always put him in front—so far’s me and
Scully wur concerned.
“ Well, Matthew talked the blacks over all
right—there wur about 40 of ’em. He read out
an agreement to ’em (verbal agreement it was), an’
took down their names—which wur mostly Sandy
an’ Jacky. They wur all to be purpendiklar
by sunrise every mornin’—strict, an’ not to
show back from work afore night. I forget
’ Wot th’ penalties wur now ; * instant dismissal ’
applied to a lot o’ oases, I know. Anyhow, he
made ’em pull down their gunyahs fust of all, an’
build ’em in two-long rows. He ’ad original ideas,
you’ll understand. Then they helped him build
an office an’ storehouse with bark, an’ a galley for
the cook. Wonder he thought of such a ’umble
person as tli’ nook at all. He spent most of his
time in the office, postin’ an’ balancin’ accounts.
There was no end o’ rows over them accounts, too.
Sandy’s notched stick seldom tallied with
Matthew’s ledger. But it was the billet his soul
hungered for, though bizness got so pressin’ after
a time that he ’ad to get ’n assistant. Her name
was Avelina, Gerls, he sed, wur more gen’ally
useful than boys. She cleaned the office out purty
often, brushed his hat, an’ greased his boots. Mat
was purtiklar ’bout his appearance, lernme tell
you. Made a good impression on the servants, he
sed. Avelina also fetched the mails. Mat sub-
scribed to the local paper, an’ he gorra letter now
’n’ again from a piece down, be Gunnedah
somewhere. I got hold o’ one of her letters
one day. He’d dropped it among th’ scalps.
Seems he’d been tellin’ her he was head manager of
th’ ‘ North Queensland Marsupial Company,
Unlimited,’ an’ had 140 men an’ 60 odd gerls
under him ; an’ she was askin’ for some purtiklars.
She trusted Matthew wouldn’t be gettin’ mixed
up with any o’ the fact’ry gerls. So far as I could
see there’d been no mention of Avelina.
“At night time Mat would stand in front of
the office, with his thumbs hooked in the armholes
of his weskit, an’ look down the street. Looked
just like a street with th’ fires goin’ in front o’ the
gunyahs. When th’ bucks wur singin’ corroboree
songs, an’ the dogs wur fightin’, an’ the kids
chasin’ ’em, an’ the gins yellin’, ’twarnt
unlike Paddy’s Market. Then he’d stroll down
an’ view the effect from the other end. I think
Matthew did really consider himself a big em-
ployer —if he didn’t feel like a bloated capitalist.
He liked effect, anyway, an’ was purtiklar on
style. I’m methodical meself, an’ Scully ses he’s
systematic, Dunno ; he upsets the billy purty
often. ’Tanyrate, after doin’ th’ block, Matthew’d
give us th’ latest wallaby quotations from th’
Bilt.abong Banner, an’ reckon up our wealth.
Wje{WfiPmakin’ 25 quid a week. After you with
tli’ fire&tick.
“ Well, everything went swimmin’ for about a
month. Then we ’ad to render our accounts an’
square up, ’cordin’ to agreement. Scully an’me
wanted to get a conveyance from Brody, but
Conyers sed there was no necessity for that. It
’ud mean tli’ loss of a day, an’ meantime the
employ-ees would be idle. ’Twas bad management
to leave the employ-ees idle. So wot does he do
but pack each blackfeller with a bundle o’ skins
an’ scalps, an’ send ’em off fust thing in the morn-
in’ to Brody. The gins follered wi’ the camp-
ware, es we wur goin’ to shift to new ground next
day. We could a-got in simul-taneous ourselves,
but Conyers ’ad a balance-sheet an’ a capital
account an’ some profit-an’-loss to make out yet,
an’ the day-book to rule off.
“ ‘ We must ’ave these matters in order when
we go to th’ office,’ he explained, ‘so we’ll be
able to check th’ station account an’ see that all’s
square.. I don’t say. es Brody would do a man out
of a scalp, mind you ; but no man’s infallible, an’
, figures is things th’ best o’ ’em might easily
blunder at —often do. Just as well to be sure.’
“ ‘Eggs-ackly,’ ses Scully, starin' at th’ ground.
“We got th’ horses an’ groomed ’em, an’pot-
tered about- till dinner time. Mat was still in
the office, perched on a saplin’ seat, an’ bent over
a bark desk, subtractin’ from a pile o’ matches in
front of him. There was scraps o’ paper an’ ink
splotches all over th’ ship, but th’ blamed accounts
warnt balanced yet. We has dinner, an’ he goes
at ’em again. ’Twas four o’clock ’fore we gorra
move on.
‘ ‘ ‘ Wot’s th’ sum total ? ’ I asks him as we rides
along, Mat with th’ ledgers under his arm, some
papers stickin’ out of his top pocket for effect, an’
a pencil stuck behind his ear.
“He ses proudly it was £126 7s. lOd. We wur
surprised.
“ ‘ How much is that each ?’ I ses.
“Mat opens th’ ledger an’ examines several
pages—folios he called ’em,
“‘lt’s £42 2s. ll|d.,’ he ses. ‘That’s ten
guineas a week we’ve been maltin'. In a year’s
time we’ll be worth £5OO each.’
“ ‘ Eggs-ackly,’ ses Scully.
“We leaves our ’orses in th’ stockyard when
we gets in, an’ Matthew sails into th’ office with
th’ ledgers. Me an’ Scully stopped at th’ door,
there bein’ no need for all to speak at once.
“‘Well, wot d’yer make of it, Mr. Brody?’
asks Mat, his face one big smile.
“ ‘ Make of wot ? ’ sea Brody.
“ ‘Th’ skins an’ scalps, of course.’
“‘Wot skins an’ scalps?’ses Brody, surprised-
like.
Why, them es we sent jn with th’ blacks this
mornin’.’ The smile was wastin’, an’ looked sick.
“Brody leaned back in his chair an’ looked
mystified. ‘l’ve not heard anything about’em,’
he ses.
“Mat’s jaw dropped an’ his eyes jumped ’sif
he’d been hit on th’ back o’ the neck.
“ ‘ Th’ only blacks that have come in wur those
I sent to Lorry’s Lagoon,’ ses Brody. ‘ They
brought their lot in, an’ I paid them. You’ll
find them in town.’
“‘My Gawd!’ ses Scully, turnin’ his head
round slowly like a bogged cow does. Poor chap—
’e was stunned. . .
“ ‘Th’ scamps ’ave took us down, Jim,’ I ses in
a whisper,
“ ‘ Eggs-ackly,’ ses Jim, an’ he looked sad.
“‘You paid ’em!’ gasps Conyers, an’ he
seemed to get something in his throat wot he
couldn’t swaller—p’rhaps th’ dead smile. ‘ Well,
that’s a dem fine go ! Them was our skins,’ he
ses. ‘We employed th’ blacks to hunt for us ’
“ ‘ I know nothing about that,’ Brody chips in,
cold-like. ‘ I employed them to hunt for me, an’
I paid them accordin’ to results. In any case, as
th’ law is in Queensland nowadays, you can’t em-
ploy aborigines without a special license.’
“ ‘ Wot are we to do, then ?’ asks Conyers.
“ Brody opens an account-book, an’ he ses,
‘ There’s ’leven pounds against you an’ your mates
for supplies. You ken pay that.’
“ That floored Conyers proper. He walked out
with his head down, tryin’ to hide them blamed
ledgers under his coat. Scully was squattin’ agin
th’ wall now, chewin’ a bit o’ grass an’ starin’ at
the floor. I goes up to Brody to arrange for the
liquidation o’ the darned company.
“‘lt’s purty evident we’ve been had,’ I ses.
‘ We’re left without a bean, so I s’pose you’ll allow
us time to settle that little account.’
“‘Well, yes,’ he ses; ‘l’ll allow you three
months ’——
“‘Thank you, Mr. Brody,’ I ses. ‘lt’s un-
fortunit
“‘ln th’ meantime,’ old Brody goes on, ‘l’ll
look after your gear for you, an’ yer ’orses ken be
fatt’nin’ in my paddock. I won’t make any charge
for that.’ _
“Darn his eyes, but that knocked me. I believe
to this day the old dog put them blacks up to it.
He went with us to th’ yard. There was only
Scully’s hack an’ ray pack-’orse there ; Conyers an’
his live stock ’ad fled. 1 took possession of rae
swag, an’Brody took possession of the rest. We
squatted down an’ filled our pipes then. ’Twas
’n occasion when a draw was comfortin’—though
’twarn’t much of a draw out of a ’hundred an’
twenty-six quid.
“‘Wot are we goin’ to do about it, Bill?’ ses
Scully.
“ ‘Damfiknow, Jim,’ I ses. ‘Don’t see es
we’ve gorra hope.’
Warn’t no use hangin’ round, anyway, so we
got goin’ purty soon on Matthew’s tracks. Come
on to him, camped, at sundown. He ’ad th’ day-
book left, an’ was tearin’ out th’ leaves an’ feedin’
th’ fire with ’em.
“‘Pity yer didn’t boil yer billy with’ em at
fust,’ I ses. ‘This is where yer management o’
th’ great Marsupial Company ’as landed us ; ’ an’
I throws me swag down, with emphasis.
“‘Never mind. Bill,’ he says, very quietly;
‘ we’ll get even with old Brody one o’ these days.’
“‘We want £3 13s. 4d. from you, anyway,’ I
ses. ‘ That’s your share o’ the bill yer left us to
foot.’
“ ‘ Eggs-ackly,’ ses Scully.
“‘Oh, that’ll be all right,' ses Mat, softly.
‘ I’ll fix that up. For the present I’ll carry your
swags along on my horse.’
“ He carried ’em along right enough—for seven
weeks; then, bein’ mostly ahead of us, he poked
into the one solitary vacancy that was out that
way, an’ me an’ Scully wur left to hump our own.
But Matthew was mag-nanimous, after all. ‘ I’v*
fixed that little account up for you,’ he ses,
handin’ me a sealed envelope es he was goin’
away. When I opens it I finds this —writ on a
survivin’ folio of th’ company’s books :
“ ‘ William Tarkalson and James Scully, Esqs
“ ‘ Dr. to Matthew Conyers.
“ ‘ To carrying their luggage on his horse for
seven weeks at 10s. per week .. .. £3 10 0
“‘ By contra a/c. .. . .. .. 313 4
“ ‘ Balance herewith .. .. ..£034
“ Th’ balance herewith was his share o’ th’ bit
o’ rations we had, an’ three tucker bags.’’
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

Carmen of the Circus.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f tired surf brushes over the bar
1 where the red Penitentiary faces Long
Bay. Here and there the .scrub is broken,
and wind-driven gulls hover around the new prison
walls. The thin road is like a wound on the liip
of the shore where it twists towards Maroubra and
the Lazarette. A newly-planted cemetery with its
gibbet-shaped crosses and headstones is a pagan
blot on the sea front.
Half-seen humpies peep from the scrub. “The
Tug’s Rest” is made of tin and bag. “The
Glutton’s Retreat” and “Nine Stomachs” are
two kerosene-tin villas, frequented by bottle-ohs
and jockeys, and numerous horse-faced mannikins.
They come on Sundays, and the clear air is soon
festering with the clamor of poultry : fighting and
other kingly inventions.
Sven and Borka the Finn live on the beach.
Their huts are not large, but their baskets are
always full of red bream, with here and there a
blackball or sclmapper. A tide-swung lugger
rubs her shoulder against the pier. The Finn
sprawls face down in the hot, wet sand —dreaming
of cold Baltic tides, or the ice on the bow of a
North Sea haddoeker.
Sven, wrapped to the waist in surf, beckoned
me nearer. I sat on a boulder where the billy
rested over smoking ashes. Hauling his boat into
the shade, Sven sat down.
“Dis fishin’ vas a fool-trade. De fish is always
wet, und when he is dry he is dead. Gif me a
trade dot vas alive—de old Queensland kanaka
boat, or an Arab slave dhow ; dey vas both de
same.”
I threw some dry leaves under the billy.
Sven’s tiger-mauled hands interest me ; his pants,
rolled up to his thigh, reveal a criss-cross of
scars and mutilated sinews. He caught my
glance and laughed harshly.
“A jaguar slut tried me for breakfast von
mornin’. Ye vas runnin’ to Hambourg from
South Americas, und she vas sick mit der smell
ob der sea, und, fool like, I go into her cage to try
un\l make friends.
“ Ven Cooper’s circus came to Australia I got a
billot mit dem as keeper. You do not remember
Carmen, de Spanish girl dot used to dive from de
roof into de net. She came troo de air like a
gull, und she vas always laughin’. She vas
married to one of der circus-grooms named Molloy.
He vas a red Irishman mit der face of a priest.
“ Carmen vas a favorite mit everyone ; but der
droble came about because Molloy vas a favorite
mit everyone too. Molloy vas nefar far from der
ladies, und der vas a lot of dem in der company.
“ In de bush or in town Carmen go to church in
her wine-colored mantilla. I tink religion make
dese Spanish women silly at times, but Carmen
vas der prettiest leedle fool dot efer listen to an
Irishman’s blarney.
“She make a good picture as we go troo de
bush towns, ridin’ her Andalusian mule mit der
trappings of silk dot vas de color of de toreador’s
cloak. Gymnastics make her body beautiful; she
haf no heavy h.ip movement. She valk like an
athlete, und she haf de eyes of a Moorish lady.
“Carmen vas slow to follow her husband’s
movements about de circus. He used to feed de
ponies und water de dogs. It is de business of a
circus pony to look shiny in de ring und to wear a
satin skin efery nide. De groom is at de beck of
de ring-master, und if a horse wear a smear on his
flank de ring-master vill bite de groom before der
whole company. Sometimes he vill bite a piece
off his screw.
“We had opened all along de line to crowded
houses. Two good seasons had. put a leedle
money into de farmers’ pockets ; und dey came
in carts from de far back homesteads to see
Sarah, de elephant, and Sultana, de big wicked
lioness mit der two cubs.
“Carmen did not like de bush- hotels. She
lived in her tent shut off from de circus by a
striped awning. Der lofe of animals vas strong
in her. She vas de daughter of Carlos Acantino,
de bull-fighter ; her moder vas a finer dancer than
La Belle Otero. Der shouts of der multitude,
der roaring of beasts vas de music of her heart*
In de hot Australian nights you vould find her
mit her cigarette und her guitar, playing till
Molloy, her husband, should finish his work.
“Molloy mit his priest’s face had noding in his
head. He gif not a dam for her Moorish romances
und songs. He vas cold to her in de presence of
oder women. Carmen remembered dot, at der
moment she took her leap from der roof efery
nide. Und Molloy deceived her in his clumsy
vay.
“De-oder woman vas Madamoiselle Cherez, de
French dancer. She used to write notes to
Molloy. She left dem in de horse chaff and under
de saddles. • One nide he dropped Mademoiselle s
last note from his pocket, und Carmen picked it
up in der tent.
“ Madamoiselle Cherez vas 19. Carmen was 30,
und mit all her arts und graces she could nod call
back de 10 years dot separated her from de French
dancer. . Dey were 10 stone vails dot could
nod be put aside. Der vas no rouge on de French
girl’s face. Carmen used to valk across de ring ;
Cherez bounded und ran—dot is de difference 10
years make.
“Dot nide de beobles go mad ven dey see
Carmen flyin’ from de trapeze under de high
canvas dome. Id vas all one mit her now vether
she broke her neck or lived.
“ Molloy vent to Carmen’s tent after de per-
formance, und told her to pack up and catch de
midnight train to Sydney. All de members of de
circus vas goin’, he said, und he vas sorry he had
to stay pehind to truck de ponies und mules.
Carmen vas used to de hurry of circus life, und
she make no complaint. Molloy, in his shirt
sleeves, -und a straw in his mouth, stayed mit
her for a few minutes, tryin’ to bluff de suspicion
from her eyes.
“ I shall nefar forget de heat of dot nide. Id
stirred up de sour-eyed vultures in de cages. A
wolf howled from de end waggon, und Sultana, de
big lioness, lay mit her hot flanks against de bars.
I)e hot nide und der suckling cubs make her cross.
I filled her drinkin’ trough mit de horse-pump,
und I gave her some on de chest und head to; cool
her a bit.
“All de company had left for de midnight
train. Dere vas a light here und dere inside de
circus, but de ring vas dark. De grooms und de
shifters ver asleep in tents, for dey would haf to
be up at dawn to see dot eferyting vas trucked.
Den I tinlc id vas alride time to have a smoke mit
Ahmed Khan, de elephant-keeper. So I Cross
der ring, und go past vere de camels vas lyin’
beside <ie hay-truck. 1 look back quick, und see
a small lamp movin' among der cages. lie lamp
vas lifted to de bars of de tiger’s cage, und den
it pass along to de pumas, und I hear de young
leopards winp round, purrin’ und strokin' de bars
ven de lamp vas lifted to de door of tlieir house.
“ ‘ Who de hell play viil-o'-de-visp among de
beasts?’ I said, und i crawled back under tie
puma s cage until 1 cooin face to face mit Carmen
starin’ into de heavy barred ring cage dot bold do
big lioness und her two cubs.
“ ‘ Vot is wrong, senora?’ I say. ‘I tort you
go avay by de night train.’
“ * i vas lookin' at de 11011 s, Herr Sven,’ she say
to me. Hiie held up de leedle lamp und point to
de cubs lyin’m de lar corner of ue cage. ‘ now
quiet dey are, she says ; und she look at me quick,
und i see dot she vas tryin’ to laugh. ‘ liovv much
for de keys of de cage, Herr 6 veil, how much ? ’
“Der vas a lot of tiny bells on Carmens wrists,
und as she laughed dey tinkled on de hot night
air. ‘ Vhatdoyou vant mit de liou-cubs, Car-
men ? Dey vas not und heavy to carry ; und de
boss does not luce de young beasts disturbed at
night.' „ , . ,
“ ‘ .Listen, Herr Sven; I vant to play joke on
my husoand. rue is unkind to me. i vouid like
to fliken h.ui a Iceo.e. Cif me de cuds for a
while, lien oven.’ _
“ I could see no harm in a joke, und id vas hard
to refuse Carmen any.ting. i said no more, in
de meat-waggon 1 laid haif-a-sneep on der hook.
Mrs. Leo vas nod asleep veil i threw it into der
open deu. IShe ioiiowed de meat nut her nose to de
boards, und Carmen, mit a quick snatch, slid da
iron gate back so dot wo haf de cubs separate from
de moder.
“Carmen laughed und took de keys from my
hand ; den she opened de gate uud lift de two
cubs in her arms. Dey vas noavy enough for a
man to carry, but Carmen vas as btrong as she vas
quick. , , ,
a “ Mit a cub under each arm she go to her own
tent. Der vas no light inside, und she placed
dem under de clothes, at de foot ot de bed. Joey
cried like puppies for shoost a minute, but dey
vas sleepy, uud uey s-on lie qu.et.
“ Away down de da.k circus we heard Molloy
singm a song about de stars of heaven uud ue
green Irish grass dot grow near de lakes of cove
jt'mn. If id had not been for Carmen und her

tink oi ue two leedle warm lions m jimmy s bed.
Ynen he turned m lie stick his long cold lect
between dem.
“ One by one de lights in de circus tents go out.
De leedle pavilion ot de French dancer was last to
go dark. But I see Molloy walk nut her across de
rmg ; und she danced as Sie valued. bioumg
couid stop de leedle i 1 relic a girl noin dancing,
tide danced m de dark mit de sawdust about ner

Come, he said, ‘ und I vill make golfee m my

Sven’s pipe had gone out; he stooped absently,
broke an old fish-basket with his hands, and
thrust it bit by bit into the fire.
“ Haf some tea mit me,” he said, without look-
ing up. There was an apology in his eyes as he
indicated the hut in which he lived. “ I cannot
ask you inside slioost now.
I turned curiously towards the small undraped
window, and saw a face recede from it hastily. I
became suddenly conscious that a pair of uufor-
getable Spanish eyes had been staring at me
through the cracked pane. A toreador-like scarf
fluttered from view.
